<section>
<h2>6 Afro-Asiatic</h2>
</section>
<section>
<h2>6.1 Proto-Afro-Asiatic to Proto-Omotic</h2>
<cite>Mecislau, from Ehret, Christopher (1995), Reconstructing Proto-Afroasiatic (Proto-Afrasian): Vowels, Tone, Consonants, and Vocabulary (Voices from Asia)</cite>
<rule>dz ʃ tʃ → ʒ s₁ s₂</rule>
<rule>dʒ → tʃ → ʃ</rule>
<rule>t → ∅ / _s#</rule>
<rule>ɬ → l</rule>
<rule>f → p</rule>
<rule>a(ː) → e(ː) / _{ʕ,q}$</rule>
<rule>q ʕ → ʔ h</rule>
<rule>a → o / #Cw_{(d)l,s₃}</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / #C_V, except _i(ː)</rule>
<rule>ʃ → s₂ / {i,j}_</rule>
<rule>VNC → VːC[+voiced]</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>6.1.1 Proto-Omotic to North Omotic</h2>
<cite>Mecislau, from Ehret, Christopher (1995), Reconstructing Proto-Afroasiatic (Proto-Afrasian): Vowels, Tone, Consonants, and Vocabulary (Voices from Asia)</cite>
<rule>u o → i e</rule>
<rule>e → i / #N_C</rule>
<rule>e → i / #l_{P,C[+voiced]}</rule>
<rule>e → i / #b_</rule>
<rule>e → i / p_r</rule>
<rule>e → i / #{s,ʃ,tsʼ}_{k(w),ʔ}</rule>
<rule>e o → i u / #C_P</rule>
<rule>e o → i u / #(ʔ)_C</rule>
<rule>e o → i u / #{k(ʼ),x}_{t(ʼ),tsʼ}</rule>
<rule>e o → i u / #(ʔ)_C$</rule>
<rule>e o → i u / #P_{tsʼ,tʃʼ}</rule>
<rule>a → o / #{z,dʒ}_P</rule>
<rule>e(ː) → i(ː) / #C[+sibilant]_{d,n,r}</rule>
<rule>Cw → C</rule>
<rule>Vː → V / #K[-voice]_C</rule>
<rule>u → uː / #S[+voice]_P[-voice]</rule>
<rule>Vː → V / #C_C$ + $(V)C$ suffix</rule>
<rule>N → ∅ / V_{C[+sibilant],p}</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>6.1.1.1 North Omotic to Bench</h2>
<cite>Mecislau, from Ehret, Christopher (1995), Reconstructing Proto-Afroasiatic (Proto-Afrasian): Vowels, Tone, Consonants, and Vocabulary (Voices from Asia)</cite>
<rule>x₁ → k</rule>
<rule>x₂ → k / #_</rule>
<rule>x₂ s₃ → ∅ ʃ / V_V</rule>
<rule>tʃ → ts</rule>
<rule>sₓ → ʃ</rule>
<rule>{ʔ,hₓ} → ∅</rule>
<rule>l → d / #_VC</rule>
<rule>l → n / #_VN</rule>
<rule>dʼ → tʼ</rule>
</section>

<section>
<h2>6.2.1.1.1 Proto-Agaw to Awngi</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Fallon, Paul D. (2009), “The Velar Ejective in Proto-Agaw”. In Selected Proceedings of the 39th Annual Conference on African Linguistics, Ojo, Akinloye and Lioba Moshi (Eds.), 10 – 22. Sommerville, MA: Cascadilla Proceedings Project. &lt;http://www.lingref.com&gt;, document #2182, citing Appleyard, David L. (2006), A comparative dictionary of the Agaw languages. (Cushitic Language studies, 24.) Cologne: Rüdiger Köppe Verlag.</cite>
; NB: Does not include vowel developments.
<rule>{x,ɢ}(ʷ) → ∅ / at word boundaries</rule>
<rule>z dz ɡ → ɡ {z,dz} ɡ(ʷ)</rule>
<rule>{x,ɢ}(ʷ) → ɣ(ʷ)</rule>
<rule>kʼ kʷʼ → {ɣ,q} ɣʷ</rule>
<rule>ʔ → ∅</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>6.2.1.1.2 Proto-Agaw to Blin</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Fallon, Paul D. (2009), “The Velar Ejective in Proto-Agaw”. In Selected Proceedings of the 39th Annual Conference on African Linguistics, Ojo, Akinloye and Lioba Moshi (Eds.), 10 – 22. Sommerville, MA: Cascadilla Proceedings Project. &lt;http://www.lingref.com&gt;, document #2182, citing Appleyard, David L. (2006), A comparative dictionary of the Agaw languages. (Cushitic Language studies, 24.) Cologne: Rüdiger Köppe Verlag.</cite>
; NB: Does not include vowel developments.
<rule>{x,ɢ}(ʷ) → ∅ / at word boundaries</rule>
<rule>ɢ(ʷ) → x(ʷ) / else</rule>
<rule>{ts,tʃ} z dz → ʃ d dʒ</rule>
<rule>t → r / medially</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>6.2.1.1.3 Proto-Agaw to Kemantney</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Fallon, Paul D. (2009), “The Velar Ejective in Proto-Agaw”. In Selected Proceedings of the 39th Annual Conference on African Linguistics, Ojo, Akinloye and Lioba Moshi (Eds.), 10 – 22. (Cushitic Language studies, 24.) Cologne: Rüdiger Köppe Verlag.</cite>
; NB: Does not include vowel developments.

<section>
<h2>6.2.1.1.4 Proto-Agaw to Xamtanga</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Fallon, Paul D. (2009), “The Velar Ejective in Proto-Agaw”. <rule>k → {k(ʼ),q}</rule>

<rule>{h,ħ} → ʔ / KV_</rule>
<rule>q → ʔ / h_</rule>
<rule>qh → ʕħ</rule>
<rule>ɣ → ʕ / ħ_</rule>
<rule>tlʼ → dl / #_Vħr</rule>
<rule>O[+lateral] → O[+palatal]</rule>
<rule>r → l / #_V(V)O[+labial]</rule>
<rule>r → ʔ / C_{t,w,j}# ! C = {ɡ,m,n,r,w,ʃ,x}</rule>
<rule>l → j / #_iC ?  </rule>
<rule>l → r / #nV_C</rule>
<rule>l → n</rule>

<h2>6.2.2.1.5 Proto-Semitic to Classical Arabic</h2>
<cite>Khavaragh</cite>
<rule>p → f</rule>
<rule>θˤ kˤ → ðˤ q</rule>
<rule>ɡ → ɡʲ → dʒ</rule>
; “anaphora and certain derivational prefixes. . .[t]his is common to many other Semitic languages as well”
<rule>s → {ʃ,h} / in</rule>
<rule>ʃ → s</rule>
<rule>ɬ → ʃ</rule>
<rule>ɬˤ → dɬˤ → dˤ</rule>
; “in certain contexts, notably in the nunation”
<rule>m → n /</rule>
; “assimilation in some of the longer vowels”
<rule>V{j,w}V → aː / some sequences</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>6.2.2.1.6 Classical Arabic to Cypriot Arabic</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Borg, Alexander (1985), Cypriot Arabic</cite>
; NB: Changes may not be in chronological order.
<rule>S[+ voice] → S[- voice]</rule>
<rule>q → k</rule>
<rule>S → [+ voice] / {V,R}_V</rule>
; {θ,ð} {f,v} {x,ɣ} voicing neutralized “in contact with other fricatives”
<rule>S → [+ voice] / V_R</rule>
<rule>S → F / _S</rule>
<rule>f θ → p t / F_</rule>
<rule>k x → c ç / _{j,E}</rule>
<rule>{l,n}j → jː</rule>
<rule>j → c / {O,r}</rule>
<rule>j → ∅ / Ck_$</rule>
<rule>nx → xː</rule>
<rule>∅ → F / N_{O,r} ! m_f</rule>
<rule>{ðˤ,dˤ} → ð</rule>
<rule>tˤ sˤ → s t</rule>
<rule>ʔ h → ∅ x</rule>
<rule>∅ → i / #al$_z</rule>
<rule>dʒ → z</rule>
<rule>ɣ ħ → ʕ x</rule>
<rule>w → v / _%</rule>
<rule>wː → v</rule>
<rule>j(ː) → ∅ / V_E</rule>
<rule>uː iː → oː eː / _ʕ</rule>
<rule>uː iː → oː eː / ʕ_</rule>
<rule>i → a / Cˤ_{q,ɣ,ʕ}</rule>
<rule>i → a / {q,ɣ,ʕ}_Cˤ</rule>
<rule>a → i / _C(C), when stressed</rule>
<rule>u → o / _{ʕ,ɣ,x,r}</rule>
<rule>u → o / {ʕ,ɣ,x,r}_</rule>
; Epenthesis in medial CCC clusters, often so that the syllable break is between the second and third consonants
<rule>{u,a,i} → ∅ / _%, when stressed (short only)</rule>
<rule>uː iː → u i</rule>
<rule>a → a / _C[+ dorsal]</rule>
<rule>a → e / _(C)(C)i(ː)</rule>
<rule>aː → a / ! _#</rule>
<rule>a → {u,o} / P_</rule>
<rule>a → {u,o} / _P</rule>
<rule>a → ∅ / _t, in the feminine ending</rule>
<rule>aːʔ → e / E(C)(C)_#</rule>
<rule>aː → a / {Cˤ,w}_#</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>6.2.2.1.7 Classical Arabic to Egyptian Arabic</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Brustad, Kristen, Mahmoud Al-Batal, and Abbas Al-Tonsi (2010), Alif Baa: Introduction to Letters and Sounds, 3rd. Ed.; http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic; At-Tonsi, Abbas, Heba Salem, and Nevenka Korica Sullivan (2013), Umm al-Dunya: Advanced Egyptian Colloquial Arabic; and from correspondence with my own Arabic professor, who is a native speaker of this dialect</cite>
; “usually in numbers or cases where a short vowel has been deleted and it’s in contact with another stop, e.g. CA/MSA kaˈθiːr → EA ktiːr”
<rule>θ ð → t d /</rule>
<rule>θ ð → s z</rule>
<rule>ðˤ → zˤ, occasionally tˤ</rule>
<rule>dˤ → zˤ (seems to be a sporadic change only affecting a few words, e.g. CA/MSA ˈdˤaːbitˤ → EA ˈzˤaːbitˤ)</rule>
<rule>dʒ → ɡ</rule>
<rule>i u → e o / only when short, ! _#</rule>
<rule>u → {o,u} / short only, _#</rule>
<rule>aj aw → eː oː / in U[+closed]</rule>
<rule>Vː → V / C_C{ː,C}V</rule>
<rule>V → Vː / C_CV in U[-stress]</rule>
<rule>V → Vː / _# + suffix</rule>
; Some other short-vowel deletions
<rule>{i,u} → ∅ / VC_CV when unstressed (short only)</rule>
<rule>∅ → e / CVCC_CVCV (applies across word boundaries)</rule>
; Resyllabification across word boundaries to prevent vowel-initial syllables
; r gains emphatic status except when next to i, and even then it’s becoming more common in that environment
<rule>a(ː) → ɑ(ː) / near emphatics</rule>
<rule>a(ː) → ɑ(ː) / if ɑ(ː) is elsewhere in the word</rule>
<rule>a(ː) → æ(ː) / else (sometimes it seems more like ɛ(ː) to me)</rule>
; Two consecutive consonants assimilate to the voicing of the second (obstruents only?)
<rule>q → ʔ / except in several words, two of which are al-Qâhira and musîqâ</rule>
<rule>{{s,z}(ˤ),ʒ}ʃ → ʃː</rule>
; Final short vowel loss
<rule>ʕ → {ʕ̞,ħ} / _h</rule>
<rule>h → ∅ / in coda</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>6.2.2.1.8 Classical Arabic to Coastal Hadhrami Arabic</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia Contributors (2013), “Hadhrami Arabic”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hadhrami_Arabic&amp;oldid=580700095&gt;</cite>
<rule>dʒ → j, occasionally ɟ or dʒ in educated speech</rule>
<rule>θ ð ðˤ → t d dˤ</rule>
<rule>q → ɡ</rule>
<rule>aː → eː / in Form VI (tafā‘ala) verbs, though these apparently coexist with forms having the original vowel as well, with semantic distinctions</rule>
; Epenthesis (it seems i is preferred) breaking up final consonant clusters
<rule>aː → æː / when not near emphatics</rule>
<rule>V[-long] → ∅ / #C_C, in some words</rule>
</section>

<h2>6.2.2.1.9 Classical Arabic to Wādı̄ Hadhrami Arabic</h2>

<rule>dʒ → j, occasionally ɟ or dʒ in educated speech</rule>
<rule>θ ð ðˤ → t d dˤ</rule>
<rule>dˤ q → ðˤ ɡ</rule>
<rule>aː → eː / in Form VI (tafā‘ala) verbs, though these apparently coexist with forms having the original vowel as well, with semantic distinctions</rule>
; Epenthesis (it seems i is preferred) breaking up final consonant clusters
<rule>aː → æː / when not near emphatics</rule>

<h2>6.2.2.1.10 Classical Arabic to Hassāniyya Arabic</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hass%C4%81n%C4%ABya</cite>
; NB: Words borrowed directly from CA/MSA seem to be immune to these changes. Also, unless otherwise noted, changes also apply to geminate consonants.
<rule>dˤ q → ðˤ ɡ</rule>
<rule>f θ → v z̪ (the article isn’t exactly clear on what this second phone is) </rule>
<rule>ʔ → {∅,j,w} / depending on the environment; again, the article is unclear</rule>
<rule>x → χ (conjectured based upon the following but not outright stated in the article)</rule>
<rule>ɣː → ʁː → qː</rule>
<rule>ɣ → {ʁ,q}</rule>
<rule>V[-long] → ∅ / C_{C,#} (except for the feminine marker)</rule>
; The conditioning on these next two changes is conjectured based upon the source:
<rule>aj aw → eː(ʲ) oː(ʷ) (sometimes, the article is unclear)</rule>
<rule>— j w → i u / #_CV</rule>
<rule>— j w → iː uː / #_CC</rule>
</section>

<h2>6.2.2.1.14 Classical Arabic to Moroccan Arabic</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moroccan_Arabic</cite>
<rule>t → t͜s / plain t only, distinguishable from the sequence ts</rule>
<rule>{a,i} → ə / short only; the change of short a blocked for some speakers before ħ ʕ</rule>
; “a labial or velar consonant”
<rule>u → ə / short only, except near</rule>
<rule>C[+labial/+velar] → ʷ / adjacent to short u</rule>
<rule>{u,ə} → ∅ / ! C_C(C)#</rule>
<rule>ə → a / near ħ ʕ</rule>
<rule>ə → ɐ / near emphatics</rule>
<rule>ə → ɪ / else</rule>
<rule>u → ʊ / short only</rule>
<rule>aː iː uː → ɑː eː oː / near emphatics</rule>
<rule>aː → æː / else</rule>
<rule>C₁ˤC₂ → C₁C₂ˤ</rule>
<rule>Cˤ → C / {#,V}_V</rule>
<rule>q → {q,ɡ}</rule>
<rule>dʒ → {d,ɡ} / if s or z occur somewhere else in the word</rule>
<rule>dʒ → ʒ / else</rule>
<rule>s → ʃ / if ʃ is somewhere in the stem after it</rule>
<rule>z → ʒ / if ʒ is somewhere in the stem after it</rule>
</section>

<h2>6.2.2.1.18 Proto-Semitic to Biblical Hebrew</h2>
<cite>Maknas, from http://www.adath-shalom.ca/history_of_hebrew.htm “and other sources”</cite>
; NB: ə could be realized as an ultrashort [a], [e], or [o] depending on its surroundings.
<rule>θ θˤ ð ɬ ɬˤ → ʃ ʃˤ z s sˤ</rule>
<rule>x ɣ kˤ → ħ ʕ q</rule>
; Some mergers involving j and w
; Frequent h-dropping
; Stressed-vowel correspondences:
<rule>— aː → oː / ! _#</rule>
<rule>— i iː u uː → eə iːə oə uːə / _R</rule>
<rule>— iː → eː / _#</rule>
<rule>— a → aː / _$</rule>
<rule>— a → {a,ɛ} (not common)</rule>
<rule>— i u → e a / _R{$,#} (in verbs)</rule>
<rule>— i u → e o / _C{$,#} (in verbs)</rule>
<rule>— i → eː oː / else</rule>
<rule>— aw → aːw</rule>
<rule>— aj → eː / _$</rule>
; Unstressed-vowel correspondences:
<rule>— aj → ɛː / _#</rule>
<rule>— {o,u}(ː) → iː / _$%oː</rule>
<rule>— oː → uː</rule>
<rule>— a → ∅ / _#</rule>
<rule>— a → _$%%(…)”</rule>
<rule>— a → ə / _R if ə in an adjacent syllable</rule>
<rule>— a → ə / R_ if ə in an adjacent syllable</rule>
<rule>— i → ə / _R if a frontal allophone of ə in an adjacent syllable</rule>
<rule>— i → ə / R_ if a frontal allophone of ə in an adjacent syllable</rule>
<rule>— u → ə / _R if a backed allophone of ə in an adjacent syllable</rule>
<rule>— u → ə / R_ if a backed allophone of ə in an adjacent syllable</rule>
<rule>— i → a / _R</rule>
<rule>— i → a / R_</rule>
<rule>— a i → aː eː / _%”</rule>
<rule>— u → ɔ / _C{$,#}</rule>
<rule>— u → ∅ / ! _Cː</rule>
<rule>— aj aw → eː oː</rule>
<rule>p b t d k ɡ → b v θ ð x ɣ / non-intial singletons</rule>
<rule>ʕ → ∅ / _{$,#}</rule>
<rule>j → ∅ / E_ (not clear whether only short E or long also)</rule>
<rule>at → aː / _# (in feminine noun endings)</rule>
</section>

<h2>6.2.2.1.19 Biblical Hebrew to Modern Israeli Hebrew</h2>
<cite>Maknas, from http://www.adath-shalom.ca/history_of_hebrew.htm “and other sources”</cite>
; NB: These aren’t all true sound changes per se, since Modern Israeli Hebrew was artificially revived and is an amalgamation of dialects.
<rule>{e(ː),ɛ} → ɛ</rule>
<rule>Vː → V[-long]</rule>
<rule>ə → a / near gutturals</rule>
<rule>ə → ɛ / #R_C or when breaking up what would otherwise be a three-consonant cluster; in the case of two schwas, only the first one is dropped</rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / else</rule>
<rule>w θ ð ɣ → v t d ɡ (sometimes)</rule>
<rule>x ʕ → χ ʔ</rule>
<rule>sˤ tˤ ħ q → s t χ k</rule>
<rule>h → ∅ / _#</rule>
<rule>ʔ → ∅ / ! in onset of U[+stress] (colloquial)</rule>
<rule>h → ∅ (colloquial)</rule>
<rule>Cː → C[-long]</rule>
<rule>r → ʁ</rule>
</section>

<h2>7.11 Proto-Algonquian to Munsee Delaware</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Goddard, Ives (1982), “The Historical Phonology of Munsee”. International Journal of American Linguistics, 48:16 – 48</cite>
<rule>tʃ → t / in nouns</rule>
<rule>t s → tʃ ʃ / in diminutives</rule>
<rule>{θ,l} → r → l</rule>
<rule>{θ,ʃ} → {r,l}</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / m_C</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / {p,m}_#</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / ! {k,p,m}_</rule>
<rule>Cʷ → C / _ə{(C){p,kʷ},m,w}</rule>
<rule>kw pw mw → kʷ pʷ mʷ</rule>
<rule>we → wə → oː / ! adjacent to {p,m,k}</rule>
<rule>j → ∅ / C_</rule>
<rule>ʔ → h / _C ! C = l, or when reduplicated</rule>
<rule>h → ∅ / _{s,x}</rule>
<rule>{nθ,nl} → hl</rule>
<rule>k → ∅ / h_ (sometimes restored via analogy, e.g., in verbs)</rule>
<rule>θ ʃ x → x s h / _{p,k}</rule>
<rule>{tʃ,ç} → h / _k</rule>
<rule>i o → iː oː</rule>
<rule>Vː → V[-long] / _hC</rule>
<rule>V → ∅ / _# ! some monosyllables and analogical developments, in the latter of which long vowels were shortened</rule>
; “weak”
<rule>{a,ə} → ∅ / _{x,h} “in the odd-numbered of any sequence of one or more short-vowel open syllables”; such vowels are considered</rule>
<rule>ə[+weak] → ∅ / #_C</rule>
<rule>ə[+weak] → ∅ / _C[+voiced] (sporadic)</rule>
; NC sequences assimilate the nasal to the POA of the following consonant, which is then voiced
<rule>a[+weak] ə[+weak] → ə ∅ / a_Z[+voiced]</rule>
; Synchronic alterations:
<rule>ə → o / _h{p,kʷ,w,m}</rule>
<rule>ə → i / _hC</rule>
<rule>ə → o / _x{p,kʷ,V[+round]}</rule>
<rule>ə → o / {p,m}_x</rule>
<rule>x → χʷ / o(ː)_{V,#}</rule>
; “[i]n a nonrounding environment”
<rule>ə → a / _x</rule>
<rule>ə → o / _ŋkʷ</rule>
<rule>V[+high] → ə / _j</rule>
<rule>V[+high] → ə / _w (sporadic)</rule>
</section>

<h2>7.12 Proto-Algonquian to Menominee</h2>
<cite>Whimemsz, from Hockett, C. F. (1981), “The Phonological History of Menominee”. Anthropological Linguistics 23(2): 51-87; and Miner, Kenneth L. (1979), “Theoretical Implications of the Great Menominee Vowel Shift”. Kansas Working Papers in Linguistics 4(1): 7-25.</cite>
<rule>we je → o i / _C</rule>
<rule>we → o / #_</rule>
<rule>θ → s / _O</rule>
<rule>θ → r</rule>
; “[does not apply in disyllabic words containing two short vowels]”
<rule>V[-long] → ∅ / _#</rule>
<rule>∅ → h / V[-long]_#</rule>
<rule>H → ∅ / _m</rule>
<rule>{s,r} → h / _O</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / h_V</rule>
<rule>a → o / $am_w</rule>
; “when V is the second vowel of a word and follows a short-vowel syllable. Does not apply in glottal words”
<rule>V → Vː</rule>
<rule>e → i / Vː%_ ! _H</rule>
<rule>N → h / _{O,r}</rule>
<rule>e → i / #(C)_ ! _H</rule>
; “when in the second syllable of glottal words”
<rule>e → i / _{k,m}</rule>
<rule>{w,j} → ∅ / C_#</rule>
<rule>C → ∅ / C_#</rule>
<rule>wi(ː) → o(ː) / C_w</rule>
<rule>ʃ tʃ → s ts</rule>
; “[i.e., when following a cluster but not followed by a cluster. Only applies ‘after the first long vowel of a nonglottal word, and everywhere in a glottal word’]”
<rule>Vː → V[-long] / CC(G)_C{V,#}</rule>
<rule>V → Vː / _CC in even syllables</rule>
; “does not apply in the second syllable of a non-glottal word”
<rule>Vː → V[-long] / _C{V,#} in even syllables;</rule>
<rule>e(ː) i → æ(ː) e</rule>
; “[blocked when iː or a C+G sequence follows anywhere in the word, but does apply if æ(ː) intervenes before any following iː or C+G]”
<rule>iː oː oʔ → eː uː uʔ</rule>
<rule>{wiː,jiː,weː,jeː,wæː,jæː} {wi,ji,we,je,wæ,jæ} → iː i / C_</rule>
<rule>æ → e / in odd syllables ! _{w,j,H}</rule>
<rule>r → n</rule>
<rule>wa ja → uə̯ iə̯ / C_</rule>
</section>

<rule>C[-voiced] → C[+voiced] / N_</rule>
<rule>VNs VNʃ → V[+nas]z V[+nas]ʒ / not universal?</rule>

<h2>8.1.1 Early Middle Japanese to Modern Japanese</h2>
<cite>Zhen Lin</cite>
; NB: The ordering of these changes may be slightly anachronic.
<rule>p → ɸ</rule>
<rule>ɸ → w / V_V</rule>
<rule>(w)e → je</rule>
<rule>∅ → w / _o</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / ! _{a,o}</rule>
<rule>au iu uu eu ou → ɔː juː uː joː oː</rule>
<rule>j → ∅ / _e</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / _o</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / k_a</rule>
<rule>ɸ → h / ! _u</rule>
; “Affrication of /ti di/ probably happened very early. Denasalization of the prenasalized stops happened relatively later. Final /m/ merged with /n/ at some point, and [dʒ] (from */dj/) and [ʒ] (&lt; */zj/) also merged.”
<rule>ɔː → oː</rule>

<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2011), “Altaic languages”. <h2>8.3 Proto-Altaic to Proto-Mongolic</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2011), “Altaic languages”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Altaic_languages&amp;oldid=453651228&gt;, citing Sarostin, Sergei A., Anna V. Dybo, and Oleg A. Mudrak (2003), Etymological Dictionary of the Altaic Languages. Leiden: Brill Academic Publishers</cite>
; NB: Does not include clusters.
<rule>a → {a,i} / _Ce</rule>
<rule>a → {a,e} / _Ci</rule>
<rule>a → {a,i,e} / _Co</rule>
<rule>a → {a,o,u} / _Cu</rule>
<rule>e o u æ ø y → {a,e} {o,u} {a,o,u a {a,o,u} {o,u,i} / _Ca</rule>
<rule>a e i o u æ ø y → {a,i} {e,ja} {e,i} {ø,y,o} {o,u,y} {i,a,e} {e,ø} {ø,y,o,u} / _Ce</rule>
<rule>i → e / P_Ci</rule>
<rule>a e u æ ø y → {a,e} {e,i} {y,ø} {i,e} {i,e,ø} {ø,y,o,u} / _Ci</rule>
<rule>e → {y,ø} / P_Co</rule>
<rule>e → {y,ø} / C_Po</rule>
<rule>e → o / P_Cu</rule>
<rule>e → o / C_Pu</rule>
<rule>a e o i æ ø y → {a,i,e} {a,e} u {o,u} e {ø,y,o,u} {o,u} / _Co</rule>
<rule>a e {o,u} æ ø y → {a,o,u} {e,a} {o,u} {a,o,u} {e,i,u} {i,o,u,y,ø} / _Cu</rule>
<rule>b → h / medially, ! {r(ʲ),l(ʲ)}_ or _ɡ</rule>
<rule>pʰ → {h,j} / #_</rule>
<rule>pʰ → {b,h} / medially</rule>
<rule>pʰ → b / #_U[+high pitch]</rule>
<rule>p → h (sporadic)</rule>
<rule>p → b</rule>
<rule>tʰ → d / _#</rule>
<rule>t(ʰ) d → tʃ dʒ / _i</rule>
<rule>tʰ → t / else</rule>
<rule>tʃ → dʒ / #_i</rule>
<rule>tʃ → d / #</rule>
<rule>tʃʰ → tʃ</rule>
<rule>ɡ → h / ! {C,V}_h</rule>
<rule>k → ɡ / ! #_</rule>
<rule>kʰ → ɡ / {C,V}_h</rule>
<rule>kʰ → k / else</rule>
<rule>z → s</rule>
<rule>ʃ → tʃ / #_a</rule>
<rule>ʃ → s / else</rule>
<rule>nʲ → dʒ / #_</rule>
<rule>nʲ → {j,n} / else</rule>
<rule>ŋ → ɡ / #_u</rule>
<rule>ŋ → n / #_{a,o,e}</rule>
<rule>ŋ → {∅,j} / #_</rule>
<rule>ŋ → {m,n,ŋ,h}</rule>
<rule>rʲ → r</rule>
<rule>l → {n,l} / #_</rule>
<rule>lʲ → dʒ / #_i</rule>
<rule>lʲ → d / #_</rule>
<rule>lʲ → l</rule>
; Loss of syllable pitch and length
<rule>j → {j,h}</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>8.4 Proto-Altaic to Proto-Tungusic</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2011), “Altaic languages”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Altaic_languages&amp;oldid=453651228&gt;, citing Sarostin, Sergei A., Anna V. Dybo, and Oleg A. Mudrak (2003), Etymological Dictionary of the Altaic Languages. Leiden: Brill Academic Publishers</cite>
; NB: Does not include clusters.
<rule>o → {o,u} / _CV</rule>
<rule>æ → i / {s,ʃ,x}_Ca</rule>
<rule>{u,ø,y} æ → {o,u} ia / _Ca</rule>
<rule>y → u / P_C{e,i}</rule>
<rule>æ ø → i {o,u} / _Ce</rule>
<rule>æ → i / {s,ʃ,x}_Ci</rule>
<rule>æ ø y → ia {o,u} i / _Ci</rule>
<rule>{u,æ} ø → {o,u} i / _Co</rule>
<rule>ø → i / {s,ʃ,x}_Cu</rule>
<rule>{u,æ,y} ø → {o,u} ia / _Cu</rule>
<rule>p → b / medially</rule>
<rule>pʰ → p</rule>
<rule>t → dʒ / #_{æ,ø,y}</rule>
<rule>t → d / #_</rule>
<rule>tʰ tʃʰ → t tʃ</rule>
<rule>k → {k,ɡ} / #_</rule>
<rule>k → ɡ</rule>
<rule>kʰ → x / #_</rule>
<rule>kʰ → {x,k}</rule>
<rule>z → s</rule>
<rule>rʲ lʲ → r l</rule>
<rule>U[-long +low pitch] U[+long -low pitch] → U[+long] U[-long]</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>8.5 Proto-Altaic to Proto-Turkic</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2011), “Altaic languages”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Altaic_languages&amp;oldid=453651228&gt;, citing Sarostin, Sergei A., Anna V. Dybo, and Oleg A. Mudrak (2003), Etymological Dictionary of the Altaic Languages. Leiden: Brill Academic Publishers</cite>
; NB: Does not include clusters.
<rule>a ø → {a,ʌ} a / P_Ca</rule>
<rule>a e i u æ ø y → a {a,ʌ,e} {ɯ,i} {u,o} {ia,ja,ɛ} {ia,ja} ɯ / _Ca</rule>
<rule>y → i / {r(ʲ),l(ʲ)}_e</rule>
<rule>e → ja / #_C{e,i}</rule>
<rule>ø → ʌ / P_Ce</rule>
<rule>i → e / {r(ʲ),l(ʲ)}_e</rule>
<rule>a {e,i} o u æ ø y → {ɛ,a} ɛ {ø,o} {y,u} {ia,ja,ɛ} {ia,ja} {y,ø} / _Ci</rule>
<rule>æ → a / P_Co</rule>
<rule>æ → ʌ / P_Cu</rule>
<rule>a e i æ ø y → {o,ja,aj} {ʌ,ɜ} ɯ {ia,ja} {o,u} {u,o} / _Co</rule>
<rule>e i æ ø y → {ɛ,a,ʌ} {ɯ,i} {e,a} {u,o} ɯ / _Cu</rule>
<rule>{pʰ,ŋ} → {∅,j} / #_</rule>
<rule>pʰ → p</rule>
<rule>tʰ → d / #_(V){lʲ,r(ʲ)}</rule>
<rule>tʰ → t</rule>
<rule>{t,tʃ} → d / #_</rule>
<rule>k → ɡ / _(V)r</rule>
<rule>kʰ → k </rule>
<rule>ʃ → tʃ / #_a</rule>
<rule>ʃ → s </rule>
; Loss of syllable pitch
<rule>m n(ʲ) → b j / #_</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>8.5.b Proto-Altaic to Proto-Turkic</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Firespeaker.org wiki contributors (2014), “Turkic sound changes”. &lt;http://wiki.firespeaker.org/Turkic_sound_changes&gt;</cite>
<rule>{ʒ,dʒ} → j / #_ (marked as to Pre-Proto-Turkic)</rule>
<rule>{d,n} → j / #_ (?) (marked as to Pre-Proto-Turkic)</rule>
<rule>{N,l,r,ʃ,z} → ∅ / #_</rule>
<rule>p → ɸ → h / #_</rule>
<rule>d ɡ → t k (may have been part of a more sweeping merger; Firespeaker calls it “lenis-fortis”)</rule>
<rule>{d,n}ʲ sʲ → j ʃ / #_</rule>
<rule>rʲ → z</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>8.5.1 Proto-Turkic to Proto-Kypchak</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Firespeaker.org wiki contributors (2014), “Turkic sound changes”. &lt;http://wiki.firespeaker.org/Turkic_sound_changes&gt;</cite>
<rule>V[- long] → ∅ (shared with Old Turkic)</rule>
<rule>h → ∅ (shared with Old Turkic)</rule>
<rule>nʲ → j</rule>
<rule>b…n → m…n</rule>
<rule>d ɣ → t x / _#</rule>
<rule>d → t / #_ (“kind of”, something about evidence from borrowings)</rule>
<rule>V → V[- round] / U_</rule>
<rule>b → v / V_</rule>
<rule>v → w</rule>
<rule>ɡm rɡ → mɡ ɡr (this second one is listed as → rɡ but it might be a typo)</rule>
<rule>rd → dr (possibly sporadic and/or confined to Kazakh)</rule>
<rule>ɣ → w / {a,u,i,o}_</rule>
<rule>{e,æ}b ub → ew uw</rule>
<rule>{d,ɡ} → j / ø_</rule>
<rule>d → ð → j / V_</rule>
<rule>ɡ → w / V_</rule>
<rule>ew (→ øj) → yj</rule>
<rule>æ → e</rule>
<rule>s → tʃ / _Vtʃ</rule>
<rule>s → ç / _Vç</rule>
<rule>a → æ / ! _B</rule>
<rule>f → w / _V</rule>
<rule>f → p / else</rule>
<rule>ŋ → ɡ / syllable-final</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>8.5.1.1 Proto-Kypchak to Kazakh</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Firespeaker.org wiki contributors (2014), “Turkic sound changes”. &lt;http://wiki.firespeaker.org/Turkic_sound_changes&gt;</cite>
; NB: Most likely incomplete; all changes listed are stated as being “[s]hared with Nogay and Karakalpak”.
<rule>tʃ → ʃ</rule>
<rule>j → dʒ / #_ (did not occur in Qara Nogay)</rule>
<rule>dʒ → ʒ (did not occur in Qara Nogay or Central Nogay)</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / ɯ_</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>8.5.1.2 Proto-Kypchak to Kyrgyz</h2>

<rule>j → ∅ / _l (sporadic?)</rule>
<rule>b → m / V_V (sporadic?)</rule>
<rule>{u,ɯ}w {i,y}w aw {æ,e}w → uː yː oː øː</rule>
<rule>ɣ → ∅ / V_V</rule>
<rule>æ Vh {ʕ,h} → ɑː Vː ∅ (seems to have largely been confined to loanwords from Persian)</rule>
<rule>j → dʒ / #_</rule>
<rule>x → q</rule>
<rule>nj → jn</rule>
<rule>∅ → U / #_{l,r} (not sure what 〈U〉 represents here; maybe just some sort of back vowel?)</rule>
<rule>e → i / _ɡ</rule>
<rule>e → i / k_y (maybe they mean k_j?)</rule>
</section>

<rule>{e,ɤ}ː → je (the second one is conjectured based on my admittedly sparse knowledge of Turkish; I can only guess that 〈ė〉 is some sort of back unrounded vowel)</rule>
<rule>o oɡ ø øɡ iɡ → wo ɥø oː øː iː</rule>
<rule>a{ɡ̌(ɯ),b} {o{ɡ̌,b},aɡ̌u} u{ɡ̌,b} → ɰa wo uː</rule>
<rule>iɡ̌ → ɯː → iː (but original ɯː unaffected?)</rule>
<rule>eɡ → {je,iː,ji}</rule>
<rule>d s {ʃ,z} → t ∅ s / V_V</rule>
<rule>s → ∅ / #_</rule>
<rule>{z,ʃ} → h</rule>
<rule>j → s (possibly only initially?)</rule>
</section>

<h2>9 Austroasiatic</h2>

<h2>9.1 Vietic</h2>
</section>
<section>
<h2>9.1.1 Proto-Vietic to Muong Khen</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Thompson, Laurence C. (1976), “Proto-Viet-Muong Phonology”. Oceanic Linguistics Special Publications 13, Austroasiatic Studies II:1113 – 1203; Wikipedia contributors (2012). “Hanoi”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hanoi&amp;oldid=509052974&gt;; Wikipedia contributors (2012), “Vietnamese Language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Cite&amp;page=Vietnamese_language&amp;id=509331797&gt;; Gage, William W. (1985), “Glottal Stops and Vietnamese Tonogenesis”. Oceanic Linguistics Special Publications 20:21 – 36; and Thompson, Laurence C. (1979?), “More on Viet-Muong Tonal Developments”</cite>
; Tonogenesis
; 1. “Constricted” (laryngealized?)
; 2. Terminates in a glottal stop if no final stop
; Presyllables don’t seem to have affected Muong much.
; Initials:
<rule>s → h</rule>
<rule>cʰ → s</rule>
<rule>tʰ → h (Only seems to have occurred with first-register tones)</rule>
<rule>{kʰ,ɡʱ} → x (Presyllables don’t seem to have affected this much)</rule>
<rule>m n → b d (Only seems to have occurred with first-register tones)</rule>
<rule>{pj,bj} {tj,dj} {cj,ɟj} → b d j</rule>
<rule>ɓ ɗ → b d (Only seems to have occurred with first-register tones)</rule>
<rule>{n̥j,nj,ɲ̊j,ɲj} → ɲ j</rule>
<rule>N[-voiced] W[-voiced] → N[+voiced] W[+voiced]</rule>
<rule>(h)ə{p,b} → t / _l</rule>
<rule>m → ∅ / _l</rule>
; Miscellanea:
<rule>tɹ → tʰ</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / tʰV_k (conjectured)</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>9.1.2 Proto-Vietic to Middle Vietnamese</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Thompson, Laurence C. (1976), “Proto-Viet-Muong Phonology”. Oceanic Linguistics Special Publications 13, Austroasiatic Studies II:1113 – 1203; Wikipedia contributors (2012). “Hanoi”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hanoi&amp;oldid=509052974&gt;; Wikipedia contributors (2012), “Vietnamese Language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Cite&amp;page=Vietnamese_language&amp;id=509331797&gt;; Gage, William W. (1985), “Glottal Stops and Vietnamese Tonogenesis”. Oceanic Linguistics Special Publications 20:21 – 36; and Thompson, Laurence C. (1979?), “More on Viet-Muong Tonal Developments”</cite>
; Initials:
<rule>bʱ {tʰ,dʱ} {t*ʰ,d*ʱ} {kʰ,ɡʱ} → pʰ t tʰ kʰ (after *kʰ *ɡʱ, only first-register tones may occur)</rule>
<rule>(h)ə{p,b} (h)ə{t,d} (h)ə{c,ɟ} (h)ə{k,ɡ} → β dʲ ɟ ɡ</rule>
<rule>{pj,bj} {tj,dj} {cj,ɟj} → {β,w} dʲ ɟ</rule>
<rule>ɓ ɗ → m n (For some reason it seems that only first-register tones can occur in this environment)</rule>
<rule>{n̥j,nj,ɲ̊j,ɲj} → ɲ (Thompson appears to me to have hedged a bit on the last one; based on other evidence in the paper I’m sticking this one as a palatal nasal)</rule>
<rule>tʃ → Ω (This is my own notation. I don’t have a clue what the intermediate form was; became something else in different dialects)</rule>
<rule>N[-voiced] W[-voiced] → N[+voiced] W[+voiced]</rule>
<rule>((h)ə)p d → b t / _l</rule>
<rule>t → ∅ / _ɹ (only first-register tones can occur in this environment)</rule>
<rule>t*ʰ d ɡ → tʰ t k / _w</rule>
<rule>s → t(ʰ?)</rule>
; Finals:
<rule>{əkʰ,əɡɦj} → ɟ (I think Thompson implied this was just a bit of a kludge)</rule>
<rule>l → ∅ / {i,e}_</rule>
<rule>l → j / else</rule>
; Thompson seems to list some changes as affecting Modern Vietnamese but I was unsure of where to put them so they’ll go here:
<rule>c ɲ → t n / ! E_ (apparently the precursor to Vietnamese short *a was treated as a short vowel here)</rule>
; In the original those first vowels were underlined.
<rule>a ɔ → ɨə uə</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>9.1.2.1 Middle Vietnamese to Hanoi Vietnamese</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Thompson, Laurence C. (1976), “Proto-Viet-Muong Phonology”. Oceanic Linguistics Special Publications 13, Austroasiatic Studies II:1113 – 1203; Wikipedia contributors (2012). “Hanoi”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hanoi&amp;oldid=509052974&gt;; Wikipedia contributors (2012), “Vietnamese Language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Cite&amp;page=Vietnamese_language&amp;id=509331797&gt;; Gage, William W. (1985), “Glottal Stops and Vietnamese Tonogenesis”. Oceanic Linguistics Special Publications 20:21 – 36; and Thompson, Laurence C. (1979?), “More on Viet-Muong Tonal Developments”</cite>
; Tonogenesis
; 1. Tense when _S#; laryngealized elsewhere
; 2. Laryngealized
; Initials:
<rule>pʰ → f</rule>
<rule>kʰ → x (only seems to have occurred with first-register tones)</rule>
<rule>{β,w} {dʲ,ɟ} → v z</rule>
<rule>ɹ → z (only seems to have occurred with first-register tones)</rule>
<rule>bl → z</rule>
<rule>ml → mɲ → ɲ (Thompson seems to indicate that this may have become [l] as well; only seems to have occurred with second-register tones)</rule>
; Vowels:
<rule>Ω cʰ → s tɕ</rule>
<rule>ɨ → i / _(ə)w</rule>
; Miscellanea:
<rule>ɛ → a / _C[+palatal]</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / tV_wk (conjectured)</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>9.1.2.2 Middle Vietnamese to Saigon Vietnamese</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Thompson, Laurence C. (1976), “Proto-Viet-Muong Phonology”. Oceanic Linguistics Special Publications 13, Austroasiatic Studies II:1113 – 1203; Wikipedia contributors (2012). “Hanoi”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hanoi&amp;oldid=509052974&gt;; Wikipedia contributors (2012), “Vietnamese Language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Cite&amp;page=Vietnamese_language&amp;id=509331797&gt;; Gage, William W. (1985), “Glottal Stops and Vietnamese Tonogenesis”. Oceanic Linguistics Special Publications 20:21 – 36; and Thompson, Laurence C. (1979?), “More on Viet-Muong Tonal Developments”</cite>
; Tonogenesis
; 1. Level when _S#; dipping otherwise
; 2. Laryngealized
; Initials:
<rule>pʰ → f</rule>
<rule>kʰ → x (only seems to have occurred with first-register tones)</rule>
<rule>{β,w} → bj~vj~v</rule>
<rule>{bl,tl} → ʈ (?)</rule>
<rule>dʲ ɟ → z j</rule>
<rule>m → ∅ / _l</rule>
<rule>Ω cʰ → ʂ ʈʂ</rule>
; Finals:
<rule>ɹ → ʐ (sometimes?)</rule>
<rule>c ɲ t n → t n k ŋ / a_ (short /a/ only)</rule>
<rule>c ɲ → t n / {i,e}_</rule>
; Vowels:
<rule>{c,ɲ} → ∅ / else</rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / {i,ɨ}_{p,m.w}</rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / ɨ_j</rule>
; The contrast between short /a/ and short /ə/ is neutralized when _w{k,ŋ}
<rule>ə → ∅ / u_{m,j}</rule>
<rule>a → aː / _{w,j}</rule>
<rule>ə(ː) ɛ → ɨ ɛə / _K</rule>
; Miscellanea:
<rule>ɛ → a / _C[+palatal]</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / tV_wk (conjectured)</rule>
</section>

<rule>j → {d,t} / #_</rule>
<rule>j z → d j</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / #_</rule>
<rule>R → r</rule>

; A:
<rule>iw uj → {i,?} i / _#</rule>
; B:
<rule>— aj aw → ej ow / _#</rule>
<rule>— aj aw → e ow / _#</rule>
<rule>C[+ voice] → C[- voice] / _#</rule>
<rule>HəS → (h)ə(N)S / #_</rule>

<section>
<h2>10.1.1.1 Proto-Bali-Sasak-Sumbawan to Balinese</h2>
<cite>TinyMusic, from Adelaar, Alexander (2005), “Malayo-Sumbawan”. Oceanic Linguistics 44(2):357 – 388</cite>
<rule>r → {r,h} → {∅,h}</rule>
<rule>h → {∅,h}</rule>
<rule>w → b / i_#</rule>
; “ə assimilated to the following vowel after the loss of *-r-”
<rule>ej ow → i u</rule>
<rule>a → ə</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>10.1.1.2 Proto-Bali-Sasak-Sumbawan to Sasak</h2>
<cite>TinyMusic, from Adelaar, Alexander (2005), “Malayo-Sumbawan”. Oceanic Linguistics 44(2):357 – 388</cite>
<rule>h → q / _# (might’ve been a retention?)</rule>
<rule>h → ∅</rule>
; “*i and *u often become mid-vowels”
<rule>iw ow ej → i o e / _#</rule>
<rule>V(h) → V(q) / _# (again, might’ve been a retention?)</rule>
<rule>a →ə / _# (Meno-mene and Mriak-mriku only)</rule>
<rule>d → r / medial (Meno-mene and Mriak-mriku only)</rule>
<rule>r → h / _# (Meno-mene and Mriak-mriku only)</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>10.1.1.3 Proto-Bali-Sasak-Sumbawan to Sumbawan</h2>
<cite>TinyMusic, from Adelaar, Alexander (2005), “Malayo-Sumbawan”. Oceanic Linguistics 44(2):357 – 388</cite>
<rule>h → q / _# (might’ve been a retention?)</rule>
<rule>h → ∅</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / i_#</rule>
<rule>ej ow → e o / _#</rule>
<rule>u i → o e / sometimes</rule>
<rule>V(h) → V(q) / _# (again, might’ve been a retention?)</rule>
<rule>S[+ voice] → ∅ / _N</rule>
<rule>S[+ voice] → ∅ / N_</rule>
; “[C]ontraction of adjacent vowels” (not in Besar)
<rule>u → i / _{s,t,r,n,l} (blocked in Pusu)</rule>
<rule>b → ∅ / medial (sporadic)</rule>
</section>

<h2>10.1.2 Proto-Austronesian to Proto-Philippine</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Llamzon, Teodoro A. (1975), “Proto-Philippine Phonology”. Archipel 9(1):29 – 42; and from other changes and information from this document</cite>
<rule>*T → t</rule>
<rule>{*D,*Z,z} → d / #_</rule>
<rule>*D → d / _#</rule>
<rule>*R → ɡ / #_</rule>
<rule>*R → {l,ɡ} / _#</rule>
<rule>ɲ → n</rule>
<rule>c → s</rule>
</section>

<rule>{a,e} → o / _w#</rule>

<rule>{ts,ʈ} {ɟ͡ʝ,ɖ} {lʲ,ɲ} {ʔ,x,s,h} ʀ → t d n ∅ r (velar fricative is conjectured; changes → d “unsure”)</rule>

<rule>z → j (fricative changes to approximant)</rule>
</section>

; “(for certain consonant combinations, which the paper doesn’t specify)”
<rule>∅ → ə / C_C</rule>

; “(result of /e/ or /o/ unpredictable)”
<rule>ə → {e,o} / stressed;</rule>
<rule>p → w</rule>

<rule>wə → u / #_</rule>
<rule>j l → r j</rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / _j</rule>

<rule>ə → ∅ / _#</rule>
; “(sporadic)”
<rule>ə → ∅</rule>
<rule>t → ð / ! adjacent to S</rule>
<rule>s → t</rule>
<rule>ʀ → r / _C[+dental]</rule>
<rule>ʀ → s</rule>
<rule>d → r</rule>
; “(only one example)”
<rule>ɖ → ɽ</rule>
<rule>ɲ → n (except possibly to n when #_)</rule>

<rule>rl → lː</rule>
<rule>ɟ → k / _C#</rule>
<rule>ɟ → s</rule>
<rule>ŋ → ∅ / C_#</rule>
; “unpredictably, to eliminate sV(C)t and tV(C)s sequences”
<rule>t → {s,ð} /</rule>
<rule>∅ → ŋ / #_V</rule>
<rule>q → χ (→ ʕ)</rule>

<h2>10.2.6 Proto-Malayo-Polynesian to Proto-North Sarawak</h2>
<cite>Whimemsz, from Blust, Robert (2002), “Kiput Historical Phonology”. Oceanic Linguistics 41(2):384 – 438; and Blust, Robert (2007), “Òma Lóngh Historical Phonology”. Oceanic Linguistics 46(1):1 – 53</cite>

<rule>h → ʔ / a_{i,u}</rule>

<rule>a → ə / _UU(U…)#</rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / _V</rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ VC_CV</rule>
; Nasal assimilation to following stops in some words; in other words it results in a geminate stop
; Postvocalic obstruents with different POAs become geminates of the second when “in reduplicated monosyllabic roots” and “in non-reduplicated bases which had undergone the change of schwa syncope in medial syllables”
<rule>C → Cː / ə_V (?)</rule>
<rule>ɟ(ː) → d(ː)</rule>
; “voiced stops with voiceless releases. . .treated as unit phonemes, not clusters)”
<rule>bː dː dzː ɡː → bʱ dʱ dzʱ ɡʱ (Whimemsz says these become</rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / #_UU(U…)# “(i.e., in word-initial position in prepenultimate syllables)</rule>
<rule>pː tː cː kː → p t c k</rule>
<rule>c → s</rule>
</section>

<h2>10.2.6.1 Proto-North Sarawak to Kiput</h2>
<cite>Whimemsz, from Blust, Robert (2002), “Kiput Historical Phonology”. Oceanic Linguistics 41(2):384 – 438</cite>
; Stress reassignment to the final syllable
<rule>ʔ → ∅ / V_V</rule>
; “(in some forms)”
<rule>k → ∅ / V_V</rule>
<rule>ai au → ai̯ au̯ / _#</rule>
<rule>ə → a / _ʔ#</rule>
<rule>ai au → ɛː ɔː / _…#</rule>
<rule>i u → əi̯ əu̯ / _#</rule>
<rule>∅ → h / a_#</rule>
<rule>s → ∅ / V_V (sporadic)</rule>
<rule>V₀V₀ → V₀</rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / adjacent to a vowel</rule>
; “(applies to diphthongal nuclei as well a[s] monophthongs)”
<rule>V[+stress] → Vː / _C# ! V = ə and/or C = h</rule>
<rule>ʀ → {l,ɾ} / ! _# (the latter is more common)</rule>
<rule>l → ∅ / ! _# (irregular)</rule>
; “(also cases of (C)u → w /__V)”
<rule>u → əw / _V</rule>
; “(also cases of (C)i → j /__V)”
<rule>i → əj / _V</rule>
<rule>w j → v ɟ</rule>
<rule>iu̯ → ui̯</rule>
<rule>s → ∅ / _#</rule>
<rule>i u → ɛ ɔ / _C# ! _P (sporadic)</rule>
; “(in a handful of forms)”
<rule>ʀ → ʔ / _#</rule>
<rule>ʀ → ɾ</rule>
; “(blocked if there was an intervening nasal, and sometimes if there was an intervening voiceless stop or liquid)”
<rule>a → i / O[+voiced]…_(C)#</rule>
<rule>i u → əi̯ əu̯ / _(ʔ)#</rule>
<rule>əi̯ əu̯ → ai̯ au̯ / ! O[+voiced] earlier in the word</rule>
<rule>bʱ {dʱ,dzʱ} ɡʱ → f s k</rule>
<rule>f → s</rule>
<rule>v ɡ ɟ → f k c / V_V</rule>
<rule>v ɟ → f c / #_</rule>
<rule>{i,ɛ} {u,ɔ} → iə̯ uə̯ / _{k,ŋ}# “(and also sporadically before final *t and *n and some other consonants)</rule>
<rule>k ŋ → ʔ ∅ / Və̯_#</rule>
; “(free variants)”
<rule>ə → {ə,a} / _C#</rule>
; “Numerous different possible reflexes of NS[+voice] clusters”
<rule>NS[-voice] → Sː</rule>
; “(irregular)”
<rule>(C)V → ∅ / #_C…</rule>
<rule>n → l / #_</rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / #_</rule>
<rule>{l,ɾ} → n / _#</rule>
<rule>dz → d / #_ (though sometimes → {s,ɟ})</rule>
<rule>{s,c} → t / _V{s,c}V</rule>
<rule>b d → p t / _#</rule>
</section>

<h2>10.4.1 Proto-Micronesian to Marshallese</h2>
<cite>Ketsuban, from Hale, Mark, Historical Linguistics: Theory and Method</cite>
; NB: “. . .the precise contrast between *s and *S, and *t and *T is unknown, as is the precise phonetic nature of *c and *Z.” Blust (v.s.) rejected *T and *D; according to his reconstruction. . .*s and *S were /ç s/, respectively. The substitution of /ts/ for *T is inferred from the Wikipedia article but may be incorrect.
<rule>K → Kʷ / _{C[+round],V[+round]}</rule>
<rule>V[+high] → ɘ / _C[-high]</rule>
<rule>V → ∅ / _#</rule>
<rule>V[+mid] → ɘ / _C[+high] when stressed</rule>
<rule>V → ∅ / ”VC_</rule>
; “in CV reduplications”
<rule>V → ∅ /</rule>
<rule>a → ɜ / _wo</rule>
<rule>f → ∅ / #_{C[-low],V[-low]}</rule>
<rule>f → ɰ / #_aCo</rule>
<rule>f → j / else</rule>
<rule>∅ → ɰ / #_aC[-low]</rule>
<rule>∅ → j / #_{aC[+low],V[-back],C[-back]}</rule>
<rule>∅ → w / #_{C[+round],V[+round]}</rule>
<rule>{i,u} {e,o} → ɨ ɜ</rule>
<rule>p {t,ts} c → pʲ tʲ rʲ</rule>
<rule>{ç,s} x → tᵚ ∅</rule>
; “The author does not elaborate on the complex development of vowels without an onset consonant, other than to say that a glide is inserted (*Saa &gt; tᵚaɰ), nor does he go into more detail than to say that l and n generally develop into lʲ and nʲ before Proto-Micronesian front vowels, and l, r, and n turn into lᶬ, rᶬ, and nᶬ before a and lʷ, rʷ, and nʷ before Proto-Micronesian round vowels, but the author does not elaborate.”
<rule>m ɲ → mʲ nʲ</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>10.5 Proto-Austronesian to Proto-Ongan</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Blevins, Juliette (2007), “A Long Lost Sister of Proto-Austronesian? Proto-Ongan, Mother of Jarawa and Onge of the Andaman Islands”. Oceanic Linguistics 46(I):154 – 198</cite>
; NB: Blevins floats the idea that Proto-Ongan was a sister of rather than a daughter of Proto-Austronesian, but for reasons of simplicity in editing this document it is placed here.
<rule>b → ∅ / #_{u,i}</rule>
<rule>q → ∅ / #_V</rule>
<rule>q → k</rule>
<rule>{qʷ,ku,qu} → kʷ (note that PAn might have had *qʷ *kʷ → q {k,w} instead; may be a change from POn-PAn, if it existed)</rule>
<rule>{c,*C,s,*S} → c (again, possibly a change from POn-PAn, if it existed)</rule>
<rule>S → ∅ / _#</rule>
<rule>u a ə → {u,o} {a,e} e</rule>
<rule>ɟ ɡ *N *R → {ɟ,j} {ɟ,ɡ} {l,j} {l,r}</rule>
<rule>z → c (again, possibly evidence of a change from POn-PAn, if it existed)</rule>
<rule>h → {h,j,∅} (Blevins has marked what apparently is *ɟ but I’m assuming it’s an error)</rule>
<rule>e → ə / _N when unstressed ! Ḱ_ (?; included here based on a comment earlier in the paper, but not listed on the correspondence list)</rule>
<rule>{m,ɲ} n → {ɲ,∅} {ŋ,∅} / _# (first change marked “in progress?”)</rule>
<rule>aj → e</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>10.5.1 Proto-Ongan to Jarawa</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Blevins, Juliette (2007), “A Long Lost Sister of Proto-Austronesian? Proto-Ongan, Mother of Jarawa and Onge of the Andaman Islands”. Oceanic Linguistics 46(I):154 – 198</cite>
<rule>e → ə / _N, when unstressed (?)</rule>
<rule>n → ŋ / _# (?)</rule>
<rule>k(ʷ) → h(ʷ)</rule>
<rule>∅ → a / h#_ (that’s not a typo; this happens across the word boundary)</rule>
<rule>ɡ → j</rule>
<rule>e → {e,ə,o} / _V</rule>
<rule>e → ∅ / _# (?)</rule>
; /a e/ reduce when unstressed (change seems to be ongoing?)
<rule>p → b / #_ (change seems to be ongoing)</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>10.5.2 Proto-Ongan to Onge</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Blevins, Juliette (2007), “A Long Lost Sister of Proto-Austronesian? Proto-Ongan, Mother of Jarawa and Onge of the Andaman Islands”. Oceanic Linguistics 46(I):154 – 198</cite>
<rule>e → ə / _N, when unstressed (?)</rule>
<rule>n → ŋ / _# (?)</rule>
<rule>d → r / V_{V,#}</rule>
<rule>{w,r} → ∅ / {a,e}_#</rule>
<rule>∅ → e / C_#</rule>
<rule>p → b</rule>
<rule>aw → o</rule>
<rule>e → {e,ə,o} / _V</rule>
<rule>N → n / _{d,l}</rule>
<rule>ɡd ɡl → dː lː</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>10.6 Proto-Austronesian to Proto-Paiwan</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Chen, Chun-Mei (2006), A Comparative Study on Formosan Phonology: Paiwan and Budai Rukai 313 – 320</cite>
<rule>t₁ d₁ d₃ Z → t d ɖ ɟ</rule>
<rule>l *L → ɭ ʎ</rule>
<rule>b d₂ → {v,b} z</rule>
<rule>S₁ s c → s t ts</rule>
<rule>Vː → V[- long]</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>10.6.1 Proto-Paiwan to Northern Paiwan</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Chen, Chun-Mei (2006), A Comparative Study on Formosan Phonology: Paiwan and Budai Rukai 313 – 320</cite>
; Something about final stress and preceding /ə/
<rule>c ɟ q ɭ → t d ʔ l</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>10.6.2 Proto-Paiwan to Central Paiwan</h2>

; Something about final stress and preceding /ə/
<rule>w → v / _#</rule>
</section>
<section>

<cite>Pogostick Man, from Chen, Chun-Mei (2006), A Comparative Study on Formosan Phonology: Paiwan and Budai Rukai 313 – 320</cite>
; Something about final stress and preceding /ə/
<rule>k r → ʔ ɣ</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>10.7 Proto-Austronesian to Proto-Rukai</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Chen, Chun-Mei (2006), A Comparative Study on Formosan Phonology: Paiwan and Budai Rukai 313 – 320</cite>
<rule>{t₁,c} {d₁,z} d₃ → t d ɖ</rule>
<rule>R l L → {r,ʔ} ɭ l</rule>
; Something about echo-vowel epenthesis and stress that isn’t really clear from skimming it
<rule>S₁ s d₂ *C → s θ ð ts (not sure what *C stands for here)</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>10.7.1 Proto-Rukai to Budai Rukai</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Chen, Chun-Mei (2006), A Comparative Study on Formosan Phonology: Paiwan and Budai Rukai 313 – 320</cite>
; Long vowels acquire a high-low contour, but it looks like this is more prosodic than anything
<rule>{v,ʔ} ð → ∅ j</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>10.8 Proto-Austronesian to Proto-Tsouic</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2016), “Tsouic languages”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tsouic_languages&amp;oldid=602917078&gt;</cite>
<rule>{*C,d} j *R → c z r</rule>
</section>

<h2>11 Northeast Caucasian</h2>
</section>
<section>
<h2>11.1 Proto-Northeast Caucasian to Proto-Avar-Andic</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from User:Petusek (2010), “User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast Caucasian”. ; *b is “[p]rone to change to *m”
<rule>l → {l,r}</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>11.1.1 Proto-Avar-Andic to Akhvakh</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from User:Petusek (2010), “User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast Caucasian”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast_Caucasian&amp;oldid=351133322&gt;, apparently citing Nichols, Johanna (2003), “The Nakh-Daghestanian Consonant Correspondences”, in Tuite, Kevin, and Dee Ann Holisky, Current Trends in Caucasian, East European, and Inner Asian Linguistics: Papers in Honor of Howard I. Aronson 207 – 251</cite>
<rule>p → h</rule>
<rule>dz dʒ → {tsːʼ,z} {tsːʼ,dʒ}</rule>
<rule>{ts,st} → tʃ</rule>
<rule>tɬː dɮ → tɬ(ː) tɬː</rule>
<rule>kː ɢ → xʲ {qːʼ,ɣ}</rule>
<rule>{l,r} → ∅ (sometimes, only from original *l)</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>11.1.2 Proto-Avar-Andic to Andi</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from User:Petusek (2010), “User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast Caucasian”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast_Caucasian&amp;oldid=351133322&gt;, apparently citing Nichols, Johanna (2003), “The Nakh-Daghestanian Consonant Correspondences”, in Tuite, Kevin, and Dee Ann Holisky, Current Trends in Caucasian, East European, and Inner Asian Linguistics: Papers in Honor of Howard I. Aronson 207 – 251</cite>
<rule>{ts,st} dz → s {tsːʼ,z}</rule>
<rule>stː → sː</rule>
<rule>dʒ → {tʃːʼ,dʒ}</rule>
<rule>tɬ tɬ(ː) tɬ(ː)(ʼ) dʒ → ɬ tɬ(ː) tɬ(ː)(ʼ) tɬː</rule>
<rule>q ɢ → x {qːʼ,ɣ}</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>11.1.3 Proto-Avar-Andic to Avar</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from User:Petusek (2010), “User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast Caucasian”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast_Caucasian&amp;oldid=351133322&gt;, apparently citing Nichols, Johanna (2003), “The Nakh-Daghestanian Consonant Correspondences”, in Tuite, Kevin, and Dee Ann Holisky, Current Trends in Caucasian, East European, and Inner Asian Linguistics: Papers in Honor of Howard I. Aronson 207 – 251</cite>
<rule>ts tsː tsʼ tsːʼ dz → sh ts tʃʼ ts(ː)ʼ tsʼ</rule>
<rule>st(ː) → ts</rule>
<rule>tʃ tʃː tʃːʼ dʒ → ts(?) tʃ tʃ(ː)ʼ tsʼ</rule>
<rule>tɬ tɬʼ tɬː tɬːʼ → ɬ tʼ tɬ tɬ(ː)ʼ</rule>
<rule>q qːʼ ɢ → x {q(ː)ʼ,ɡh} qʼ (not sure if that last 〈gh〉 should be ɣ)</rule>
<rule>s sː ɬː x xː → {s,ʃ} x ɬ {x,h} x</rule>
<rule>m → {m,n}</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>11.2 Proto-Northeast Caucasian to Dargi</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from User:Petusek (2010), “User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast Caucasian”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast_Caucasian&amp;oldid=351133322&gt;, apparently citing Nichols, Johanna (2003), “The Nakh-Daghestanian Consonant Correspondences”, in Tuite, Kevin, and Dee Ann Holisky, Current Trends in Caucasian, East European, and Inner Asian Linguistics: Papers in Honor of Howard I. Aronson 207 – 251</cite>
; *b is “[p]rone to change to *m”
<rule>{tsː,st} tsːʼ dz stː → ts z tsː s</rule>
<rule>tʃ tʃː dʒ → {tsʼ,tʃʼ} ʒ ts</rule>
<rule>tɬ tɬː tɬʼ tɬːʼ → k {xʲː,kː} kʰ {ɡ,q}(?)</rule>
<rule>qːʼ → ʕ</rule>
<rule>ʃ ʃː ɬ ɬː → {s,ʃ} ʃ xʲ {xʲ,ʃ}</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>11.3 Proto-Northeast Caucasian to Khinalug</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from User:Petusek (2010), “User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast Caucasian”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast_Caucasian&amp;oldid=351133322&gt;, apparently citing Nichols, Johanna (2003), “The Nakh-Daghestanian Consonant Correspondences”, in Tuite, Kevin, and Dee Ann Holisky, Current Trends in Caucasian, East European, and Inner Asian Linguistics: Papers in Honor of Howard I. Aronson 207 – 251</cite>
; The development of *tsʼ is unclear; in the user page there’s a slash but it might be a typo for an apostrophe
<rule>b d → {b,v} z</rule>
<rule>{tsː,st} {tsːʼ,dz} → ts tsʼ</rule>
<rule>{tʃ(ː),dʒ} → tʃʼ</rule>
<rule>tɬ tɬː tɬʼ tɬːʼ dɮ → k {k,xʲ} {kʼ,ɡ} {kʼ,kː} kʼ</rule>
<rule>kː kʼ ɡ → k {kʼ,ɡ} {kʼ,kː}</rule>
<rule>{qːʼ,ɢ} → qʼ</rule>
<rule>sː ɬ(ː) xː → h xʲ x</rule>
<rule>r → n / _C</rule>
<rule>r → {r,∅}</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>11.4 Proto-Northeast Caucasian to Lak</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from User:Petusek (2010), “User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast Caucasian”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast_Caucasian&amp;oldid=351133322&gt;, apparently citing Nichols, Johanna (2003), “The Nakh-Daghestanian Consonant Correspondences”, in Tuite, Kevin, and Dee Ann Holisky, Current Trends in Caucasian, East European, and Inner Asian Linguistics: Papers in Honor of Howard I. Aronson 207 – 251</cite>
<rule>b d → {pː,b} {tː,d} (but *b is “[p]rone to change to *m”)</rule>
<rule>{tsː,stː} dz st → sː {tsː,z} ts</rule>
<rule>tʃ tʃː dʒ → {ts,tʃ} {tsʼ,tʃʼ} tʃ(ː)ʼ</rule>
<rule>tɬ tɬː tɬ(ː)ʼ dɮ → xʲ xː kʼ {kː,l}</rule>
<rule>ɡ → kː</rule>
<rule>qʼ qʼː ɡ → {qʼ,j} qʼ {qː,ɣ}</rule>
<rule>ʃ ɬ ɬː → s {xʲ,ʃ} xː</rule>
<rule>m l → {m,n} {l,∅}</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>11.5 Proto-Northeast Caucasian to Proto-Lezgic</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from User:Petusek (2010), “User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast Caucasian”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast_Caucasian&amp;oldid=351133322&gt;, apparently citing Nichols, Johanna (2003), “The Nakh-Daghestanian Consonant Correspondences”, in Tuite, Kevin, and Dee Ann Holisky, Current Trends in Caucasian, East European, and Inner Asian Linguistics: Papers in Honor of Howard I. Aronson 207 – 251</cite>
; NB: These changes here probably aren’t “proper” sound changes, whatever that’s supposed to mean, but there doesn’t seem to be any particular sound change or set of sound changes that defines this family, so I’ve elected to go with something that seems to nearly work and note the exceptions.
<rule>q ɢ → {x,q} ɣ</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>11.5.1 Proto-Lezgic to Agul</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from User:Petusek (2010), “User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast Caucasian”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast_Caucasian&amp;oldid=351133322&gt;, apparently citing Nichols, Johanna (2003), “The Nakh-Daghestanian Consonant Correspondences”, in Tuite, Kevin, and Dee Ann Holisky, Current Trends in Caucasian, East European, and Inner Asian Linguistics: Papers in Honor of Howard I. Aronson 207 – 251</cite>
<rule>b d → {b,w} {d,z}</rule>
<rule>{ts,st} tsː tsːʼ stː dz → {s,ts} {ts,tʃ} tː s z</rule>
<rule>tʃːʼ → tʃː</rule>
<rule>tɬ tɬː tɬʼ tɬːʼ dɮ → xʲ x kʼ kː {,j,xʲ}</rule>
<rule>qːʼ → qː</rule>
<rule>ɬ(ː) xː → xʲ x</rule>
<rule>m → {m,b}</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>11.5.2 Proto-Lezgic to Archi</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from User:Petusek (2010), “User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast Caucasian”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast_Caucasian&amp;oldid=351133322&gt;, apparently citing Nichols, Johanna (2003), “The Nakh-Daghestanian Consonant Correspondences”, in Tuite, Kevin, and Dee Ann Holisky, Current Trends in Caucasian, East European, and Inner Asian Linguistics: Papers in Honor of Howard I. Aronson 207 – 251</cite>
<rule>d → r / _#</rule>
<rule>d → {d,tː}</rule>
<rule>{ts,st} tsː tsːʼ stː dz → s {s,ʃ} tsʼ sː ts</rule>
<rule>tʃ(ː) dʒ → ʃ tʃ</rule>
<rule>tɬ tɬː tɬʼ tɬːʼ dɮ → ɬ ɬ(ː) kʼ tɬʼ tɬː</rule>
<rule>kː ɡ → x {kː,ɡ}</rule>
<rule>ɣ → q (more likely, *ɢ → q instead of → ɣ)</rule>
<rule>q qːʼ → x q(ː)ʼ</rule>
<rule>sː xː → {sː,h} x</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>11.5.3 Proto-Lezgic to Lezghi</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from User:Petusek (2010), “User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast Caucasian”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast_Caucasian&amp;oldid=351133322&gt;, apparently citing Nichols, Johanna (2003), “The Nakh-Daghestanian Consonant Correspondences”, in Tuite, Kevin, and Dee Ann Holisky, Current Trends in Caucasian, East European, and Inner Asian Linguistics: Papers in Honor of Howard I. Aronson 207 – 251</cite>
<rule>b d → {pː,b} {tː,d}</rule>
<rule>{ts,st} tsː tsːʼ dz → {ʃ,tʃ} tʃ(ʼ) {tʃː,dʒ}</rule>
<rule>tɬ tɬː tɬʼ tɬːʼ dɮ → xʲ {ɣʲ,ɡ} qʼ k(ʼ) {kː,ɣʲ}</rule>
<rule>kː ɡ → ɣ kː</rule>
<rule>ɣ → {qː,ɣ} (again, probably a difference in the development of *ɢ than this strict sound change)</rule>
<rule>qːʼ → q(ʼ)</rule>
<rule>ʃː ɬ ɬː x xː → ɣʲ j ʃ ɣ x</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>11.5.4 Proto-Lezgic to Rutul</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from User:Petusek (2010), “User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast Caucasian”. <rule>qːʼ → q(ʼ)</rule>

<section>
<h2>11.5.6 Proto-Lezgic to Udi</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from User:Petusek (2010), “User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast Caucasian”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast_Caucasian&amp;oldid=351133322&gt;, apparently citing Nichols, Johanna (2003), “The Nakh-Daghestanian Consonant Correspondences”, in Tuite, Kevin, and Dee Ann Holisky, Current Trends in Caucasian, East European, and Inner Asian Linguistics: Papers in Honor of Howard I. <rule>tʃ tʃː tʃʼ tʃːʼ dʒ → {∅,ʃ} tʃ ∅ tʃʼ {dʒ,tʃ}</rule>

<rule>kː → q</rule>

<section>
<h2>11.6 Proto-Northeast Caucasian to Nakh</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from User:Petusek (2010), “User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast Caucasian”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast_Caucasian&amp;oldid=351133322&gt;, apparently citing Nichols, Johanna (2003), “The Nakh-Daghestanian Consonant Correspondences”, in Tuite, Kevin, and Dee Ann Holisky, Current Trends in Caucasian, East European, and Inner Asian Linguistics: Papers in Honor of Howard I. Aronson 207 – 251</cite>
<rule>st stː → st(ʼ) st</rule>
<rule>tsː tʃː → ts tʃ / #_</rule>
<rule>tsːʼ → tsː</rule>
<rule>{tsː,tʃː,dz,dʒ} → tː / _#</rule>
<rule>tɬ(ː) tɬʼ tɬːʼ → x qʼ ʕ</rule>
<rule>kː qːʼ → xk(?) qʼ</rule>
<rule>ʃː ɬː xː → ʃ ɬ x</rule>
<rule>l → r / _#</rule>
<rule>r → d / #_</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>11.7 Proto-Northeast Caucasian to Proto-Tsezic</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from User:Petusek (2010), “User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast Caucasian”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast_Caucasian&amp;oldid=351133322&gt;, apparently citing Nichols, Johanna (2003), “The Nakh-Daghestanian Consonant Correspondences”, in Tuite, Kevin, and Dee Ann Holisky, Current Trends in Caucasian, East European, and Inner Asian Linguistics: Papers in Honor of Howard I. Aronson 207 – 251</cite>
; *b is “[p]rone to change to *m”
<rule>{ts,st} {tsːʼ,dz} → s ts</rule>
<rule>tʃ {tʃː,dʒ} → ʃ tʃ (the change of *tʃːʼ is conjectured for Bezhta, as the change is only listed in Tsez, but given the development of *tsːʼ I don’t find it unreasonable to put it here)</rule>
<rule>tɬ tɬːʼ → ɬ tɬ</rule>
<rule>ɢ → q</rule>
<rule>s xː → z x</rule>
<rule>l → {l,r}</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>11.7.1 Proto-Tsezic to Bezhta</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from User:Petusek (2010), “User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast Caucasian”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast_Caucasian&amp;oldid=351133322&gt;, apparently citing Nichols, Johanna (2003), “The Nakh-Daghestanian Consonant Correspondences”, in Tuite, Kevin, and Dee Ann Holisky, Current Trends in Caucasian, East European, and Inner Asian Linguistics: Papers in Honor of Howard I. Aronson 207 – 251</cite>
; *ɬ may have remained ɬ
; *r remained when intervocalic
</section>
<section>
<h2>11.7.2 Proto-Tsezic to Tsez</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from User:Petusek (2010), “User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast Caucasian”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=User:Petusek/Drafts/Northeast_Caucasian&amp;oldid=351133322&gt;, apparently citing Nichols, Johanna (2003), “The Nakh-Daghestanian Consonant Correspondences”, in Tuite, Kevin, and Dee Ann Holisky, Current Trends in Caucasian, East European, and Inner Asian Linguistics: Papers in Honor of Howard I. Aronson 207 – 251</cite>
<rule>tsː tɬː → z l / V_V</rule>
<rule>tsː tɬː → s ɬ</rule>
<rule>kːʼ ɡ → k kʼ</rule>
<rule>q qːʼ → x(?) q</rule>
<rule>sː ʃ ʃː ɬ ɬː x → s ʒ ʃ l ɬ ɣ</rule>
<rule>r → {r,l,∅}</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>12 Northwest Caucasian</h2>
</section>
<section>
<h2>12.1 Proto-Northwest Caucasian to Proto-Abazgi</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Proto-Northwest Caucasian language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Proto-Northwest_Caucasian_language&amp;oldid=596995618&gt;, presumably citing Starostin, Sergei A. and Sergei L. Nikolayev (1994), A North Caucasian Etymological Dictionary</cite>
<rule>{pʷ,pʲ} {p(ʲ)ː,bˤ} bʷ pʷʲ bʷʲ → p b f tsʲ dzʲ</rule>
<rule>mˤ → m</rule>
<rule>rʲ → r</rule>
<rule>tʷː tʷʲ(ʼ) → d(w) tʷ(ʼ)</rule>
<rule>tsʲː → dzʲ (marked as dubious in the source)</rule>
<rule>sʷʲ zʲ → sʲ z</rule>
<rule>tsʷː tsʷʲ → tsʷ (tʃ)</rule>
<rule>zʷʲ tsʷʲʼ dzʷʲ → dzʲ~zʲ tʃʼ dʒ~ʒ</rule>
<rule>tʃ(ʼ) dʒ → tsʲ(ʼ) dzʲ</rule>
<rule>ʃ(~ʃː) ʒ → sʲ sʲ</rule>
<rule>tɕː → dzʲ(~tɕ)</rule>
<rule>ɕ(ː) ʑ → ʃ ʒ (the change of singleton *ɕ to ʃ is marked as dubious)</rule>
<rule>tʃʷ(ʼ) tʃʷː dʒʷ → tʃ(ʼ) zʷ dzʷ~zʷ</rule>
<rule>ʃʷ ʃʷː ʒʷ → sʷ ʃ zʲ (this final change is marked as dubious)</rule>
<rule>tɕʷ(ʼ) tɕʷː → tʃ(ʼ) dʒ~ʒ</rule>
<rule>ɕʷ ɕʷː ʑʷ → ʃʷ ʃ ʒʷ</rule>
<rule>tɬ tɬː tɬʼ dɮ → x ts {x,tsʼ} l</rule>
<rule>ɬː → x</rule>
<rule>tɬʲ tɬʲʼ dɮʲ → xʲ ɕ ɣʲ</rule>
<rule>ɬʲ(~ɬʲʷː) ɮʲ → ɕ ʑ</rule>
<rule>lʲ → r / #_</rule>
<rule>lʲ → l~ɣʲ</rule>
<rule>tɬʷ(ː) tɬʼ dɮʷ → tsʷ tsʼ(ʷ) l</rule>
<rule>ɬʷ(ː) → ʃ</rule>
<rule>tɬʷʲ tɬʷʲː tɬʷʲʼ dɮʷʲ → tʃʷ ʒʷ tʃʷʼ dʒʷ</rule>
<rule>ɬʷʲ(~ɬʷː) → ʃ</rule>
<rule>ɮʷʲ → ʒ</rule>
<rule>kʷː → ɡʷ</rule>
<rule>kʷʲʼ → kʷʼ</rule>
<rule>xʷʲ ɣʷʲ → sʷ zʷ (*ɣʷʲ is marked as dubious)</rule>
<rule>q qː ɢ → (ħ) q ɣ (*ɢ is marked as *G in the document)</rule>
<rule>χ(ʲ) ʁ(ʲ) → ħ ʕ</rule>
<rule>qʲː ɢʲ → q ɣʲ</rule>
<rule>qʷ qʷː ɢʷ → ħʷ qʷ ɣʷ</rule>
<rule>χʷ ʁʷ → ħʷ ʕʷ</rule>
<rule>qʷʲ ɢʷʰ → ħ(ʷ) ɣ(ʷ)</rule>
<rule>qʷʲʼ → ʕʷ (qʷʼ?)</rule>
<rule>χʷʲ ʁʷʲ → ħ(ʷ) ʕ(ʷ)</rule>
<rule>qˤː qˤʼ → ʕ ħ (this latter is marked as dubious)</rule>
<rule>ʁʕ → ʕ</rule>
<rule>qˤʲ qˤʲː qˤʲʼ → q ʔ ħ(?)</rule>
<rule>χˤʲ ʁˤʲ → ħ ʕ</rule>
<rule>qˤʷ qˤʷː qˤʷʼ χˤʷ ʁˤʷ → ħʷ ʕʷ qʷ(ʼ) ħʷ (ħʷ?)</rule>
<rule>qˤʷʲ qˤʷʲ qˤʷʲʼ ʁˤʷʲ → qʷ ʕʷ ħʷ ʕʷ</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>12.1.1 Proto-Abazgi to Ashkharywa Abaza</h2>
<cite>Nortaneous, from Chirikba, Viacheslav A. (2003), “Abkhaz”. Languages of the World/Materials 119.</cite>
<rule>tʃʷ(ʼ) tɕ(ʼ) dʒʷ dʑ → f(ʼ) ts(ʼ) v dz</rule>
<rule>ɕ ʑ → s z</rule>
<rule>”Vʕ ʕ”V → ”aa a”a (but stays /ʕ/ sometimes?)</rule>
<rule>ʕʷ → ɥ</rule>
<rule>tʷ(ʼ) dʷ → {tʷ(ʼ),p(ʼ)} {dʷ,b}</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>12.1.2 Proto-Abazgi to Tapanta Abaza</h2>
<cite>Nortaneous, from Chirikba, Viacheslav A. (2003), “Abkhaz”. Languages of the World/Materials 119.</cite>
<rule>{tʃʷ(ʼ),tɕ} {dʒʷ,dʑ} → ts(ʼ) dz</rule>
<rule>tɕʷ(ʼ) dʑʷ → {tɕ(ʷ)(ʼ),tʃʷ(ʼ)} {dʑ(ʷ),dʒʷ}</rule>
<rule>ɕ ʑ → s z</rule>
<rule>ʃʷ ʒʷ ɕʷ ʑʷ → {ɕ(ʷ),ʃ(ʷ)} {ʑ(ʷ),ʒ(ʷ)} {ɕ(ʷ),ʃʷ} {ʑ(ʷ),ʒʷ}</rule>
<rule>{tʷ(ʼ),dʷ} → {tɕ(ʷ)(ʼ),tʃ(ʷ)(ʼ)} {dʒ(ʷ),dʑ(ʷ)}</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>12.1.3 Proto-Abazgi to Ahchypsy Abkhaz</h2>
<cite>Nortaneous, from Chirikba, Viacheslav A. (2003), “Abkhaz”. Languages of the World/Materials 119.</cite>
<rule>tʃʷ tʃʷʼ dʒʷ tɕ(ʼ) dʑ → f pʼ ts(ʼ) v dz</rule>
<rule>ɕ ʑ → s z</rule>
<rule>”Vʕ ʕ”V → ”aa a”a</rule>
<rule>ʕ ʕʷ → aː ɥ</rule>
<rule>q qʷ → χˤ χˤʷ</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>12.1.4 Proto-Abazgi to Bzyp Abkhaz</h2>

<h2>12.1.5 Proto-Abazgi to Abzhywa Proper</h2>

<rule>tʃʷ(ʼ) tɕ(ʼ) tɕʷ(ʼ) dʒʷ dʑ dʑʷ → f(ʼ) ts(ʼ) tɕʷ(ʼ) v dz dʑ</rule>

<rule>”Vʕ ʕ”V → ”aa a”a</rule>

<rule>q qʷ → χ χʷ</rule>
</section>

<h2>12.1.6 Proto-Abazgi to Tsabal Abzhywa</h2>

<rule>tʃʷ tʃʷʼ tɕ(ʼ) tɕʷ(ʼ) dʒʷ dʑ dʑʷ → f pʼ ts(ʼ) tɕʷ(ʼ) v dz dʑ</rule>

<h2>12.1.7 Proto-Abazgi to Khaltsys Sadz</h2>

<rule>tʃʷ(ʼ) tɕ(ʼ) tɕʷ(ʼ) dʒʷ dʑ dʑʷ → f(ʼ) ts(ʼ) tɕʷ(ʼ) v dz dʑʷ</rule>

<h2>12.2 Proto-Northwest Caucasian to Proto-Circassian</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Proto-Northwest Caucasian language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Proto-Northwest_Caucasian_language&amp;oldid=596995618&gt;, presumably citing Starostin, Sergei A. and Sergei L. Nikolayev (1994), A North Caucasian Etymological Dictionary</cite>
<rule>f → xʷ</rule>
<rule>pʲ(ː) pʲʼ bʲ → t(ː) tʼ d</rule>
<rule>pʷ pʷʲ pʷʲʼ bʷ bʷʲ → p tʷ tʷʼ b d</rule>
<rule>pˤ(ː) pˤ bˤ → p(ː) pʼ b</rule>
<rule>mˤ → m</rule>
<rule>r l → tː tħ / #_</rule>
<rule>l → ɮ</rule>
<rule>tʷ(ː) tʷʼ dʷ → t(ː) tʼ d</rule>
<rule>tʷʲ tʷʲʼ → ts tsʼ</rule>
<rule>ts tsː dz → {s,c} tsː dz~z</rule>
<rule>tsʲ tsʲː tsʲʼ dzʲ → (s) tsː tsʼ dz~z</rule>
<rule>sʲ zʲ → s z</rule>
<rule>tsʷ lʲ {qʲʷ,qˤʷ} → sʷ d qʷ / #_ (data not given for non-initial forms)</rule>
<rule>tsʲʷ tsʲʷ dzʲʷ → tsʲ tsʲʼ dzʲ</rule>
<rule>sʲʷ zʲʷ → sʲ zʲ</rule>
<rule>tʃ tʃʼ dʒ dʑ → s tsʲʼ(?) dz~z dʑ~ʑ</rule>
<rule>ʃ(~ʃː) → s</rule>
<rule>ɕ(ː) ʑ → ʃ(ː) ʒ</rule>
<rule>tʃʷ(ː) tʃʷʼ dʒʷ → tɕ(ː) tɕʼ dʑ~ʑ</rule>
<rule>ʃʷ(ː) ʒʷ → ʃ(ː) ʒ</rule>
<rule>tcʷ(ː) tɕʷʼ → tʃ(ː) tʃʼ</rule>
<rule>ɕʷ ɕʷ ʑʷ → sʲ ʃː zʲ</rule>
<rule>ɬ(ː) tɬ(ː) tɬʼ dɮ → ɕ(ː) tɕ(ː) tɕʼ tħ</rule>
<rule>tɬʲ tɬʲʼ dɮʲ → tɕ tɬʼ ɣ</rule>
<rule>tɬʷ(ː) tɬʷʼ dɮʷ → tʃ(ː) tʃʼ ħ</rule>
<rule>ɬʷ ɬʷː → x(ʷ) ɕː</rule>
<rule>tɬʲʷ tɬʲʷ tɬʲʷ dɮʲʷ → x tɕː tɕʼ ɮ</rule>
<rule>ɬʲʷ(~ɬʷː) ɮʲʷ → x(ʷ) ɣʲ</rule>
<rule>k kʼ ɡ → kʲ kʲʼ ɡʲ</rule>
<rule>xʲ ɣʲ → ɕ ʑ</rule>
<rule>xʷ → x(ʷ)</rule>
<rule>ɡʲʷ xʲʷ ɣʲʷ(?) → ɡʷ xʷ ʁʷ</rule>
<rule>ɢ → ʁ</rule>
<rule>qʲʼ ʁʲ → ʔ ʁ</rule>
<rule>qʷ → qʷː / !_</rule>
<rule>qʷʼ ɢʷ → qʷː ʁʷ</rule>
<rule>qʲʷː qʲʷ ɢʲʷ,ʁʲʷ} χʲʷ → qʷː ʔʷ ʁʷ χʷ</rule>
<rule>{qˤː,qˤʼ} χˤ ʁˤ → qː χ ʁ</rule>
<rule>{qˤʲ,χˤʲ} qˤʲ ʁˤʲ {qˤʲː qˤʲʼ} → ħ ʔ j</rule>
<rule>{qˤʷː,qˤʷʼ} χˤʷ ʁˤʷ → qʷː χʷ ʁʷ</rule>
<rule>qˤʲʷ {qˤʲʷː,qˤʲʷ} ʁˤʲʷ → ħ ʔʷ w~ʁʷ</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>12.2.1 Proto-Circassian to Adyghe</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Proto-Circassian language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia, &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Proto-Circassian_language&amp;oldid=591849172&gt;; and Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Adyghe language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Adyghe_language&amp;oldid=593857358&gt;</cite>
; — Consonant correspondences:
<rule>tsʲ → tɕ</rule>
<rule>tsʷ → tsʲʷ</rule>
<rule>tʃ tɕ → ʃ ʂ</rule>
<rule>ʔ(ʷ)~qʼ(ʷ) → ʔ(ʷ)</rule>
<rule>dɮ → ɣ</rule>
<rule>dzʲ dzʷ → dʑ ʑʷ</rule>
<rule>tsʲʼ → ɕʼ~ʃʼ</rule>
<rule>sʷ ɕ → ɕʷ~ʃʷ ɕ~ʃ</rule>
<rule>xʷ χʲ → f~ɸ? ħ</rule>
<rule>ɮ → l</rule>
<rule>zʷ → ʑʷ~ʒʷ</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>12.2.1.1 Adyghe to Abadzekh Adyghe</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Proto-Circassian language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia, &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Proto-Circassian_language&amp;oldid=591849172&gt;; and Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Adyghe language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Adyghe_language&amp;oldid=593857358&gt;</cite>
<rule>tsʲ(ː) → tɕ</rule>
<rule>tsʷ → tʃʷ</rule>
<rule>pː tː tsː tsʷ ʃː tʃː tɕː kʲː kʷː qː~qχ qʷː~qχʷ → p t ts tʃʷ ʃ ɕ tɕ tʃ kʷ qː qʷ</rule>
<rule>tʃʼ tɬʼ → ʔaj~ʔ ɬʼ</rule>
<rule>tʃʷʼ~ʃʷʼ → ɕʷʼ~ʃʷʼ</rule>
<rule>kʲʼ → tʃʼ</rule>
<rule>ʂː ʃː → ʂ ʃ</rule>
<rule>sʼ ʃʼ~ɕʼ → tsʼ ʃʼ</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>12.2.1.2 Adyghe to Bzhedug Adyghe</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Proto-Circassian language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia, &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Proto-Circassian_language&amp;oldid=591849172&gt;; and Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Adyghe language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Adyghe_language&amp;oldid=593857358&gt;</cite>
<rule>tsʲ tsʲː → tɕ tɕː</rule>
<rule>kʲ(ː) kʲʼ ɡʲ → tʃ(ː) tʃʼ dʑ</rule>
<rule>tsʷː → tsʲʷː</rule>
<rule>qː~χ qʷː~qχʷ → qː qʷː</rule>
<rule>tsʷʼ~ʃʷʼ → ɕʷʼ~ʃʷʼ</rule>
<rule>tɬʼ → ɬʼ</rule>
<rule>sʼ ʃʼ~ɕʼ → tsʼ ʃʼ</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>12.2.1.3 Adyghe to Shapsug Adyghe</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Proto-Circassian language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia, &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Proto-Circassian_language&amp;oldid=591849172&gt;; and Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Adyghe language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Adyghe_language&amp;oldid=593857358&gt;</cite>
<rule>tsʲ(ː) tsʷ tʃ tɕ → tɕ tʃʷ ʃ ʂ</rule>
<rule>pː tː tsː tsʷː ʃː tsː tcː kʲː kʷː qː~qχ qʷː~qχʷ → p t ts tʃʷ ʃ tʃ tɕ kʲ kʷ χ~q χʷ~qʷ</rule>
<rule>p(ʷ)ʼ t(ʷ)ʼ tsʼ tsʷʼ~ʃʷʼ → pˤ tˤ tsˤ ʂʷ</rule>
<rule>tɬʼ → ɬˤ</rule>
<rule>ʂː ʃː → ʂ ʃ</rule>
<rule>sʼ ʃʼ~ɕʼ → sˤ ʂ</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>12.2.1.4 Adyghe to Temirgoy Adyghe</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Proto-Circassian language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia, &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Proto-Circassian_language&amp;oldid=591849172&gt;; and Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Adyghe language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Adyghe_language&amp;oldid=593857358&gt;</cite>
<rule>tsʲ(ː) tsʷ → tɕ tsʲʷ</rule>
<rule>kʲ(ː) kʲʼ kʷː ɡʲ → tʃ tʃʼ kʷ dʑ</rule>
<rule>q qʷ → qː qʷː / ! #_</rule>
<rule>pː tː tsː tsʷː ʃː tʃː tɕː → p t ts tsʷʲ ʃ tʃ tɕ</rule>
<rule>qː~qχ qʷː~qχʷ → qː qʷː</rule>
<rule>tsʲʼ tsʷʼ~ʃʷʼ → ɕʼ~ʃʼ ɕʷʼ~ʃʷʼ</rule>
<rule>tɬʼ → ɬʼ</rule>
<rule>ʂː ʃː → ʂ ʃ</rule>
<rule>ɣ → ɣ~ɡ</rule>
<rule>sʼ ʃʼ~ɕʼ → tsʼ ʃʼ</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>12.2.2 Proto-Circassian to Kabardian</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Proto-Circassian language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia, &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Proto-Circassian_language&amp;oldid=591849172&gt;</cite>
; — Consonant correspondences:
<rule>tsʲ(ː) tsʷ {tʃ,tɕ} → ɕ f ʃ</rule>
<rule>kʲ → tʃ</rule>
<rule>ʔ~qʼ → ʔʷ</rule>
<rule>pː tː tsː tsʷː ʃː {tʃː,tɕː} kʷː qː~qχ qʷː~qχʷ → b d dz v ɕ ʒ dʒ ɡʷ qʼ~qχ qʷʼ~qχʷ</rule>
<rule>dɮ dzʲ dzʷ dʑ ɡʲ → ʒ ʑ {v,w} ʒ dʒ</rule>
<rule>tsʲʼ tʃʷʼ~ʃʷʼ tʃʼ tɕʼ tɬʼ kʲʼ → ɕʼ fʼ ɕʼ {ɕʼ,cçʼ} ɬʼ tʃʼ</rule>
<rule>sʷ zʷ {ʃ,ʂ} {ʑ,ʐ,ɣʲ} ʑ χʲ → f v ɕ ʑ ʑ~ʒ χ</rule>
<rule>ʂː ʃː → ɕ ʒ</rule>
<rule>sʼ(?) ʃʼ~ɕʼ → tsʼ ɕʼ</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>12.3 Proto-Northwest Caucasian to Ubykh</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Proto-Northwest Caucasian language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Proto-Northwest_Caucasian_language&amp;oldid=596995618&gt;, presumably citing Starostin, Sergei A. and Sergei L. Nikolayev (1994), A North Caucasian Etymological Dictionary</cite>
<rule>pʲ(ː) bʲ → t(ː) d</rule>
<rule>pʲʼ → tʷʼ</rule>
<rule>{pʷ,bʷ} → f</rule>
<rule>pʲː → tʷ~dʷ</rule>
<rule>pʷʲ bʷʲ → tʷ dʷ</rule>
<rule>pʷʲʼ pˤ pˤː pˤʼ bˤ → tʷʼ vˤ bˤ pˤʼ bˤ</rule>
<rule>t(ʷ)ː tʷʲʼ → t(ʷ) tʷʼ</rule>
<rule>{r,l} lʲ → d r / #_</rule>
<rule>l lʲ → ∅~j l~ɣʲ</rule>
<rule>r rʲ → r~ʁ ɮ</rule>
<rule>ts(ʲ)ː tsʷː dzʲ → ts tsʷ dz</rule>
<rule>z(ʲ) zʷ → dz(ʲ)~z(ʲ) dzʷ~zʷ</rule>
<rule>sʷʲ zʷʲ tsʷʲʼ dzʷʲ → tʃʷ ʒʷ tʃʲʼ dʒʲ</rule>
<rule>tʃ(ʼ) dʒ → ts(ʼ) dz</rule>
<rule>ʃ(~ʃː) ʒ → s z</rule>
<rule>ɕː tɕː → ɕ tɕ</rule>
<rule>ʃʷ(ː) ʒʷ tʃʷ(ʼ) dʒ → ʃ ʒ tʃ(ʼ) dʒ</rule>
<rule>tɕʷ(ː) tɕʷʼ → tɕ tɕʼ</rule>
<rule>ɕʷ ɕʷː ʑʷ → ʃʷ sʷ ʒʷ</rule>
<rule>tɬ tɬː tɬʼ dɮ → ɕ (sʲ) tsʲʼ ɮ</rule>
<rule>ɬ(ː) → sʲ</rule>
<rule> tɬʲ tɬʲʼ dɮʲ → ɕ tɬʼ ɕ ʁ(~zʲ)</rule>
<rule>ɬʲ(~ɬʲʼː) → ɬ</rule>
<rule>ɮ → ʑ</rule>
<rule>tɬʼ → tsʲʼ</rule>
<rule>{ɮʲ,lʲ} → ɮ</rule>
<rule>tɬʷ(ː) tɬʷʼ dɮʷ → tsʷ ts(ʷ)ʼ w</rule>
<rule>ɬʷ ɬʷː → sʷ s(ʷ)</rule>
<rule>tɬʷʲ tɬʷʲː tɬʷʲʼ dɮʷʲ → f dʑ tsʼ dʒ</rule>
<rule>ɬʷʲ(~ɬʷː) ɮʷʲ → ʃʷ ʒʷ</rule>
<rule>k kʼ ɡ x ɣ → kʲ kʲʼ ɡʲ ɕ ɣ~ʁ</rule>
<rule>xʲ ɣʲ → sʲ zʲ</rule>
<rule>kʷː xʷ → ɡʷ x</rule>
<rule>kʲʷː xʲʷ ɣʲʷ(?) → ɡʲ kʲʼ xʲ ʁʲ</rule>
<rule>ɣ → ʁ</rule>
<rule>qʲː(ʼ) ɢʲ χʲ → qʲ(ʼ) ʁʲ xʲ</rule>
<rule>qʷː ɢʷ → qʷ ʁʷ</rule>
<rule>qʲʷ qʲʷː qʲʷʼ ɢʲʷ χʲʷ ʁʲʷ → xʲ qʲ qʲʼ ʁʲ χʲ ʁʲ</rule>
<rule>qˤʲ → q(ˤ)</rule>
<rule>qˤʲʷ {qˤʲʷː,qˤʲʷ} ʁˤʲʷ → χʷ qʷʼ w</rule>
</section>

<rule>ŕ → r / V_V</rule>

<rule>l → ɭ / V_#</rule>

<h2>14.3.1 Standard Tamil to Colloquial Tamil</h2>
<cite>schwatever, from Shiffman, Harold F. A Reference Grammar on Spoken Tamil</cite>
; “(exception: never finally in monosyllables, never initially in multisyllabic words)”
<rule>aj → eː</rule>
<rule>avu aji → aw aj</rule>
<rule>i u → e o / _Ca</rule>
<rule>{k,v} → ∅ / V_V</rule>
<rule>aː eː iː oː uː → a ɛ i o u / _#</rule>
<rule>am an {aːm,aːn} → õ æ̃ ã / _#</rule>
<rule>{om,on} {em,en} {oːm,oːn} {eːm,eːn} → ɔ̃ ɛ̃ õ ẽ</rule>
<rule>um → ũ / _#</rule>
<rule>∅ → ɨ / _N#</rule>
<rule>{ɭ,ɽ} → ∅ (sporadic, the latter very much so and contributing some compensatory lengthening)</rule>
<rule>l ɭ → lːʉ ɭːʉ / _#(C)V[-long]</rule>
<rule>∅ → ʉ / {l,ɭ}_# if {M,Vː} previously in the lexeme</rule>
; “in most dialects”
<rule>r → ɾ</rule>
<rule>ɽ → ɭ</rule>
<rule>{ɾ,l,ɭ} → ∅ / V_S</rule>
<rule>i u → ɨ ʉ / short only when unstressed ! in #U</rule>
<rule>ɨ ʉ → ∅ / ! _#</rule>
<rule>∅ → {ɨ,ʉ} / to break up clusters</rule>
<rule>n → ŋ / _{k,ɡ}</rule>
<rule>i(ː) e(ː) → u(ː) o(ː) / {m,v,p}_C̣</rule>
<rule>j → ∅ / V[-front]_#</rule>
<rule>j → jːi / E_#</rule>
<rule>tː nt → cː ɲc / {i,j}_</rule>
<rule>ʈk → kː</rule>
<rule>n tː → n̪ t̪ː  </rule>
; “(sporadic and dialect development)”
<rule>ɳ → n</rule>
; “(again, sporadic)”
<rule>ɭ → l</rule>
<rule>c → s / _{a,o,u,e}</rule>
; “(most dialects)”
<rule>cː → tʃː</rule>
; “There’s also only a few changes necessary to turn this into the British dialect (which didn’t merge retroflexes with alveolars):”
; “(highly sporadic)”
<rule>o e → u i / _C{u,i}</rule>
<rule>— i(ː) e(ː) → u(ː) o(ː) / _ɭ</rule>
<rule>— eCə oCə → Ceː Coː / #_</rule>
</section>

<h2>15 Eskimo-Aleut</h2>
</section>
<section>
<h2>15.1 Proto-Eskimo-Aleut to Proto-Aleut</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Marsh, Gordon and Morris Swadesh (1951), “Kleinschmidt Centennial V: Eskimo Aleut Correspondences”, International Journal of American Linguistics, Vol. 17, No. 4 (Oct., 1951), pp. 209 – 216</cite>
<rule>a → i / i_ </rule>
<rule>u → a / a_ </rule>
<rule>p → h / #_ </rule>
<rule>v → m / medial </rule>
<rule>v → w / a_a (in eastern dialects) </rule>
<rule>{t,ð} → n / _# </rule>
<rule>ð → t / else </rule>
<rule>∅ → t / #_s </rule>
<rule>z → s / #_ </rule>
<rule>z → ð / medial </rule>
<rule>l̥ → l </rule>
<rule>m → w / #_ </rule>
<rule>n → t / #_ (except, maybe, “in exclamations”) </rule>
<rule>dʒ → ð / i_ (in eastern and central dialects) </rule>
<rule>dʒ → ð / u_a (in eastern dialects) </rule>
<rule>i →∅ / #_{z,dʒ} </rule>
; Deletion of medial vowels as per stress rules, “mostly affecting vowels before the accented syllable” 
<rule>ə → ∅ / #_, “under certain conditions not yet discovered” </rule>
<rule>nV₁nV₂ → nV₂nV₂</rule>
</section>

<rule>ð z → t s </rule>

<section>
<h2>15.2.1 Proto-Eskimo to Barrow Iñupiaq</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Swadesh, Morris (1952), “Unaaliq and Proto Eskimo IV: Diachronic Notes”, International Journal of American Linguistics, Vol. 18, No. 3 (Jul., 1952), pp. 166–171</cite>
<rule>l̥ → l / medial </rule>
<rule>t → s / i_ </rule>
<rule>ə → i / at word boundaries </rule>
<rule>ə → u / u_ </rule>
<rule>ə → a / a_ </rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / else </rule>
<rule>ɣ ʁ → k q / _# </rule>
; Regressive MOA assimilation and progressive voicing assimilation in consonant clusters (at least, when C₂ is either /l/ or /l̥/) 
<rule>C₀VC₀ → C₀ː </rule>
<rule>m n ŋ → v t ɣ / _C[-nasal] </rule>

<rule>v → p / _s </rule>
<rule>v ʁ → p q / S_ </rule>

<rule>{p,v} t {k,ɣ} → m n ŋ / _N </rule>
<rule>v → ∅ / u_i </rule>
<rule>dʒ → ʁ / i_u </rule>
<rule>ədʒ → i / _{a,u} (except in #U) </rule>
<rule>adʒ → i / _a (except in #U?)</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>15.2.2 Proto-Eskimo to Greenlandic Iñupiaq</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Swadesh, Morris (1952), “Unaaliq and Proto Eskimo IV: Diachronic Notes”, International Journal of American Linguistics, Vol. 18, No. 3 (Jul., 1952), pp. 166–171</cite>
<rule>ə → u / u_ </rule>
<rule>ə → a / a_ </rule>

<rule>t → s / i_  </rule>
<rule>ə → i / else </rule>
<rule>dʒ → tʃ → s / “in certain positions” (except for Thule Greenlandic, where dʒ → tʃ and stayed there, apparently) </rule>
<rule>m n ŋ t {{ɣ,ʁ} → {k,q}} → p t k n ŋ / _# </rule>

<rule>l̥ → ʁ / _C </rule>
<rule>v ʁ → p q / S_ </rule>
<rule>v ɣ ʁ → p k q / _C (except where C = /l dʒ m n ŋ/) </rule>
<rule>{p,v} t {k,ɣ} → m n ŋ / _N </rule>
<rule>S₁S₂ → F₁F₂</rule>
<rule>Some metathesis in consonant clusters, the conditions of which are not elaborated upon; the given example cited within the text is lʁ → ʁl </rule>
<rule>v → ∅ / u_a </rule>
<rule>iv → uj / _u </rule>
<rule>dʒ → tʃ / i_{u,i} </rule>
<rule>ədʒ → i / _{a,u} (except in #U) </rule>
<rule>adʒ → i / _a (except in #U?)</rule>

<h2>15.2.5 Proto-Eskimo to Kuskokwim Yup’ik</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Swadesh, Morris (1952), “Unaaliq and Proto Eskimo IV: Diachronic Notes”, International Journal of American Linguistics, Vol. 18, No. 3 (Jul., 1952), pp. 166–171</cite>

<section>
<h2>15.2.7 Proto-Eskimo to Siberian Yup’ik</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Swadesh, Morris (1952), “Unaaliq and Proto Eskimo IV: Diachronic Notes”, International Journal of American Linguistics, Vol. 18, No. 3 (Jul., 1952), pp. 166–171</cite>
<rule>Cː → C </rule>
<rule>S → ∅ / #_F </rule>
<rule>s → ts → tʃ / in some dialects? </rule>
<rule>C[+voice] → C[-voice] / next to {S,s,l̥} </rule>
<rule>ɣ ʁ → k q / _# </rule>
<rule>ə → a / </rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / #_ </rule>
<rule>i → ∅ / #C[+dental]_C[+dental]V </rule>
<rule>a → ∅ / C[+velar]_C[+velar] </rule>
<rule>F[+voice] → F[-voice] / adjacent to {S,ts} </rule>
<rule>F[+voice] → S / l̥_ </rule>
<rule>∅→ n / #_iN (This one is sort of a guess, given a singular example in the text that isn’t really commented upon) </rule>
<rule>t → s / _{k,q} </rule>
<rule>u → a / a_ </rule>
<rule>ə → i / {u,a}_ </rule>
<rule>v → ∅ / u[+short]_V[+short] </rule>
<rule>v → ∅ / V[+short]_u[+short] </rule>
<rule>u → ∅ / #_vV </rule>
<rule>iv → j / #_u </rule>
<rule>s → dʒ / {i,u}_V </rule>
<rule>dʒ → ∅ / i_i </rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / _dʒ{a,u} except in #U </rule>
<rule>a → ∅ / _dʒa (except in #U?) </rule>
<rule>in → dʒ / _u (possibly only word-initially?)</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>15.2.8 Proto-Eskimo to Unaaliq Yup’ik</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Swadesh, Morris (1952), “Unaaliq and Proto Eskimo IV: Diachronic Notes”, International Journal of American Linguistics, Vol. 18, No. 3 (Jul., 1952), pp. 166–171</cite>
<rule>Cː → C </rule>
<rule>C → Cː / _V(…V), after #U </rule>
<rule>S → ∅ / #_F </rule>
<rule>s → ts / in certain situations? </rule>
<rule>C[+voice] → C[-voice] / next to {S,s,l̥} </rule>
<rule>ɣ ʁ → k q / _# </rule>
<rule>ə → a / _# </rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / #_ </rule>
<rule>i → ∅ / #C[+dental]_C[+dental]V </rule>
<rule>a → ∅ / C[+velar]_C[+velar] </rule>
<rule>v → ft / _s </rule>
<rule>F[+voice] → F[-voice] / adjacent to {S,ts} </rule>
<rule>F[+voice] → S / l̥_ </rule>
<rule>t → s / _{k,q} </rule>
<rule>i a u → ii aa uu / C_ in U[+open -initial -final] such that U[+open]_ </rule>
<rule>ə → i / {u,a}_ (though aə seems to have become i in some circumstances) </rule>
<rule>v → ∅ / u[+short]_V[+short] </rule>
<rule>v → ∅ / V[+short]_u[+short] </rule>
<rule>u → ∅ / #_vV </rule>
<rule>iv → j / #_u </rule>
<rule>dʒ → ∅ / i_i </rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / _dʒ{a,u} except in #U </rule>
<rule>a → ∅ / _dʒa (except in #U?) </rule>
<rule>s → dʒ / {i,u}_V </rule>
<rule>in → dʒ / _u (possibly only word-initially?)</rule>
</section>

<section>
<h2>17.1.1 Proto-Indo-European to Gheg Albanian</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2013), “Albanian language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Albanian_language&amp;oldid=582390175&gt;</cite>
<rule>d dʱ → ð / V_V</rule>
<rule>d dʱ → ð / r_</rule>
<rule>ḱ → s / _{u̯,u,i̯,i}</rule>
<rule>ḱ → k / _R</rule>
<rule>ḱ → {ts,tʃ} (“[a]rchaic relic”)</rule>
<rule>ḱ → θ</rule>
<rule>kʷ → s / _”E? </rule>
<rule>kʷ → c / _B?</rule>
<rule>kʷ → k / else?</rule>
<rule>ɡ́(ʱ) → d / %_C[+sibilant]</rule>
<rule>ɡʷ(ʱ) → {ɡ,z} </rule>
<rule>bʱ dʱ ɡ́(ʱ) → b d dh</rule>
<rule>s → ɟ / #_</rule>
<rule>s → ʃ / V{i̯,u̯,r,k}_V</rule>
<rule>sd → θ / medial</rule>
<rule>sḱ → h / medial</rule>
<rule>sp → f / medial</rule>
<rule>st → ʃt / medial</rule>
; “[d]issimilation with following vowel”
<rule>s → θ / sometimes, involving</rule>
<rule>s → h / V_V</rule>
<rule>i̯ → ɟ / {a,e,i}_</rule>
<rule>i̯ → j / _B</rule>
<rule>i̯ → ∅ / E_</rule>
<rule>i̯ → h / V_</rule>
<rule>u̯ → v</rule>
<rule>Vn → V[+nasal] / _C?</rule>
<rule>n → ɲ (sometimes?)</rule>
<rule>l r → l(ː) r(ː)</rule>
; Loss of laryngeals, with the possible exception of h₄, if it existed; h₃ and h₄ seem to have possibly fronted a following back vowel
<rule>{m̩,n̩} l̩ r̩ → e uj {ri,ir}</rule>
<rule>eː iː oː uː → o i e {y,i}</rule>
; Gheg seems to have maintained or innovated vowel length whereas Tosk has not
<rule>a e i o → {a,e} (j)e {e,i} a</rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / _#</rule>
<rule>c ɟ → tʃ dʒ (for most speakers)</rule>
</section>

<h2>17.2 Proto-Indo-European to Common Anatolian</h2>
<cite>Alces, from http://www.unc.edu/~melchert/anathistphon.pdf (link is dead)</cite>
; “tautosyllabic”
<rule>eh₂ → æː /</rule>
<rule>ei eu → ɛː uː</rule>

<rule>h₁ → ∅</rule>
; “medially”
<rule>h₃ → ∅ /</rule>
<rule>T h₂ → D h₃ / _#</rule>
<rule>T h₂ → D h₃ / V[-stress]_V[-stress]</rule>
<rule>T h₂ → D h₃ / ”Vː</rule>
<rule>T h₂ → D h₃ / ”W</rule>
; “(allophonic)”
<rule>t → z / j</rule>
<rule>r → ∅ / #_ (unclear)</rule>
<rule>j → ∅ / #_e (not widely attested)</rule>
<rule>{{h₁,h₃}s,s{h₁,h₃}} → sː (contested)</rule>
</section>

<cite>Alces, from http://www.unc.edu/~melchert/anathistphon.pdf (link is dead)</cite>

<h2>17.3 Armenian</h2>
