<section>
<h2>17.3.1 Proto-Indo-European to Artsakh Armenian</h2>
<cite>Mecislau &amp; Pogostick Man, the latter citing Wikipedia contributors (2013), “Armenian Language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Armenian_language&amp;oldid=582063933&gt;</cite>
; NB: The changes in plosives are the most contentious; the Wikipedia article gives differences between the seven dialects in initial position for only the alveolar series by way of comparison, so take plosive changes with a huge grain of salt.
<rule>{e,i}ː {u,o}ː → i u</rule>
<rule>{e,o}j ɛw → ɛj ow</rule>
<rule>{e,o} → a (rare)</rule>
<rule>aː → a</rule>
<rule>e → ɛ</rule>
<rule>ɛ o → i u / _N</rule>
<rule>ej ia → e ɛa</rule>
<rule>{i,u} → ə / in some unstressed syllables</rule>
<rule>e oj ɛa → i u ɛ / when unstressed</rule>
<rule>p t → h tʰ / #_ (?)</rule>
<rule>t → tʰ / {aw,ow}_</rule>
<rule>kʷ → tʃʰ / _{e,i}</rule>
<rule>t k(ʷ) → d ɡ / {N,L}_</rule>
<rule>p ḱ k(ʷ) → {w,v} s kʰ</rule>
<rule>ɡ́ → ts (?)</rule>
<rule>b d ɡ(ʷ) → p t k</rule>
<rule>bʱ dʱ ɡ́ʱ ɡʷʱ → p t j k / #_</rule>
<rule>bʱ dʱ ɡ́ʱ ɡʱ ɡʷʱ → {w,v} d z ɡ ʒ</rule>
<rule>j → w / _o</rule>
<rule>j → ?</rule>
<rule>{sk,ks} kj → tsʰ tʃʰ (?)</rule>
<rule>{sr,rs} → rː</rule>
<rule>r → rː / _N</rule>
<rule>l → ɫ / {C,lV}_</rule>
<rule>l → ɫ / V_V</rule>
<rule>Ns sN → s N</rule>
<rule>N → w / S_S</rule>
<rule>VN → V[+nasal] → V → (?) / _#, in polysyllables</rule>
<rule>N → n / _#, in monosyllables</rule>
<rule>N̩ → n / _#</rule>
<rule>m̩ n̩ r̩ l̩ → am an ar aɫ </rule>
<rule>V → (?) / _(C)#</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>17.3.2 Proto-Indo-European to Erevan Armenian</h2>
<cite>Mecislau &amp; Pogostick Man, the latter citing Wikipedia contributors (2013), “Armenian Language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Armenian_language&amp;oldid=582063933&gt;</cite>
; NB: The changes in plosives are the most contentious; the Wikipedia article gives differences between the seven dialects in initial position for only the alveolar series by way of comparison, so take plosive changes with a huge grain of salt.
<rule>{e,i}ː {u,o}ː → i u</rule>
<rule>{e,o}j ɛw → ɛj ow</rule>
<rule>{e,o} → a (rare)</rule>
<rule>aː → a</rule>
<rule>e → ɛ</rule>
<rule>ɛ o → i u / _N</rule>
<rule>ej ia → e ɛa</rule>
<rule>{i,u} → ə / in some unstressed syllables</rule>
<rule>e oj ɛa → i u ɛ / when unstressed</rule>
<rule>p t → h tʰ / #_ (?)</rule>
<rule>t → tʰ / {aw,ow}_</rule>
<rule>kʷ → tʃʰ / _{e,i}</rule>
<rule>t k(ʷ) → d ɡ / {N,L}_</rule>
<rule>p ḱ k(ʷ) → {w,v} s kʰ</rule>
<rule>ɡ́ → ts (?)</rule>
<rule>b d ɡ(ʷ) → p t k</rule>
<rule>ɡʷʱ → ɡʱ / #_</rule>
<rule>bʱ dʱ ɡ́ʱ ɡʱ ɡʷʱ → {w,v} d z ɡ ʒ</rule>
<rule>j → w / _o</rule>
<rule>j → ?</rule>
<rule>{sk,ks} kj → tsʰ tʃʰ (?)</rule>
<rule>{sr,rs} → rː</rule>
<rule>r → rː / _N</rule>
<rule>l → ɫ / {C,lV}_</rule>
<rule>l → ɫ / V_V</rule>
<rule>Ns sN → s N</rule>
<rule>N → w / S_S</rule>
<rule>VN → V[+nasal] → V → (?) / _#, in polysyllables</rule>

<rule>N̩ → n / _#</rule>

<h2>17.4.1 Proto-Indo-European to Avestan</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, Alex Fink, and Tropylium, the former two citing Wikipedia contributors (2013), “Proto-Indo-Iranian language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-Iranian_language&amp;oldid=543625693&gt;; and Alex Fink citing https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avestan_phonology</cite>
; NB: Tropylium wishes to note that his sound changes are subject to change.
<rule>bʱ dʱ ɡ́ʱ → b d z</rule>
<rule>ḱ ɡ́ → s z</rule>
<rule>k(ʷ) ɡ(ʷ)(ʱ) → tʃ dʒ / _E</rule>
<rule>k kʷ ɡ(ʷ)(ʱ) → x k ɡ / else</rule>
<rule>rt → š (Alex Fink says that the realization of /š/ “is unclear”)</rule>
<rule>s → {s,h}</rule>
<rule>u̯ → v</rule>
<rule>l → r</rule>
<rule>{n̩,m̩} → a</rule>
<rule>{l̩,r̩} → ər(ə(r))</rule>
<rule>e eː → a aː</rule>
<rule>o oː → {a,aː} aː</rule>
<rule>h(j) → ŋh / a_a</rule>
<rule>hw → ŋʷh / a_a</rule>
<rule>h → ŋ / a_ra</rule>
<rule>hₓ → ∅</rule>

<section>
<h2>17.5 Proto-Indo-European to Proto-Celtic</h2>
<cite>dhokarena56, from Matasović (2009), Etymological Dictionary of Proto-Celtic (ed. Lubotsky).</cite>
; “PIE Dialectal”
<rule>— h₁e h₂e h₃e → e a o</rule>
<rule>— eh₁ eh₂ eh₃ → eː aː oː</rule>
<rule>— H → a / C_C ! #_</rule>
<rule>— SS → sː</rule>
<rule>— ∅ → a / CR_HC</rule>
<rule>— H → ∅ / V_C when pretonic</rule>
<rule>— H → a / #R_C</rule>
; Early Proto-Celtic
<rule>— Ḱ → K</rule>
<rule>— ɡʷ → b</rule>
<rule>— h → ∅ / C_</rule>
<rule>— ∅ → i / C{l,r}S</rule>
; “[t]he e was often restored by analogy”
<rule>— e → a / _Ra (short a only), though</rule>
<rule>— ∅ → a / C_RC</rule>
; “if not in a syllabic position”
<rule>— H → ∅ /</rule>
<rule>— p…kʷ → kʷ…kʷ</rule>
<rule>— eː → iː</rule>
<rule>— oː → uː / in U#</rule>
<rule>— Vː → V[-long] / _RC</rule>
<rule>— C₁C₂ → xC₂ / if C₂ was a plosive or s</rule>
; Late Proto-Celtic
<rule>— p → b / _{r,l}</rule>
<rule>— p → w / B_N</rule>
<rule>— p → f </rule>
<rule>— oː ej → aː eː</rule>
<rule>— e → o / _w</rule>
<rule>— u → o / _wO</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>17.5.1 Proto-Indo-European to Old Irish</h2>
<cite>dhokarena56</cite>
; “Laryngeal rules (the ones common to all branches except Anatolian)”
<rule>Kʷ → K</rule>
<rule>“The PIE rules for the voicing of s → z, as in [nizdos] for *nisdos, are assumed to apply”</rule>
<rule>Cʱ → C</rule>
; Obstruent clusters assimilate in voicing to that of the final obstruent
<rule>eː → iː / ! _{i,u}</rule>
<rule>tː → sː</rule>
<rule>p → f / {V,#}_</rule>
<rule>f → x / _O</rule>
<rule>f → ∅ / else</rule>
<rule>r̩ l̩ → {ri,ra} {li,la} / _{S,R} (which vowel crops up is unpredictable)</rule>
<rule>r̩ l̩ → {ra,ar} {la,al} / _{s,CC,V,#} (the results are unpredictable)</rule>
<rule>m̩ n̩ → am an / _{s,({m,j,w)V}</rule>
<rule>m̩ n̩ → em en / else</rule>
; Stress change:
; — Pronouns, articles, and conjunctions become unstressed.
; — First syllables stress in all verbal imperatives.
; — First syllables stress in all other parts of speech except preverbs and the exceptions noted above.
; — Second syllables receive stress otherwise.
; — “This, unlike the preceding rules, remained a morphologically conditioned rule in Old Irish.”
<rule>ɡʷ → b / #_V ! _u(ː)</rule>
<rule>ɡʷ → b / #_N</rule>
<rule>ɡʷ → b / C_V</rule>
<rule>ɡʷ → ɡ</rule>
<rule>p t k kʷ b d ɡ m n l r s → f θ x xʷ v ð ɣ M N L R h / V(#)_{R,V} (“We don’t know the exact values of lenited /m n l r/. We can guess that lenited m became a nasalized labial continuant of some sort, but beyond that, we don’t know.”)</rule>
<rule>k → x / V_t</rule>
; “[i]t is thought that the vowel needs to be unstressed, but this is not certain”
<rule>m → n / V_#;</rule>
; “[i]t is thought that the long vowel probably needed to be unstressed- again, this is uncertain”
<rule>Vː → V / _N#;</rule>
<rule>p t k kʷ b d ɡ ∅ → b d ɡ ɡʷ mb nd ŋɡ n / n#_”V</rule>
<rule>oː → uː / _(C…)#</rule>
<rule>oːi → uː / _#</rule>
<rule>oː → aː / else</rule>
; “The following three rules only apply if the vowel is unstressed”:
<rule>Vː → V[-long] / _H (includes diphthongs)</rule>
<rule>— e → i / _(C…)#</rule>
<rule>— o → a / _{(C…),u}#</rule>
; “The following two rules apply if the vowel in question is stressed or follows the stressed syllable”; consonant clusters cannot be /nt nd/:
<rule>— {ai,oi} → iː / _#</rule>
<rule>— i u → e o / _C(…C){a(ː),e(ː),o(ː)}</rule>
<rule>— e o → i u / _C(…C){H,j}</rule>
<rule>C → Cʲ / _{F,j}</rule>
<rule>C → Cʷ / _{B,w}</rule>
; For the following: “The book says nothing about length in the input vowels, but I think they could be either short or long from the examples given.”
<rule>Kʷ → K</rule>
<rule>— n → ∅ / {i,o,u}_{p,t,k,s}</rule>
<rule>— {a,e}n → eː / _{p t k s}</rule>
<rule>w → f / #_</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / {#,C}C_</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / {θ,x}_</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / V_{V,#}</rule>
; “The following changes. . .are, quoth the book, ‘somewhat approximative’”:
<rule>w → v / else</rule>
<rule>— {p,t} → ∅ / #s_r</rule>
; “(although it says that occasionally st &gt; t / #_)”
<rule>— {p,t} → ∅ / #s_</rule>
<rule>— s → ∅ / [anything]{l,r}_O</rule>
; — “[A] sequence of two plosives becomes a geminate of the second one”
<rule>— hn hm → nː mː / [anything]_ (“[t]his change is a bit speculative”)</rule>
<rule>— st zd → sː dː / [anything]_</rule>
<rule>— {l,h}l {l,h}r l{p,s,n} r{p,s} ln → lː rː lː rː (lː?) / [anything]_</rule>
<rule>— Cː → C[-long]</rule>
<rule>V → ∅ / C_# when unstressed ! C = j</rule>
; “[t]his remained a phonologically conditioned rule in OIr”
<rule>C(…C) → ∅ / _# ! /l r/ and clusters containing them;</rule>
<rule>{au,eu,ou} → oː</rule>
<rule>ei → eː</rule>
<rule>oː → ua / _[anything], when stressed</rule>
; “ai and oi remain, but are written as &lt;ae ai oe oi&gt; seemingly randomly”
<rule>eː → ia / _{#,Cʲ} when stressed;</rule>
; The second and third rule below “may well have been for the most part optional”; every one of the three “only applies to unstressed vowels” and “remained as a phonologically conditioned rule”:
<rule>j → ∅</rule>
<rule>— Vː → V[-long]</rule>
<rule>— a → e / _#</rule>
<rule>— {e,o} → a / _[anything]</rule>
; “[t]his remained as a phonologically conditioned rule in OIr”
<rule>V → Vː / _#, when stressed;</rule>
; “this is a tad unclear, because in some instances it didn’t seem to apply”
<rule>VOR → VːR;</rule>
; “[t]hat’s a little unclear, so let me try and enumerate: in words of more than three syllables, every other vowel (only the even ones) dropped, if it’s unstressed. In some words, syncope didn’t apply because it would create an unwieldy consonant cluster: so PIE *komaktyom → OIr cumachte, not *cumchte”
<rule>V → ∅ / #UU(_)U(U(_)U) / unstressed; this “remained as a phonologically conditioned rule in OIr”;</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>17.5.2 Proto-Celtic to Middle Welsh</h2>
<cite>Dewrad &amp; Pogostick Man, the latter citing Willis (David), “Old and Middle Welsh”</cite>
<rule>kʷ → p</rule>
<rule>Vː → V / _#</rule>
<rule>ei → eː</rule>
<rule>st → sː (with some exceptions)</rule>
<rule>ai → ɛ</rule>
<rule>s → ∅ / V_V</rule>
<rule>V → ə / _(C)#, also in proclitics</rule>
<rule>s → ∅ / x_</rule>
<rule>{au,eu,ou} → ∅</rule>
<rule>uː {oi,ɔː} → yː uː</rule>
<rule>j → ð / V_</rule>
<rule>i u → e o / _Ca</rule>
<rule>yː → ɨ</rule>
<rule>p t k {b,m} d ɡ → b d ɡ v ð ɣ / _V</rule>
<rule>aː → ɔː</rule>
<rule>a o → ei {ɨ,ei} / _(C…)j(C…)#</rule>
<rule>a → {ɨ,ei} / _(C…)j(C…)#</rule>
<rule>V → ɨ / _(C…)j(C…)#</rule>
<rule>{a,o} → e / _(C…)i(ː)</rule>
<rule>{a,e,o} → ei / _(C…)j</rule>
<rule>V → ∅ / _#</rule>
<rule>mb nd ŋɡ → mː nː ŋː</rule>
<rule>e → i / _N</rule>
<rule>$ → h / V_ (what $ is is unclear)</rule>
<rule>V → ∅ / _[+intertonic]</rule>
<rule>pː tː kː → f θ x</rule>
<rule>p t k → f θ x / {r,l}_</rule>
<rule>ɣ → i / _C</rule>
<rule>xt → iθ</rule>
<rule>ɣ → i / C_V</rule>
<rule>ɛː → ui</rule>
<rule>ɔː → au / when stressed</rule>
<rule>l → ɬ / _t</rule>
<rule>w → ɡw / #_</rule>
<rule>mp nt ŋk → m̥ n̥ ŋ̊</rule>
<rule>ɔ → ə / #_sC</rule>
<rule>l r → ɬ r̥ / #_</rule>
<rule>ɣ → ə / _#</rule>
</section>

<section>
<h2>17.7.1 Common Germanic to Gothic</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wright, Joseph (1910). Grammar of the Gothic Language, 2nd Ed.; and Wikipedia contributors (2014). “Gothic language”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Gothic_language&amp;oldid=635946920&gt;</cite>
; NB: Wright seems to regard Germanic labiovelars as sequences of velar + w if I’m reading this right; additionally, it looks like some of what Wright considers diphthongs may have been long monophthongs.
; Stressed vowels:
<rule>— o e → u i</rule>
<rule>— u → ɔ / _{r,h} (unless this r “arose from older s by assimilation”)</rule>
<rule>— i → ɛ / _{r,h,ʍ}</rule>
<rule>— æː → eː</rule>
; Unstressed vowels:
<rule>— ew → iw</rule>
<rule>— V[- long] → ∅ / _# ! V = u</rule>
; — Inherited “long final vowels…became shortened in polysyllabic words, when the vowels in question originally had the ‘broken’ accent, but remained unshortened when they originally had the ‘slurred’ accent”
<rule>— V[- long] → ∅ / U_C# ! V = u</rule>
; — “Originally long diphthongs became shortened in final syllables”
<rule>— aj → a / U_#</rule>
<rule>iw → ju / [- stress]</rule>
<rule>w → u̯ / V[- long]_{#,C}</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / oː_j</rule>
<rule>oːw æːj → ɔː ɛː / _V</rule>
; “after the loss of a final vowel or syllable”
<rule>j → i / C_#</rule>
; “after the loss of a vowel in final syllables”
<rule>ij → iː / _s</rule>
; “after the loss of a final vowel or syllable”
<rule>ij → i / _#</rule>
; “In a few instances medial -w- (or -ww- the origin of which is uncertain) after short vowels became -ggw- in Gothic…”; similarly, medial *-j(j)- became -ddj- in uncertain conditions
<rule>Vw → u / _s (to wit, the vowel is deleted and the *w syllabifies)</rule>
<rule>iji → iː / U[- stress](C…)_</rule>
<rule>iji → iː / U[+ long + closed]_ in the stem</rule>
<rule>i → ∅ / _ji</rule>
<rule>m → β / C[- voiced]_n, when medial</rule>
<rule>m → ɸ / C[+ voiced]_n, when medial</rule>
<rule>nː → n / _C ! _j</rule>
<rule>β → b / {r,l}_</rule>
<rule>ð → d / C[+ voiced]_</rule>
; “The final -h [= /h/?] in unaccented particles was often assimilated to the initial consonant of the following word”
<rule>β ð ɣ → ɸ θ x / V_(s)#</rule>
<rule>ɣ → ɡ / #_</rule>
; “In the forms of the strong verbs, medial z was supplanted by s through the levelling out of the s-forms…z was also supplanted by s in several weak verbs, which in some cases was due to the influence of the corresponding strong verbs”
<rule>ɣ → ɡ / C_V</rule>
; “remained when protected by a particle”
<rule>z → s / _#, though “[t]his s was dropped when it came to stand after an original s through the loss of a vowel”, though it</rule>
<rule>s → ∅ / V[- long]r_#</rule>
; “[t]he prep[osition]. us…before r in compounds”
<rule>s → r / in</rule>
<rule>s → ∅ / in “[t]he prep[osition]. us…in compounds before st”, though this seems to have been less common</rule>
</section>

<h2>17.7.2.1 West Germanic to Anglo-Frisian</h2>
<cite>Siride? &amp; Pogostick Man, the latter citing Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Anglo-Frisian languages”. <rule>a → ɑ̃ / _N (short only)</rule>
<rule>VN → Ṽː / _F</rule>
<rule>a → æː / short only, includes diphthongs ! B or *ã in next syllable</rule>
<rule>k ɡ → tʃ j(?)</rule>
<rule>æː → aː “under to [sic] the influence of neighboring consonants”, but the article doesn’t elaborate</rule>
<rule>æː → eː</rule>

<rule>h → ∅ / V_V</rule>
; θ resists change to d until the 14th Century
<rule>{i,u} → ∅ / -# ! VC_</rule>

<cite>Pogostick Man, from Moore, Samuel (1919), Historical Outlines of English Phonology and Midd le English Grammar for Courses in Chaucer, Middle English, and the History of the English Language; and Wikipedia contributors (2011), “Middle English phonology”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Middle_English_phonology&amp;oldid=456896605&gt;</cite>

; eːɑ eːo iːe become sounds of uncertain identity; Moore says they were probably diphthongs

<h2>17.7.2.1.4 Midlands Middle English to Early Modern English</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from FireSpeakerWiki contributors (2013), “English sound changes”. FireSpeakerWiki. &lt;http://wiki.firespeaker.org/English_sound_changes&gt;</cite>
<rule>ʊ → ɤ ! P_ and _l</rule>
<rule>mb ŋɡ → m ŋ / _#</rule>
<rule>tj sj dj zj → tʃ ʃ dʒ ʒ / ! _uː (perhaps only before stressed uː?)</rule>
<rule>a ɑ {ɛ,ɪ,ɤ} → ɑː ɔː ɜː / _ɹ{C,#}</rule>
<rule>ɑʊ → ɑː / _P</rule>
<rule>ɑʊ → ɑː / _N (sometimes)</rule>
; “[A] large number of cases that were ɑː have become ɔː subsequently for non-phonetic reasons, like laundry”
<rule>ɑʊ → ɔː / else</rule>
; “in a few words, like ‘father’”
<rule>a → ɑː /</rule>
<rule>a → æ / else</rule>
; “in some parts of South-Eastern England”
<rule>əɪ əʊ → ɑi æʊ /</rule>
; “in most of Britain”
<rule>əɪ əʊ → aɪ aʊ /</rule>
<rule>eː oː → eɪ oʊ / ! _ɹ</rule>
</section>

<h2>17.7.2.1.5 Early Modern English to American English</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from FireSpeakerWiki contributors (2013), “English sound changes”. FireSpeakerWiki. &lt;http://wiki.firespeaker.org/English_sound_changes; and my Phonetic Description class&gt;</cite>
<rule>æ → æː (e.g., NYC) or ɑː (e.g., Boston) / _{F[-voiced],N[-voiced]} (“words which change vary between dialects”)</rule>
<rule>ɒ → ɒː → ɔː / _F[-voiced]</rule>
<rule>æː ɑː ɔː → æə~eə ɑ ɔ</rule>
<rule>ʍ → w (regional)</rule>
; “in some conditions”
<rule>l → ɫ /</rule>
<rule>ɪ → i / _# when unstressed</rule>
<rule>{t,d} → ɾ / V_V[-stress]</rule>
<rule>i u e → ɪ ʊ ɛ / _ɹ</rule>
<rule>o ɔ → ɔ ɒ / _ɹ (most dialects have at least one if not both)</rule>
<rule>æ → ɛ / _ɹ</rule>
<rule>j → ∅ / {θ,s,z,l,n,t,d}_ when in onset position</rule>
; “(partial)”
<rule>ɒ → ɔ / _K</rule>
; “and others depending on dialect”
<rule>æ → ɛə / _{n,m}</rule>
<rule>eə → eː</rule>
<rule>iə → ɪ (ongoing)</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / C_ɹ for some C (toward(s), quart(er), sword)</rule>
<rule>t → ∅ / f_n̩</rule>
; Stuff regarding syllabification (e.g., of /ɹ/) and hiatus
; Loss of pretonic /ə/ in #U (ongoing)
</section>

<h2>17.7.2.1.6 Early Modern English to Australian English</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from FireSpeakerWiki contributors (2013), “English sound changes”. FireSpeakerWiki. &lt;http://wiki.firespeaker.org/English_sound_changes&gt;; and my Phonetic Description class</cite>
<rule>ɜːɹ ɑːɹ ɔːɹ eːɹ oːɹ iːɹ uːɹ → ɜː ɑː ɔː ɛə ɔə ɪə ʊə / syllable-finally</rule>
<rule>ɑ → ɒ</rule>
<rule>æ → æː → ɑː / _{F[-voiced],N[-voiced]}</rule>
<rule>ɒ → ɒː → ɔː / _F[-voiced]</rule>
<rule>ʍ → w</rule>
<rule>l → ɫ (the conditions of this are not elaborated upon)</rule>
<rule>oʊ iː → ɔʊ ɪə / _ɫ</rule>
<rule>oʊ iː → əʊ ɪi / else</rule>
<rule>uː → ʊə → uː / _ɫ ! in Queensland and New South Wales</rule>
<rule>uː → ʊʉ → ʉː / else</rule>
<rule>ɫ → əɫ / ! if one of the above vowel changes after the formation of /ɫ/ apply</rule>
<rule>ɪ → iː / _# when unstressed</rule>
; “unstressed foot-finally if the next syllable is stressed and begins with /k ɡ tʃ dʒ ʃ ʒ/”
<rule>ɪ → iː /</rule>
<rule>ɪ → ə / unstressed</rule>
<rule>ə → ɪ / _{k,ɡ,tʃ,dʒ,ʃ,ʒ,v}</rule>
<rule>t d → ɾ / V_V[-stress]</rule>
<rule>ɔə → ɔː</rule>
<rule>ɛə ʊə → ɛː ɔː (ongoing)</rule>
<rule>ʊə → oː / ! {j,dʒ}_</rule>
; “almost always otherwise, but see [above vowel changes after /ɫ/ is formed]”
<rule>ʊə → ʉːwə /</rule>
<rule>oə → oː</rule>
<rule>j → ∅ / %{θ,s,z,l}_”V</rule>
; “(j~ɫj fluctuation is formality)”
<rule>sj zj lj → ʃ ʒ j~ɫj / else</rule>
; “after any segment after which coda-/ɫ/ is forbidden, e.g. failure [fæiɫiɐ]”
<rule>ɫj → ɫi /</rule>
<rule>tj dj → tʃ dʒ</rule>
<rule>ɔː → ɔ / _ɹV[-stress]</rule>
<rule>ɔː → ɔ / _F[-voiced]</rule>
; “in ‘gone’ and some derivatives”
<rule>ɔ → ɔː /</rule>
<rule>æ → æː / _{n,m,ɡ,ɫ% ! _n,m,ɡ,ɫ}%{j,w} or a form of a strong verb</rule>
<rule>æ → æː / _d (rare) ! form of a strong verb</rule>
</section>

<h2>17.7.2.1.8 Old English to Northern Middle English</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Moore, Samuel (1919), Historical Outlines of English Phonology and Midd le English Grammar for Courses in Chaucer, Middle English, and the History of the English Language; and Wikipedia contributors (2011), “Middle English phonology”. <rule>eɑ eːɑ eo eːo → ɑ ɛː e eː</rule>

<rule>{æːj,e(ː)j} → ej</rule>

<section>
<h2>17.7.2.1.9 Early Northern Middle English to Scots</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Phonological history of Scots”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Phonological_history_of_Scots&amp;oldid=582962563&gt;; and Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Scottish Vowel Length Rule”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Scottish_vowel_length_rule&amp;oldid=589349104&gt;</cite>
<rule>b → ∅ / m_l</rule>
<rule>t → ∅ / {p,k}_# (“except in some inflected forms” for *kt)</rule>
<rule>d → ∅ / n_</rule>
<rule>d → ∅ / l_#</rule>
<rule>s → ʃ / _E (E_ also?)</rule>
; /k ɡ/ remain unpalatalized when E_
; “in certain contexts”
<rule>f → ∅</rule>
<rule>{ɸ,x} → ∅ / _# (seems to be sporadic)</rule>
<rule>ʍ → xw (some speakers seem to have resisted this)</rule>
; ul became some sort of diphthong or vowel (possibly one of uː, uw, ʌw), but the article isn’t very clear
<rule>oɡ → ʌu</rule>
; Vowel shift:
<rule>ol al → ou ɑː → ʌu {ɑ,ɔ}</rule>
<rule>— ai → ɛi → əi / when stem-final</rule>
<rule>— uː → ʌu / when-stem final, in northern varieties</rule>
<rule>— øː → wi / {k,ɡ}_ (in Mid Northern dialects)</rule>
<rule>— øː → i (in northern dialects)</rule>
<rule>— øː → (j){u,ʌ} / _{k,x} (outcome varies depending upon dialect)</rule>
<rule>— a → i / _n (in northern varieties)</rule>
<rule>— a → e / _n (otherwise)</rule>
<rule>— a → {ɛ,e} / _rC</rule>
<rule>— ai oi ui ei au ou iu ɛ(o)u → eː oe əi iː {ɑː,ɔː} ʌu ju j(ʌ)u</rule>
<rule>— ɛː → ɛi (→ əi?) / in some northern varieties</rule>
<rule>— iː eː ɛː aː oː uː {øː,yː} → əi i {i,e} e o u ø</rule>
<rule>— æ → ɛ / _C[+alveolar]</rule>
; Application of the Scottish vowel-length rule:
<rule>— a ɔ u → {a,ɑ} ɔ ʌ</rule>
<rule>— V → Vː / _{r,F[+voiced],$,#}</rule>
<rule>— əi → aɪ / _{r,F[+voiced],$,#} (pursuant to the above)</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>17.7.2.1.10 Old English to Scots</h2>
<cite>Marcas Brian MacStiofáin Ó Mhaitiú Ó Domhnaill, from personal research</cite>

<rule>æː → ɛː</rule>
<rule>ɑ → a / ! _{l,r} (sporadic)</rule>
<rule>oː → ju / {n,x}_</rule>
<rule>oː → iu / _K</rule>
<rule>oː → ø</rule>
; “(not a thorough change)”
<rule>øː → eː</rule>
<rule>ø → ɪ</rule>
<rule>æː(ɑ) eː(o) → ɛ iː</rule>
<rule>ɛ → ɜi → i / _{m,ɲ}</rule>
<rule>ai → aː</rule>
<rule>e → ɛ / _nt</rule>
<rule>{y,i} → ɪ</rule>
<rule>ɪ → ɜ / _{K,r}</rule>
<rule>e(o) → ɛ</rule>
<rule>u → ʊ → ɵ → ʌ</rule>
<rule>uː → u</rule>
<rule>o → ʌ / P_r</rule>
<rule>æ → ɑ / _{x,l}</rule>
<rule>æ(ɑ) → e</rule>
<rule>ɔ{ɡ,j} → ʌu</rule>
<rule>ɑː → e / ! _{ŋ,n}</rule>
<rule>ʌ → ɪi (sporadic)</rule>
; “unstressed and/or final”
<rule>a → ɪ /</rule>
<rule>N → ∅ / _C ! _%C</rule>
<rule>xw → ʍ</rule>
<rule>S[+ voice] → S[- voice] / _#</rule>
<rule>d~ð → d / V_u</rule>
<rule>d~ð → ð / V_V</rule>
<rule>t → ∅ / p_</rule>

<rule>θ → f / {V,r}_# (sometimes blocked)</rule>

; “unstressed”
<rule>e → ɛ /</rule>
<rule>∅ → ə̆ / _{n,r}</rule>

<rule>{w,k} → ∅ / _{n,r}</rule>
<rule>m → n / _f</rule>
<rule>s → ʂ / _{t,r}</rule>
<rule>t → ʈ / _r</rule>

<rule>t → ʔ / _#</rule>

; “(works across word boundaries)”

<h2>17.7.2.2 West Germanic to Old Low Franconian</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Old Dutch”. <h2>17.7.2.2.1 Old Low Franconian to Middle Dutch</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Middle Dutch”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Middle_Dutch&amp;oldid=602536434&gt;; and Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Hieronymous Bosch”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hieronymus_Bosch&amp;oldid=601403790&gt;</cite>
<rule>uː → yː</rule>
<rule>iu → ju / #_ (in some northern dialects)</rule>
<rule>iu → {yː,io} (outcome varies depending upon dialect; the former seems more typical)</rule>
<rule>iw → yw (dialectal)</rule>
; Umlaut phonemicizes, but only for umlauts of non-dipthongal short vowels (except in extreme eastern dialects); [ʏ] becomes a phoneme
<rule>{ie,ia,io} uo → iə uə</rule>
<rule>f θ s → v ð z / syllable-initially (h → ɦ?)</rule>
<rule>V → ə / if short and unstressed</rule>
<rule>f → {x,ç} / _t (the former seems to have occurred in northern dialects, the latter in southern ones)</rule>
<rule>θ ð → t d</rule>
<rule>{uː,uw} u → ɔw o (except in the southeast)</rule>
<rule>{ol,al} {ar,er} or → ɔu aːr oːr / _C[+dental]</rule>
<rule>V[-long +stress] → Vː / in open syllables (ʏ → {œː,øː} here but this is not phonemically important; there seem to have been qualitative differences between original long vowels and long vowels resulting from this change—lengthened iː seems to have become eː, but lengthened aː merged with original aː); does not affect original long vowels or vowels in diphthongs</rule>
</section>

; The change of /f/ to a velar fricative is often reverted by analogy

<rule>— x ɣ → {x,χ} {ɣ,x,χ} / in northern dialects</rule>
<rule>— x ɣ → ç ʝ / in southern dialects (the articles use velar phonemes here but describes them as “front velar”; based on the description and on representations in other articles, the palatal phonemes are used here)</rule>

<h2>17.7.2.3 Middle High German to Standard German</h2>
<cite>Paweł Ciupak, from Behr, Hans-Joahim, Ingrid Bennewitz, et al. (2004). Die Bamberg (BA)-Braunschweiger (BS) Grammatik des Alt- und Mittelhochdeutschen im Internet. &lt;https://www.tu-braunschweig.de/Medien-DB/germanistik/babs260304.pdf&gt;; Kundert, Ursula (2009). <rule>s → ʃ / #_{l,m,n,w,p,t}</rule>
<rule>s → ʃ / r_</rule>
<rule>t → {ts,k} / _w</rule>

<rule>{h,j} → ∅ / V_V</rule>
<rule>w j → b ɡ / {l,r}_ (occasionally otherwise)</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / {ou,øy,yː}_</rule>
<rule>w → v</rule>
<rule>aː → oː / _{N,C[+ dental],P,h} (sporadic?)</rule>
<rule>aː → oː / {N,C[+ dental],P,h}_ (sporadic?)</rule>
<rule>e(ː) i → ø(ː) y / _C[+ affricate]</rule>
<rule>e(ː) i → ø(ː) y / _{P,l,ʃ} (sporadic?)</rule>
<rule>e(ː) i → ø(ː) y / {P,l,ʃ}_ (sporadic?)</rule>
<rule>y(ː) yə ø(ː) øy → i(ː) iə e(ː) ei (intermittent)</rule>
<rule>uː yː iː → ou øy ei, except in certain unstressed endings and monosyllables, _C{C,V,#} (“especially before /xt/”), and Low German borrowings</rule>
<rule>uə yə iə → uː yː iː</rule>
<rule>ou øy ei → au oy ai</rule>
<rule>u y → o ø / _N (with some occasional exceptions)</rule>
<rule>aːw → au</rule>
<rule>Vː → V[- long] / _CC (some exceptions; the change was more common around _xt and _rC)</rule>
<rule>Vː → V[- long] / _%Cə{r,l,n}# (some exceptions)</rule>
<rule>V → Vː / _%, when stressed (except for /ə/?)</rule>
<rule>V → Vː / _r{t,d,s,ts} (except /ə/)</rule>
<rule>V → Vː / in some monosyllables ending in alveolar resonants or vowels</rule>
<rule>V → Vː / by analogy in some cases</rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / unstressed, but not in every case</rule>
<rule>∅ → ə / M_r% (I don’t know what Mr. Ciupak means by 〈M〉)</rule>

<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Old Saxon phonology”. <h2>17.7.3.1.3 Old Norse to Shetland Norn</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man and Marcas Brian MacStiofáin Ó Mhaitiú Ó Domhnaill, from http://nornlanguage.x10.mx/index.php?shet_phon, citing Jakobsen, Jakob, An Etymological Dictionary of the Norn Language</cite>
; NB: For the most part, these changes are not in chronological order and are often tendencies more than strict sound-change laws. Further, I’m assuming that 〈ä〉 is /æ/ and that 〈o̧〉 and 〈ȯ〉 are /ɒ/, and since I’m not sure what the conditions are for (apparent) reflexes with long vowels, I’m ignoring the vowel length in the Shetland Norn reflexes.
<rule>p t k → b d ɡ / V_V (the second V at least can be a syllabic consonant)</rule>
<rule>b d → p t / N_</rule>
<rule>b → v / #_ (sporadic?)</rule>
<rule>lm → ml</rule>

<rule>k ɡ → c ɟ / _E</rule>

<rule>tː → {tʲ,dʲ}</rule>
<rule>tj → tʃ / _#</rule>
<rule>tj → ʃ</rule>
<rule>ɡl → lɡ</rule>
<rule>f → v / #_ (sporadic?)</rule>
<rule>f → m / _n</rule>
<rule>f → p / _t</rule>
<rule>v → w / #_</rule>
<rule>n → ∅ / _m#</rule>
<rule>vl → lv</rule>
<rule>θ → {t,d}</rule>
<rule>ð → ∅ / _#</rule>
<rule>ð → d</rule>
<rule>s → ∅ / k_l</rule>
<rule>∅ → h / #_V</rule>

<rule>h → ∅ / _l</rule>
<rule>h → {∅,h,k} / _r</rule>
<rule>h → {∅,h,k,s} / _n</rule>
<rule>hv → {h,k,s}w</rule>
<rule>hj → ʃ</rule>

<rule>nː → ɲ(d)</rule>
<rule>rn {lː,rl} → ɲ ʎ</rule>
; r alternates with l
<rule>ms → ŋ(k)s</rule>
<rule>{u,o}(ː) a aː {ɒ,œ,y} e i(ː) → {o,ɔ}(i) æ(i) {ɔ,ɒ}(i) {o,ɔ}(i) {æ,e}  / _{Ḱ,Cʲ}</rule>
<rule>u → {o,ɒ} / _CC</rule>
<rule>uː → {u,o,ɒ,ø} (conditioning unclear; it seems the presence of a velar consonant may have helped to retain the quality of /u/)</rule>
<rule>oː → u</rule>
<rule>ɒ → ɛ / _Cː</rule>

<rule>j{u,o,a}ː → ø</rule>
<rule>a → ∅ / C[+ dental/alveolar]_u</rule>
<rule>a → {o,ɔ} / _{K,r} (! K = w ?)</rule>
<rule>aː → wo (dialectal)</rule>
<rule>aː → ɔ(u) / _{l,r}</rule>
<rule>au → {o,ɔ,ɒ} / j_</rule>
<rule>au → j{o,ɔ}</rule>
<rule>y → ə / _r(ː)</rule>
<rule>yː → ø / _O[+ dental/alveolar]</rule>
<rule>æ → e / ! _{Ḱ,Cʲ}</rule>
<rule>e → {o,ɒ} / _w</rule>
<rule>eː → {ɒ,ə} / w_</rule>
<rule>e eː → {æ,ɛ,e} {(j)ɛ,je}</rule>
; Final short vowels drop
<rule>ey → ø</rule>

<h2>17.8.2 Proto-Indo-European to Attic Greek</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Tucker, R. Whitney (1969), “Chronology of Greek Sound Changes”. The American Journal of Philology 90(1):36 – 47; and Wikipedia contributors (2013), “Ancient Greek dialects”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Greek_dialects&amp;oldid=575325271&gt;</cite>
<rule>{Hₓ,m̩,n̩} → a</rule>

<rule>t → ts / _i</rule>

<rule>kʷ kʰʷ ɡʷ → t tʰ d / _E</rule>
<rule>kʷ kʰʷ ɡʷ → p pʰ b / _{a,o,C}</rule>
<rule>kʷ kʰʷ ɡʷ → k kʰ ɡ / _u</rule>
<rule>kʷ kʰʷ ɡʷ → k kʰ ɡ / u_</rule>
<rule>ts → s</rule>
<rule>h → ∅</rule>
<rule>ɑː → æː</rule>
<rule>Vns → Vːs</rule>
<rule>n → ∅ / _s</rule>
<rule>tʃ → t / #_</rule>
<rule>tʃ → tː / medial</rule>
<rule>w → ∅</rule>
; Vowel contraction (on which the author does not elaborate much)
; Some “metathesis of quality as well as of quantity” with regards to vowels
<rule>u(ː)(j) → y(ː)(j)</rule>
<rule>æː → ɛː (includes diphthongs)</rule>
<rule>ej ow → eː oː</rule>
<rule>eː → iː / _C</rule>
<rule>j → ∅ / Vː_</rule>
<rule>eː → iː / _V</rule>
<rule>ɛː → eː</rule>
<rule>ɑj → ɛː</rule>
<rule>h → ∅</rule>
<rule>oj → øj → yj (→ yː sometimes)</rule>
<rule>e o → ɛ ɔ</rule>
; Pitch-accent lost
<rule>pʰ tʰ kʰ → f θ x</rule>
<rule>b d ɡ → v ð ɣ / V_V</rule>
<rule>dz → z</rule>
<rule>Vː → V[-long]</rule>
<rule>Cː → C[-long]</rule>
<rule>ɑu ɛu eu → ɑv ɛv ev</rule>
<rule>ɔ → ∅ / in the suffixes -ios and -ion</rule>
<rule>n → ∅ / _#</rule>
<rule>e → i</rule>
<rule>p t k → b d ɡ / N_</rule>
<rule>y → i</rule>
<rule>ɡ x → j ç / _{ɛ,i}</rule>
<rule>p k → f x / _t</rule>
<rule>”{i,e}V → j”V</rule>
</section>

<rule>ə → i</rule>

<rule>h₂ → *H</rule>
<rule>VH → Vː / _{C,#}</rule>
<rule>R̊H → {u,i}R / _V (sporadic)</rule>
<rule>R̊H → {u,i}ːR / _C</rule>
<rule>ʃ ʒ → ʂ ʐ</rule>
<rule>n → ɳ / R(V)_</rule>
<rule>t d(ʱ) n → ʈ ɖ(ʱ) ɳ / C̣_ ! _r</rule>
<rule>s t d(ʱ) n → ʂ ʈ ɖ(ʱ) ɳ / _C̣</rule>
; “disputed”
<rule>ls lt ld(ʱ) ln → ʂ ʈ ɖ(ʱ) ɳ /</rule>

<rule>p → ∅ / #_st</rule>
<rule>ɟʱ → ɦ</rule>
<rule>dʱ → ɦ / (unclear environment)</rule>
<rule>bʱ → ɦ (very rare)</rule>
<rule>Vm → Ṽ / _(#){s,ɕ}</rule>
<rule>n → ɲ / ɟ_</rule>

<section>
<h2>17.10.1.1 Proto-Indo-Aryan to Central Middle Indo-Aryan</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Shukla, Shaligram (1974), “Phonological change and dialect variation in Middle-Indo-Aryan”. In Anderson, J., and C. Jones (Eds.), Historical Linguistics II:391-401.</cite>
<rule>C(C) → ∅ / C_#</rule>
<rule>VN VC[-nas] → V[+nas] Vː / _#</rule>
<rule>a{i,j}(a) a{u,w}(a) → e o</rule>
<rule>j w → dʒ b / V_V</rule>
<rule>C → C[+voiced] / V_V</rule>
<rule>{bʱ,dʱ,ɡʱ} {j,v} → h ∅ / V_V</rule>
<rule>Vm → Vv → V[+nas]v / _V</rule>
<rule>e o → i u / _#</rule>
<rule>Vː → V[-long] / _#</rule>
<rule>ah → o </rule>
<rule>ɽ → i</rule>
<rule>ʂ → x / k_</rule>
<rule>{ʂ,ç} → s</rule>
<rule>v → ∅ / {t,d}_</rule>
<rule>C₁C₂ → C₂C₂ / V_V</rule>
<rule>Cn → CC / V_V ! C = dʒ</rule>
<rule>dʒɲ → ɳː / V_V</rule>
</section>

<rule>C(C) → ∅ / C_#</rule>

<rule>ʂ s → s ç</rule>
<rule>r → l</rule>
<rule>v → ∅ / {t,d}_</rule>

<h2>17.10.1.4 Proto-Indo-Aryan to Western Middle Indo-Aryan</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Shukla, Shaligram (1974), “Phonological change and dialect variation in Middle-Indo-Aryan”. In Anderson, J., and C. Jones (Eds.), Historical Linguistics II:391-401.</cite>
<rule>C(C) → ∅ / C_#</rule>
<rule>VN VC[-nas] → V[+nas] Vː / _#</rule>
<rule>a{i,j}(a) a{u,w}(a) → e o</rule>
<rule>j w → dʒ b / V_V</rule>
<rule>C → C[+voiced] / V_V</rule>
<rule>{bʱ,dʱ,ɡʱ} {j,v} → h ∅ / V_V</rule>
<rule>Vm → Vv → V[+nas]v / _V</rule>
<rule>e o → i u / _#</rule>
<rule>Vː → V[-long] / _#</rule>
<rule>ah → o </rule>

<rule>kʂ → tʃː</rule>
<rule>{ʂ,ç} → s</rule>
<rule>tv dv → p b</rule>
<rule>C₁C₂ → C₂C₂ / V_V</rule>
<rule>Cn → CC / V_V ! C = dʒ</rule>
<rule>dʒɲ → ɳː / V_V</rule>

<rule>V → {Vː,V[+nas]} / _n{f,s}</rule>
<rule>n → ∅ / V[+nas]_</rule>
<rule>C₁C₂C₃C₄ → (C₃)C₄</rule>
<rule>C₁C₂C₃ → C₁C₃</rule>
</section>

<h2>17.12.1.1.1 Classical Latin vs. Vulgar Latin</h2>
<cite>“The following relate to the changes of vowels as found in the evolution to the written medieval languages of Iberia, Gallia and Italia (Anglo-Norman, Old Spanish, etc.). The Latin of Africa, Sardinia and the easternmost parts of the Empire exhibited different mergers.”</cite>
<rule>{e,i} → j / C_V when unstressed</rule>
<rule>{e,ai} → ɛ</rule>
<rule>{i,eː,oi} → e</rule>
<rule>iː → i</rule>
<rule>o → ɔ</rule>
<rule>{u,oː} → o</rule>
<rule>uː → u</rule>
<rule>aː → a</rule>
; “(in certain common monosyllabic words, as well as some common compounds of them)”
<rule>m → n / _#</rule>
<rule>m → ∅ / _#</rule>
<rule>h → ∅</rule>
<rule>w → β</rule>
<rule>ɛ ɔ → e o / when unstressed</rule>
<rule>j → ʝ / #_V</rule>
<rule>j → ʝː / V_V</rule>
<rule>“In contrast, Romanian exhibits u, uː → u (and ultimately also ɔ, oː → o); and Sardinian and African Latin underwent a straight merger of the vowels by length without considering quality (e, eː → e; i, iː → i; u, uː → u; etc.)”</rule>

<h2>17.12.1.1.2 Latin to Catalan</h2>

; NB: Due to problems when the board migrated to a different system, a lot of the special characters were replaced with 〈?〉. In many cases these have been replaced with 〈∅〉 because it was likely that this was what was meant, but conditional 〈?〉 has either been left alone or attempted to have been filled in from context. In some cases, conditional 〈?〉 may have been used to mark stress or syllable boundaries. Take such changes with a grain of salt and use at your own risk.

<h2>17.12.1.1.3 Latin to French</h2>
<cite>pharazon</cite>
; NB: The vowels here marked 〈ó〉 and 〈ò〉 seem to have had some sort of open-close distinction similar to /o ɔ/.
; Vulgar Latin:
<rule>— h → ∅</rule>
<rule>— V₀V₀ → V₀ː</rule>
<rule>— n → ∅ / _{f,v,s}</rule>
<rule>— r → s / _s</rule>

<rule>— m → n / _#</rule>

; “from Germanic loanwords”
<rule>— w → ɡu /</rule>
<rule>— V → ”V / ”VSr_</rule>
<rule>— V → ”V / _C*”{i,e}V</rule>
; Stressed vowels:
<rule>— {i,e} → j / _V</rule>
<rule>— aː → a</rule>
<rule>— (a)e → è</rule>
<rule>— {eː,i,oe} → é</rule>
<rule>— iː oː → i ó</rule>
<rule>— o → ò</rule>
<rule>— u → ó / !_iː</rule>
; Initial vowels (first vowel of a word):
<rule>— uː → u</rule>
<rule>— aː → a</rule>
<rule>— {e(ː),i,ae,oe} → e</rule>
<rule>— iː → i</rule>
; Final vowels:
<rule>— {o(ː),u} → o</rule>
<rule>— aː → a</rule>
<rule>— {e(ː),i,ae,oe} → e</rule>
<rule>— iː oː → i o</rule>
<rule>— u(ː) → o / except _V (?)</rule>
<rule>k ɡ → tj dj / _E</rule>

<rule>è → ɛ / in U[+closed]</rule>
<rule>ò → uo → uɛ / in U[+open] ! _N</rule>
<rule>ò → ɔ / in U[+closed]</rule>
<rule>dj → dʒ / r_</rule>
<rule>d → ∅ / _j</rule>
<rule>j → ∅ / V_”E</rule>
<rule>j tj → dʒ ts / #_</rule>
<rule>j → dʒ / V_V (rare)</rule>

<rule>{ɡn,nj} → ɲ</rule>
<rule>nk → ɲ / _t</rule>
<rule>V → ∅ / in the unstressed penult</rule>

<rule>a → ə / intertonic</rule>
<rule>∅ → b / m_{r,l}</rule>
<rule>∅ → d / {n,l,ɲ,z_r</rule>
<rule>∅ → t / s_r</rule>
<rule>k ɡ → t d / {n,r}_r</rule>
; “[T]wo obstruents in contact with different voicing assimilate to the voicing of the second”
<rule>n → r / {ɡ,p}_</rule>
<rule>C → ∅ / C₁_C₂ ! C₂ = {r,l}</rule>
<rule>t → s / _{n,m}</rule>
<rule>{kl,ɡl,lj} → ʎ</rule>
<rule>{p,b} {t,d} → v ð / V_{V,r}</rule>
<rule>v → ∅ / V_B</rule>
<rule>p → b / _l</rule>

<rule>k → js / V_sV</rule>

<rule>{k,ɡ} → ∅ / B_a</rule>
<rule>{k,ɡ} → j / _{a,C}</rule>
<rule>kʷ → {v,u} / V_E</rule>
; “[N]ote that the [following] clusters are the only case where a consonant does not receive intervocalic treatment before /j/”:
<rule>kʷ → j{v,u} / V_a</rule>
<rule>— (k)kj → ts</rule>
<rule>— ɡ → ∅ / _j</rule>

<rule>V₀V₀ → V₀</rule>

<rule>t → ∅ / {ʃ,s}</rule>
<rule>d → ∅ / _{z,ʒ}</rule>

<rule>∅ → j / {ʃ,ʒ,sj,zj}”{a,é}_ in U[+open]</rule>
<rule>sːj zj rj → jsː jz jr</rule>
<rule>j → ∅ / s_ (sː_?)</rule>
<rule>ɛ → ɛa / _l{C,#}</rule>
<rule>l → u / _{C,#}</rule>
<rule>l → ∅ / {i,u}_</rule>
; “[this is actually an analogical development, but it applies as regularly as a sound law]”
<rule>{lːe,lːo} → u / {e,o}_#</rule>
<rule>(ɛ)au → ɔ</rule>
<rule>é → ɛi / in U[+open]</rule>
<rule>é → ɛ / in U[+closed]</rule>
<rule>ó → ou → ɛu / in U[+open]</rule>
<rule>ó → ɔ / _N</rule>
<rule>ó → ou / in U[+closed]</rule>
<rule>e → ə / #(C…)_(%…)” in U[+open]</rule>

; “(the outcome fluctuates, but ɔ is often the result of analogy rather than strict sound change; always ou before another vowel)”
<rule>o → {ou,ɔ}</rule>
<rule>a → ə / #{tʃ,dʒ}_(%…)” in U[+open]</rule>
; “(but a following ʎ creates a [closed] syllable)”
<rule>a → ɛ / in U[+open]</rule>
<rule>kʷ ɡʷ → k ɡ</rule>
<rule>C₀C₀ → C₀</rule>
<rule>t → ∅ / V_#</rule>
<rule>ɛ → i / _C(C…)i#</rule>
<rule>V → ∅ / _# “(except in monosyllables or after another vowel)” ! V = a</rule>
<rule>a → ə</rule>
<rule>V → ə / _{CC,tʃ,dʒ} ! _{nt,nɡ,mp,rt,rd}</rule>
<rule>s → ∅ / _C</rule>
<rule>{p,b} → ∅ / _{t,d}</rule>
<rule>v → ∅ / _C</rule>
<rule>v → ∅ / C_</rule>
<rule>ð → ∅</rule>
<rule>uɛ → ɛu</rule>
<rule>ai → e / _#</rule>
<rule>ai iɛɪ → ɛ i</rule>
<rule>ou ɛu u uɛi → u œ y yi</rule>
<rule>{ei,ɔi} → oi / C[-nas]</rule>
<rule>ɔ → u / _”V</rule>
; “(except that a is kept before o)”
<rule>V[-high] → ə → ∅ / _V</rule>
; V{n,m} / V[+nas] / _{C,#}
<rule>ɲ → in / _{C,#}</rule>
<rule>ɛ̃ → ã</rule>
<rule>{aı̃,eı̃} → ɛ̃</rule>
<rule>ỹ → œ̃</rule>

<rule>j → ∅ / {ʃ,ʒ}_V[-nas]</rule>
<rule>ʎ r → j ʁ</rule>
<rule>oi → wɛ → wa</rule>
; “([pharazon has] omitted the loss of ə in various contexts, since it often resurfaces)”
<rule>oı̃ → wɛ̃</rule>

<h2>17.12.1.1.5 Latin to Portuguese</h2>
<cite>Mecislau</cite>
; NB: Due to problems when the board migrated to a different system, a lot of the special characters were replaced with 〈?〉. In many cases these have been replaced with 〈∅〉 because it was likely that this was what was meant, but conditional 〈?〉 has either been left alone or attempted to have been filled in from context. In some cases, conditional 〈?〉 may have been used to mark stress or syllable boundaries. Take such changes with a grain of salt and use at your own risk. Further, Mecislau gives some dual-output changes, which distinguish between vulgar and “semi-learned” outcomes.
<rule>h → ∅</rule>

<rule>o → ∅ / _{r,l} when between #U and U[+tonic]</rule>

<rule>p → ∅ / V_{t,s}V</rule>
<rule>k → j / V_tV</rule>
<rule>k → ∅ / Vn_tV</rule>
<rule>ks ɡn tj → ʃ ɲ {z,s} / V_V (tj → s is learned)</rule>
<rule>tj → s / C_V</rule>
<rule>dj → dʒ → ʒ / V_V</rule>
<rule>dj → dz → ts → s / r_V</rule>
<rule>ndj → nts → ns → ɲ / V_V</rule>
<rule>sj → jʒ / V_V</rule>
<rule>j → ∅ / i_ʒV</rule>
<rule>sːj (m)nj lj rj → jʃ ɲʎ jr / V_V</rule>
<rule>kj → ts → s / V_V</rule>
<rule>ɡj → {ʒ,j} / V_V (ɡj → j is learned)</rule>
<rule>pj {bj,vj} → jb jv / V_V</rule>
<rule>mj → jm (irregular)</rule>
<rule>C₀C₀ → C₀ / ! C = r</rule>
<rule>C → ∅ / C₁_C₂ ! C₂ = L</rule>
<rule>k → j / _s#</rule>
<rule>f → v / V_V (irregular)</rule>
<rule>s l → z ∅ / V_V</rule>
<rule>a → ∅ / ”ɔ_#</rule>
<rule>sk k → jʃ z / V_{i,e}</rule>
<rule>k → ɡ / V₁_V₂ ! V₂ = ɔ</rule>
<rule>j → {∅,ʒ} / V_V (j → ʒ is learned)</rule>
<rule>b → v / VL_V</rule>
<rule>m → ∅ / _n</rule>
<rule>{e,i} → ∅ / {L,N}_(C)(C)V(C)#</rule>
<rule>o → ∅ / _(C)(C)V(C)#</rule>
<rule>e → j / {a,o,u}_</rule>
<rule>a → e / _j when stressed</rule>
<rule>o → u / _ɲ when stressed</rule>
<rule>V₀V₀ → V₀ (irregular)</rule>
<rule>d → ∅ / V_V (in Portugal)</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>17.12.1.1.6 Vulgar Latin to Old Provençal</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Grandgent, Charles Hall (1905), An outline of the phonology and morphology of old Provençal, Revised Edition</cite>
; NB: Use at your own peril. Trying to put a chronology to this is sort of like what I imagine undergoing a root canal would be like, as is figuring out the conditioning on a lot of these things because of the convention Grandgent uses. Nevertheless, I have triedâand probably largely failed. In any case the sections regarding the development of the vowels are placed first, because the source does that and other Romance changes posted here do similarly, and the grouping of the consonants is in large part informed by the surce. Also, I use % here to denote a syllable boundary because I didn’t want to have to open yet another window so I could throw a sigma into my document.
; Stress shift: Secondary stress shifts to two syllables away from the penult. If the secondary tonic precedes the tonic, that vowel is considered stressed for the purpose of subsequent sound changes, and at some point the intervening vowel drops. Vowel changes assume the changes in Vulgar Latin as listed elsewhere in this thread.
; STRESSED VOWELS
<rule>VN → Vː / _S (except for the prefixes con-, in-); I’m assuming this change happened in Vulgar Latin and then vowel length went to quality</rule>
<rule>— ɪ → e</rule>
<rule>— ʊ → o (this change seems to have happened later, hence is listed separately)</rule>
<rule>— ɔ → y / _{u,P,k,ɡ,i} (in northwestern dialects)</rule>
<rule>— ɔ → ɥe / _{u,P,k,ɡ,i} (in western dialects, Limousin, and Auvergne)</rule>
<rule>— ɔ → ɥo / _{u,P,k,ɡ,i} (in Languedoc)</rule>
<rule>— ɔ → {ɥe,ɥo,ɔ} / _{u,P,k,ɡ,i} (in southern dialects)</rule>
<rule>— a → a / _N (Rouergue, Limousin, Auvergne, Dauphiné)</rule>
<rule>— a → a / _# in monosyllables and oxytones (Rouergue, Limousin, Auvergne, Dauphiné)</rule>
<rule>— a → å (I have no idea what is going on here. Grandgent seems to distinguish an open and close /a/, and I have listed his open a as /å/, which seems to have been distinct from /ɔ/, but beyond this section it doesn’t really seem to matter very much)</rule>
<rule>— å ɛ ɔ → a e o / _N (in Limousin and neighboring regions, the last two particularly in Limousin, Languedoc, and Gascon, though when _ɲ this change may have been blocked)</rule>
<rule>— ɛ ɔ → jɛ wɔ (intermittent, “least common in the southwest”)</rule>
<rule>— e → i / _V (and possibly V_?)</rule>
<rule>— e → i / _(C…)i{C(C…)V,#}</rule>
<rule>— ɛ → jɛ / _{u,i,ʎ,rʲ,ʃ,ʒ,j,tʃ,dʒ} (except in some northern and western dialects, or if this u ← l or if this i ← ð)</rule>
<rule>— ju → jeu</rule>
<rule>— o → y / _{tʃ,dʒ,it,id} (did not occur in Dauphiné)</rule>
<rule>— o → y / _ɲ (in northern and western dialects)</rule>
<rule>— o → y / _i# (in Bordeaux, Auvergne, and some of Languedoc)</rule>
<rule>— ɔ → {ɔ,we} (in southwestern dialects)</rule>
<rule>— u → y</rule>
; UNSTRESSED VOWELS
<rule>— o → u (during the literary period)</rule>
<rule>— E → ∅ / _e</rule>
<rule>— B → ∅ / _o</rule>
<rule>— E → j (presumably in the vicinity of another vowel)</rule>
; INITIAL-SYLLABLE VOWELS
<rule>— V → ∅ (though /a/ seemed to resist this)</rule>
<rule>— {a,œ,e,i} → e (here, 〈œ〉 denotes the reflex of the Latin vowel written this way, not a front rounded vowel)</rule>
<rule>— u → o</rule>
<rule>— au → a / _(C…)u</rule>
<rule>— V → ∅ / _r (seemed to be an intermittent change)</rule>
; — Lots and lots of analogical formations; 
; INTERTONIC VOWELS
; PENULT VOWELS
<rule>— V → ∅ (again, /a/ seemed to resist this change, which was itself generally blocked by analogy)</rule>
<rule>— V → ∅ / “penult of paroxytones”, though /a/ often remained “as an indistinct e”, probably /ə/</rule>
; — V often remained when {tʃ,dʒ,j}_, especially if CC_, or when {(k)s,sː,sj}_
; — V is retained when P_C[+dental]
<rule>— CVK → CVj (intermittent if C was a resonant)</rule>
<rule>— V → ∅ / lv_r (dialect-dependent)</rule>
<rule>— e → i / _(C…)i(C…)#</rule>
; FINAL VOWELS
; — (Again, /a/ seems to be a persistent exception to these)
; — Grandgent remarks that /i/ was probably the last vowel to drop out
<rule>— V → ∅ / R_(C…)</rule>
; — /i/ and /u/ remain when ”V_ (then u is subject to the changes listed above—u → y, &amp;c.)
; “Limousin and some others”
<rule>— a → o in most dialects except Gascon and Languedoc; final -as remained in</rule>
<rule>— e u → e o / _nt#</rule>
; — -os remains “in the extreme east”
; — Final -i remains “as late as the 12th century” in some regions (Aude, Tarn, Aveyron, Corrèze, and some pockets of Haute-Garrone)
; — Epenthesis of /e/ in 2<span class="smallcaps">sg</span> “of some verbs”
<rule>— V → “indistinct e” (probably /ə/) if dropping it would create ugly consonant clusters:</rule>
; —— C_L, P_C[+dental], C_tʃ, C_k, C_m, C_n where “originally separated by the vowel of the penult” (proparoxytones)
; —— Ḱ_r (paroxytones)
; —— Where the cluster would be unwieldy otherwise, apocope happened
; —— Final /(m)bj mnj pj mj/ “required a supporting vowel” (dialect-dependent)
; “in many late words”
<rule>— V → e</rule>
<rule>K → ∅ / _l (not always, but this was a general change)</rule>
<rule>v → b / r_ (sporadic)</rule>
<rule>h → ∅</rule>
<rule>d → ∅ / V_V (seems to have happened in the north and northeast at some point)</rule>
<rule>b d ɡ → β ð ɣ / V_V</rule>
<rule>βj → j (in forms of habeo and debeo)</rule>
<rule>k ɡ → tʃ dʒ / _E</rule>
<rule>tʃ → ts (sometimes)</rule>
<rule>ɡ → ∅ / V_(VC…)”V</rule>
<rule>j → ∅ / V_”E</rule>
<rule>N → ∅ / _#, in polysyllables</rule>
<rule>kʷ ɡʷ → k ɡ / _B</rule>
<rule>rs → sː</rule>
<rule>sː → s / Vː_</rule>
<rule>p t k s → b d ɡ z / V_V (this b → v?)</rule>
<rule>∅ → i / #_sC</rule>
<rule>w → v → ∅ / _u (“restored by analogy in many words”)</rule>
<rule>w → v → ∅ / _”o</rule>
<rule>w → v → β</rule>
<rule>β → w / V_C</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / C_B (when from earlier B?)</rule>
<rule>k → ∅ / _s{C,#} (the latter in polysyllables only)</rule>
<rule>{d,ɡ} → ∅ / _j</rule>
; Loan phonemes:
; — Loaned /b/ did not lenite
; — Loaned /k/ did not palatalize
<rule>— Loaned w → ɡw</rule>
; Greek /k/ shows up variously as /k ɡ/
<rule>Original z (/ts/?) → dj → j</rule>
; Some reanalysis of initials as medials if a prefixed form was reanalyzed as a single morpheme
<rule>pʰ → f</rule>
<rule>(s)k ɡ → (s)tʃ dʒ / #_a (in the north and northeast)</rule>
<rule>tʃ → ts → s / #_</rule>
<rule>j → dʒ / #_ (but not in Béarn)</rule>
<rule>f → h (in Béarn and Gascon)</rule>
<rule>β → b (in Béarn, Gascon, and Languedoc)</rule>
<rule>β → v (though sometimes → ɡw instead if analogy interfered)</rule>
<rule>kʷ ɡʷ → kw ɡw (in western dialects)</rule>
<rule>kʷ ɡʷ → k ɡ (else)</rule>
<rule>∅ → k / #s_l</rule>
<rule>i → e / #_sC</rule>
<rule>b {d,dz,dʒ} z ʒ ɡ → p {t,ts,tʃ} s ʃ k / _(s)#</rule>
<rule>tʃs → {ts,tʃ} (varies)</rule>
<rule>j → i / _(s)#</rule>
<rule>ð → ∅ / _#</rule>
<rule>ð → t / _s#</rule>
<rule>β → u / V_(s)#</rule>
<rule>β → {∅,f} / C_(s)# (the latter is rare)</rule>
<rule>n → ∅ / V_# (did not occur in extreme western areas, some northern areas, the southeast, and the east)</rule>
<rule>n → ∅ / _s# (except for eastern and southeastern dialects)</rule>
<rule>n → ∅ / r_(s)#?</rule>
<rule>kː → tʃ / _a (in the east and northeast)</rule>
; rː~r stuff—not sure what was going on here, but it seems like this distinction lingered on into the literary period, but the two may have been in the process of merging
<rule>lː → l (in the south)</rule>
<rule>mn → mpn → nː (dialectal)</rule>
<rule>Cː → C</rule>
<rule>ɡ → k / B_ (your guess is probably better than mine)</rule>
<rule>ɡ → {k,j} / V_ (if /j/ resulted, it dropped after /i/; forms with k are “most persistent in the west” and more common overall)</rule>
<rule>l → w / V_s</rule>
<rule>ms ns → mps nts (sporadic?)</rule>
<rule>{p,b} → ∅ / R_s# (unless blocked by analogy)</rule>
<rule>ts → s / _# (Provence, Limousin, some Languedoc and Gascon)</rule>
<rule>β → ∅ / when pretonic and immediately adjacent to a back vowel</rule>

<rule>β → v (otherwise)</rule>
<rule>tʃ → i / _C</rule>

<rule>dʒ → j</rule>

<rule>{d,k}w → ɡʷ → ɡ</rule>

; The outcome of final nt was usually n, but in the extreme north and some areas of the south, the full cluster was kept as part of the ending -ant; further, “in some dialects the n fell after o, u”
; “in strong preterits”
<rule>t → ∅ / _# ! “in the preterit of verbs”, though it tended to drop</rule>

<section>
<h2>17.12.1.1.7 Vulgar Latin to Rhaeto-Romance</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Rhaeto-Romance languages”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Rhaeto-Romance_languages&amp;oldid=607581179&gt;</cite>
; NB: These are probably very incomplete and too general, but they seem to be the key distinguishing features of Rhaeto-Romance.
<rule>ɛ e → ej je</rule>
<rule>a → e / stressed, usually when Ḱ_</rule>
<rule>uː → y (→ i in most descendants, with the exception of Engadine)</rule>
<rule>a V → e (= /ə/?) ∅ / in final syllables (though Friulian preserves the ending -is)</rule>
<rule>k ɡ → tʃ dʒ / _a (note the similarity with some varieties of Old Provençal)</rule>
; /pl fl kl/ preserved
; Germanic loaned /w/ preserved—i.e., it did not become /ɡw/
<rule>C[+ voiced] → ∅ / V_V (only for obstruents?)</rule>
; Final /s/ preserved
<rule>C[- voiced] → C[+ voiced] / V_V</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>17.12.1.1.8 Latin to Romanian</h2>
<cite>pharazon, from Jensen, A Comparative Study of Romance, as well as other sources</cite>
<rule>h → ∅</rule>
<rule>V₀V₀ → V₀ː</rule>
<rule>n → ∅ / _{f,v,s}</rule>
<rule>r → s / _s</rule>
<rule>{m,n,s} → ∅ / _# in polysyllables</rule>
<rule>m s → n i / _#</rule>
<rule>u → ∅ / CC_V</rule>
<rule>V → ”V / ”VSr_</rule>
; “Stressed vowels (note the difference from the French development)”:
<rule>V → ”V / _(C…)”{i,e}V</rule>
<rule>— aː → a</rule>
<rule>— (a)e → ɛ</rule>
<rule>— eː,i,oe → e</rule>
; Word-initial vowels:
<rule>— iː o(ː) u(ː) → i o u</rule>
<rule>— aː → a</rule>
<rule>— {e(ː),i,ae,oe} → e</rule>
<rule>— iː → i</rule>
; Word-final vowels:
<rule>— {o(ː),u} → o</rule>
<rule>— aː → a</rule>
<rule>— {e(ː),i,ae,oe} → e / _#</rule>
<rule>— iː → i</rule>
<rule>— {o(ː),uː} → o</rule>
<rule>— u → o / ! V_</rule>
<rule>s → i / #(C…)V_#</rule>
<rule>k → ∅ / _s#</rule>
<rule>{s,t} → ∅ / _#</rule>
<rule>kʷ ɡʷ → p b / V_a</rule>
<rule>ɡ → m / _n</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / {k,ɡ}_</rule>
<rule>k ɡ → tʃ dʒ / _E</rule>
<rule>ɛ → ie</rule>
<rule>t d s → ts dz ʃ / _i</rule>
<rule>o e → u i / _N</rule>
<rule>a → ɨ _N ! _{nː,mn}</rule>
<rule>i → ∅ / OL_e</rule>
<rule>e → a / i_(C…){a,e}#</rule>
<rule>i → ∅ / {ts,dz,S}_V</rule>
<rule>li → lj / _V</rule>
<rule>l → lj / _i</rule>
<rule>lː → ∅ / _i</rule>
<rule>”elːa → e”a / _#</rule>
<rule>{b,v} → ∅ / V_{V,t}</rule>
<rule>l → r / V_V</rule>
<rule>m → u / a_nV</rule>
<rule>b → u / V_{l,r}</rule>
<rule>p → ∅ / _s</rule>
<rule>c → p / _{s,t}</rule>

<rule>ts (n)dz → t nd</rule>
<rule>h → ∅ / V_C</rule>
<rule>ʔ → ∅</rule>
<rule>r ŋʷ → n β / V[+nas]_V[+nas]</rule>
<rule>ð → (n)d</rule>
<rule>ŋɡ → k / ! _V[-nas]</rule>
<rule>u → ɨ / _{p,b}i</rule>
<rule>{a/e} {e/a} {ı̃,ẽ} → a e ı̃</rule>
</section>

<rule>u → ∅ / _o</rule>
<rule>o → ∅ / _u</rule>
<rule>o → a / _Ca</rule>
; “Some weird stuff with vowel pharyngealization/glottalization; some of the pharyngealized proto-vowels stayed that way, others glottalized”
<rule>a → ∅ / _(C)o</rule>
<rule>ʕm → {b,ɓ}</rule>
<rule>n → ∅ / _#</rule>
<rule>ts(ʼ) s → tʃ(ʼ) ʃ</rule>
<rule>!! ʘ → ǁ ǀ</rule>
<rule>ʔ → ∅ / _nQ</rule>
<rule>ⁿQʰ {ǀʰ͜q,ǀʰ͜ɢ} Q͡q → ⁿQ(ʰ) ⁿǀʰ Q[+voiced]</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>21 Lakes Plain</h2>
</section>
<section>
<h2>21.1 Proto-Lakes Plain to Proto-Far West</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Clouse, Duane (1993), “Languages of the Western Lakes Plains”. IRIAN: Bulletin of Irian Jaya XXI:1 – 17</cite>
<rule>ɾ → ∅</rule>
<rule>ku →∅ / #_</rule>
<rule>d → ɾ / V_V</rule>
<rule>∅ → echo vowel / C_CV</rule>
<rule>CV → ∅ / _# (possibly only when CV_#, possibly sporadic)</rule>
<rule>e o → ɛ ɔ (?)</rule>
</section>

<cite>Pogostick Man, from Clouse, Duane (1993), “Languages of the Western Lakes Plains”. IRIAN: Bulletin of Irian Jaya XXI:1 – 17</cite>

<rule>b → β~m / #_V[-high]</rule>
<rule>b d ɡ → β~w ɾ~∅ ɣ / V_V</rule>
<rule>ti → s / #_V</rule>
<rule>t d → t~ɾ~n n / #_</rule>
<rule>iiɛ Vdiɛ → ijɛ βe</rule>
</section>

<h2>22.1 Proto-Arawá to Arawá</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Dixon, R.M.W. (2004), “Proto-Arawá Phonology”. Anthropological Linguistics 46(1):1 – 83</cite>
<rule>p → f</rule>
<rule>pʰ → p / #_</rule>
<rule>pʰ → ɸ / else</rule>
<rule>ɗ → t / #_</rule>
<rule>dz → s / medially</rule>
<rule>tsʰ → s</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>22.2 Proto-Arawá to Banawá-Jamamadi</h2>
<cite>chris_notts &amp; Pogostick Man, the latter citing Dixon, R.M.W. (2004), “Proto-Arawá Phonology”. Anthropological Linguistics 46(1):1 – 83</cite>
<rule>ɓ → b / #_</rule>

<rule>p(ʰ) → ɸ</rule>
<rule>{tʰ,ɗ} → t</rule>
<rule>kʰ ɡ → k w</rule>
<rule>{ts(ʰ),tʃ} dz → s ɟ</rule>
<rule>ʔ → ∅</rule>
</section>

<rule>i → ∅ / a_ (usually; sometimes the diphthong is retained or → aj)</rule>

<rule>u → ∅ / a_</rule>

<rule>e → ∅ / u_C</rule>

<section>
<h2>23.1.1.1.6 Proto-Chibchan to Tunebo</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wheeler, Alva (1972), “Proto-Chibchan”. In Matteson, Esther, ed., Comparative Studies in Amerindian Languages 93 – 108</cite>
<rule>ɡ ɡʷ → ∅ b</rule>
<rule>{d(V)s,n(V)j} n(V)s → r {VsV,r}</rule>
<rule>d(V)j → r(V)</rule>
<rule>d → r</rule>
<rule>s → h / _(V)C</rule>
<rule>ts tʃ → tʃ s</rule>
<rule>m n h j → b {n,r,∅} {h,∅} {j,∅}</rule>
<rule>e → a</rule>
<rule>ai → e / if the *a is not stressed</rule>
<rule>a → ∅ / _u</rule>
<rule>i → a / _Ca</rule>
<rule>{a,i} → ∅ / C”V(C)_</rule>
<rule>i → ∅ / _(C)”V</rule>
<rule>e → ∅ / u_C</rule>
; “conditioning undetermined”
<rule>e → i /</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>23.1.2 Proto-Lenmichian to Proto-Lencan</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2015), “Macro-Chibchan languages”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Macro-Chibchan_languages&amp;oldid=672637970&gt;, presumably citing Constenla UmaÃ±a, Adolfo (2005), “?‘Existe relación genealógica entre las lenguas misumalpas y las chibchenses?”. Estudios de LingÃ¼Ã­stica Chibcha 24:7 – 85</cite>
<rule>b d → m n / _V[+ nasal] (I’m inferring this from the statement that “[t]here are also a series…of nasal vowels”)</rule>
<rule>b d → p l</rule>
<rule>{ʔ,h} → ∅</rule>
<rule>{s,ts} → tsʼ</rule>
<rule>o a → {u,o} {a,e}</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>23.1.3 Proto-Lenmichian to Proto-Misumalpan</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2015), “Macro-Chibchan languages”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Macro-Chibchan_languages&amp;oldid=672637970&gt;, presumably citing Constenla UmaÃ±a, Adolfo (2005), “?‘Existe relación genealógica entre las lenguas misumalpas y las chibchenses?”. Estudios de LingÃ¼Ã­stica Chibcha 24:7 – 85</cite>
<rule>b d → m n / _V[+ nasal] (I’m inferring this from the statement that “[t]here are also a series…of nasal vowels”)</rule>

<rule>{ʔ,h} → ∅</rule>

<cite>NB: These changes are most likely largely incomplete, especially for languages with fewer changes shown.</cite>

<section>
<h2>24.1.1 Proto-Paman to AritinÅitiÉ£</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Hale, Kenneth (1964), “Classification of Northern Paman Languages, Cape York Peninsula, Australia: A Research Report”. Oceanic Linguistics Vol. 3, No. 2, 248 – 265</cite>
<rule>C → ∅ / #_</rule>
<rule>i[-long]C → Cj / #_ ! _i</rule>
<rule>u[-long]C → Cw / #_</rule>
<rule>a[-long]C → Ca / #_ (! _a?)</rule>
<rule>u i → w j / a_ when this a is a result of metathesis (?)</rule>
<rule>Vː → V[-long] / in #U</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>24.1.2 Proto-Paman to AwÅtim</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Hale, Kenneth (1964), “Classification of Northern Paman Languages, Cape York Peninsula, Australia: A Research Report”. Oceanic Linguistics Vol. 3, No. 2, 248 – 265</cite>
<rule>iC aC uC → Cj Ca Cw / #_ ! before an identical vowel</rule>
<rule>u i → w j / a_ when this a is a result of the preceding metathesis</rule>
<rule>(N)S → F / #(C)V_</rule>
<rule>∅ → j / #(C)iː(C)_V ! _i</rule>
<rule>∅ → w / #(C)uː(C)_V ! _u</rule>
<rule>∅ → a / #(C)aː(C)_V ! _a</rule>
<rule>C → ∅ / #_</rule>
<rule>Vː → ə / in #U</rule>
</section>

<rule>V → ∅ / Vk_#</rule>
<rule>x → j / V₀_V₀</rule>
<rule>x → h</rule>
</section>

<h2>29.1.1.1.10 Proto-Athabaskan to Easter Gwich’in</h2>
<cite>Whimemsz, from Krauss, Michael and Victor Golla (1981), “Northern Athapaskan Languages”. Handbook of North American Indians, Vol. 6 (Subarctic), 67 – 85</cite>
; NB: Here, 〈TS〉 represents a sound that Whimemsz says “is between” the TH and TS series POA-wise.
<rule>TS K → TŠ TS / _E</rule>
<rule>TS K → TH TŠ / else</rule>
<rule>TŠ TŠʷ Q → TS TṢ K</rule>
<rule>j w → ʒ v</rule>
<rule>{n,ɲ} → ⁿdʒ / _E[-nas]</rule>
<rule>{n,ɲ} → ⁿd / _V[-nas]</rule>
<rule>{i,e} → {i,ja} (this latter due to the loss of final consonants within the stem)</rule>
<rule>a u {ɑ,ə} ʊ → {i,e} ju a o</rule>
; “An ‘extensive reduction’ of stem-final consonants; however, reflexes of final *-ɲ and *-n after PA *a and *e are kept distinct”
<rule>Vˀ → V[+low tone]</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>29.1.1.1.11 Proto-Athabaskan to Han</h2>
<cite>Whimemsz, from Krauss, Michael and Victor Golla (1981), “Northern Athapaskan Languages”. Handbook of North American Indians, Vol. 6 (Subarctic), 67 – 85</cite>
; Occasional palatalization in front of high vowels
<rule>TS TŠ TŠʷ K Q → TH TS TṢ TŠ K</rule>
<rule>n → (ⁿ)d / $_V[-nas]</rule>
<rule>j → ʒ / $_</rule>
; Acquisition of vowel length, but how this occurs is not described
<rule>a ɑ ə ʊ → æ a {ə,ë} o</rule>
; Majority of stem-final consonants lost; the only stem-finals permitted in comtemporary Han are /t k w j r n h ʔ/, with the addition of /l/ in Dawson Han
<rule>Vˀ → V[+low tone]</rule>
</section>

<h2>30.1 Proto-Potou-Akanic-Bantu to Proto-Bantu</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Stewart, John M. (2002), “The potential of proto-Potou-Akanic-Bantu as a pilot Proto-Niger-Congo, and the reconstructions updated”. JALL 23:197 – 224</cite>
; NB: For at least the first batch of sound changes herein, the sound changes applying to those consonants in #U will also apply in U₂ under the following conditions, as reported by Stewart (2002): If V₂ = V₁ (vowel nasality does not necessarily have to be the same, however), changes affecting the vowels will also affect V₂. If C is an approximant, changes involving a nasalized V₁ will also affect C₂ and V₂.
<rule>ʋ̃ → l̃ / #_</rule>
<rule>u ũ → i ı̃ / #R[-labial]_</rule>
<rule>ʋ̃ ɪ̃ → ũ ı̃ / #N[-labial]_</rule>
<rule>i ı̃ V[-round] → uɪ uɪ̃ V[+round] / Cʷ_</rule>
<rule>ʋ̃ → ʋ / #R[-labial]_</rule>
<rule>ʄ C → c R / #_</rule>
<rule>ɠʷ → w / #_V[-nas]</rule>
<rule>ɠʷ → w̃ / #_V[+nas]</rule>
<rule>V[+nas] → V[-nas] / #S[+voiced]_</rule>
<rule>ɪ̃ → ɛ̃ / #(C)V_C</rule>
<rule>ɰ ɰ̃ → j j̃ / #C_</rule>
<rule>ɪ ɪ̃ → i ı̃ / #(C)V[-high]C_</rule>
<rule>ʋ̃ → m / #(C)V_</rule>
<rule>O[+nas -voiced] → O[-nas] / #(C)VC_</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>30.1.1 Pre-Proto-Bantu to Proto-Bantu</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Hedinger, Robert (1987), The Manenguba Languages (Bantu A.15, Mbo Cluster) of Cameroon</cite>
<rule>S[+ lenis] N[+ lenis] → S[- lenis] N[- lenis] / in C₁ position</rule>
<rule>N[+ lenis] N[-lenis] → N[- lenis] ⁿS / in C₂ position</rule>
<rule>d₂ → d / in C₂ position</rule>
</section>
<section>

<rule>i u VS → j w A / _V[+high +ATR]</rule>
<rule>S → Sʰ → Aʰ</rule>
<rule>V[+high +ATR] → V[+high -ATR]</rule>

<rule>t d l → {ʈ,tʲ} {ɖ,dʲ} {ɭ,lʲ,ʎ} (The paper is a bit unclear as to which is meant, as the transcription and the textual aspects of the paper seem to disagree here)</rule>

<rule>l → d / n_</rule>
</section>

<h2>30.1.1.2 Proto-Bantu to Tswana</h2>
<cite>Whimemsz, from Creissels, Dennis (1999), “Remarks on the Sound Corresponences between Proto-Bantu and Tswana (S.31), with Particular Attention to Problems Involving *j (or *y), *j̬ and Sequences *NC”. Bantu Historical Linguistics: Theoretical and Empirical Perspectives, ed. Jean-Marie Hombert and Larry M. Hyman</cite>
<rule>{p,t,tʃ,k} {mp,nt,ɲtʃ,nk} {(m)b,(n)d,(ɲ)dʒ,(n)ɡ} {m,n} → s tsʰ ts ɲ / _iV</rule>
<rule>{tʃ,k} {ntʃ,nk,r} {ɲdʒ,nɡ} mp mb p b {d,l} m n → s tsʰ ts tʃʰ(w) tʃ(w) ʃ(w) dʒ(w) dʒ ŋw ɲ / _{ɪ,e}V</rule>

; “In these cases, the initial vowel of the sequence drops following the consonant change”; Whimemsz doesn’t specify if all V₁V₂ sequences drop the V₁
<rule>mp mb p b m → tʃʰ(w) tʃ(w) ʃ(w) dʒ(w) ŋw / _{ʊ,o}V</rule>
<rule>{tʃ,k} {ɲtʃ,nk} {ndʒ,nɡ} → s tsʰ ts / _{i,ɪ,e}</rule>
<rule>nk k → kʰ h / _u</rule>
<rule>mp nt ɲtʃ nk mp nd ɲdʒ nɡ → pʰ tʰ tɬʰ qʰ p t tɬ k</rule>

<rule>oi ɔi ɔ̃i {a,ɛ}i {ɛ̃,ẽ}i ei → oe ɔɛ ɔ̃ɛ̃ ɛɛ ɛ̃ɛ̃ ee</rule>

<h2>32.1.1 Proto-Chatino to Papabuco Chatino</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Upson, B.W., and Robert E. Longacre (1965), “Proto-Chatino Phonology”. International Journal of American Linguistics 31(4):312 – 322</cite>
<rule>t → ɾ / _{u,ẽ} when unstressed</rule>
<rule>t → ɾ / _”a</rule>
<rule>t → tʃ / _”{e,iʔ}</rule>
<rule>t → ʃ / i_i</rule>
<rule>t → s / ! “in cluster with š” (presumably [ʃ])</rule>
<rule>tʲ → s / _u</rule>
<rule>tʲ → tʃ / else</rule>
<rule>k → ɡ / a_a</rule>
<rule>kʷ → ɾ / #_ek</rule>
<rule>kʷ → b / else</rule>
<rule>ʔ → ∅ (?)</rule>
<rule>{c,tʃ} → ʃ</rule>
<rule>s → tʃ (in certain cases? Not a lot of data available on this one)</rule>
; “under obscure conditions”
<rule>l → {lʲ,n}</rule>
<rule>n → nʲ</rule>
<rule>nʲ → l / #_i</rule>
<rule>h → d / _a (…lolwut)</rule>
<rule>h → t / else (…again, lolwut)</rule>
; “under obscure conditions (PC morphophonemics?)”
<rule>j → nʲ</rule>
<rule>i → e / tʃ_</rule>
<rule>ı̃ → i</rule>
<rule>e → a / _{l,ʔn} when unstressed</rule>
<rule>e → i / {kʲ,nt}_ when stressed</rule>
; “where obscure morphological developments (in the ultimate or penultimate syllable) have resulted in regressive assimilation of vowel quality”
<rule>e → i / in a few data sets</rule>
<rule>ẽ → a / tʲ_</rule>
<rule>ẽ → i / h_</rule>
<rule>ẽ → i / in U[+long -stress]</rule>
<rule>ẽ → e / else</rule>
; “under special conditions”
<rule>a → {i,e}</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>32.1.2 Proto-Chatino to Tataltepec Chatino</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Upson, B.W., and Robert E. Longacre (1965), “Proto-Chatino Phonology”. International Journal of American Linguistics 31(4):312 – 322</cite>
<rule>t → tʲ / _õ (…again…lolwut)</rule>

<cite>Pogostick Man, from Upson, B.W., and Robert E. Longacre (1965), “Proto-Chatino Phonology”. International Journal of American Linguistics 31(4):312 – 322</cite>
; Some consonant disharmony involving reflexes of *k, *kʷ
<rule>tʲ → tj</rule>

<rule>kʷ → kw / else</rule>
<rule>ts → tʃ / #_{a,õ}ʔ</rule>
<rule>ts → tʃ / V[+high]_</rule>
<rule>ts → ʃ / #_ẽ</rule>
<rule>c → ts</rule>
<rule>tʃ → ts / a_</rule>
<rule>s → ʃ / V_V</rule>
<rule>s → ʃ / if /l/ is present in the same syllable</rule>
<rule>s → tʃ / #_a</rule>
<rule>ʃ → s / _{ik,e,ı̃,ẽ}</rule>
<rule>ʃ → ts / _i, in monosyllables</rule>
<rule>lʲ → l / #_ in U[-long -stress]</rule>
<rule>lʲ → lj / else</rule>
<rule>nʲ → j / _ã (with some exceptions?)</rule>
<rule>nʲ → nj / else</rule>
<rule>h → ʔ / _ã</rule>
<rule>ç → hj</rule>
<rule>hʷ → ʍ</rule>
<rule>ẽ → ı̃ / {t,h}_#</rule>
<rule>ẽ → ı̃ / _ʔ#</rule>
; “in one case”
<rule>ẽ → ı̃ / ʔ_#</rule>

<h2>32.2 Proto-Oto-Manguean to Tlapanec</h2>

<rule>{iHn,eHn,aHn} uHn → ã ũ</rule>
<rule>e → i</rule>
<rule>ʔ → ∅ / #_</rule>
<rule>h → ʃ / _C[-voice] (? “both h and š occur before nasals, so it is possible that š has a separate source in Proto Otomanguean”)</rule>

<h2>36.1 Proto-Sino-Tibetan to Middle Chinese</h2>
<cite>Ran &amp; thedukeofnuke, from Handel, Z. (1998), The Medial Systems of Old Chinese and Proto-Sino-Tibetan</cite>
; NB: “P T Ṭ K represent labial, dental, retroflex, and velar obstruents respectively. *r is reconstructed as being an approximant ɹ. . . .PST and OC lacked any initial/medial clusters of the form Tr- and Tl-. . . .The initials and medials for Old Chinese are the same as those for PST. Note that initial consonants separated by a hyphen (e.g., C-r-) are derived from prefixes and are not true consonant clusters.
<rule>(C-)r- → l-</rule>
<rule>s(-)r → ʂ-</rule>
<rule>r → ∅ / C_-</rule>
<rule>r-T- → Ṭ</rule>
<rule>(C-)l- → {d,ji}-</rule>
<rule>s-l- → {tʰ,z}-</rule>
<rule>l → ∅ / s_-</rule>
<rule>{m,ŋ}l- → dʑ-</rule>
<rule>Cl- → T(ʑ)-</rule>
<rule>Kw → Kʷ- / _a-</rule>
<rule>Kwə- → Kʷɨ-</rule>
<rule>w → r → ∅ / P_-</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>36.1.1 Late Middle Chinese to Old Mandarin</h2>
<cite>Ran, from Hsueh, F.S. (1975), Phonology of Old Mandarin</cite>
; NB: Ran says, “The author uses V1, V2, V3, V4, Vn, Vch, and Vta to represent Late Middle Chinese vowels. I am going to very tentatively assign the values of o, a, ia, e,?, a(ch) and a(ta) to these vowels [emphasis added]. These should be taken as orthographical convenience rather than actual speculation.” It should be noted that the vowel represented by &lt;?&gt; could be palatalized. Ran adds, “I am also going to number tones according to their traditional order, i.e. F1, F2, F3, F4, F5. Since tones change rapidly, it is impossible to accurately reconstruct their values; we can only know how many there were, and agree on an order to renumerate them. Middle Chinese starts out with no F2.” For the purposes of this list of sound changes, tones are superscript numbers following vowel markers.
<rule>∅ → w / P_V</rule>
<rule>xɦ → ∅ / _j(w){?,ia,a(ta)}</rule>
<rule>Cʲ → C {Ạ,F̣}_</rule>
<rule>Cʲ → C̣</rule>
<rule>ɳ → ɽ</rule>
<rule>Vⁱ → V² / in syllables with /ɦ/, a nasal, or a liquid</rule>
<rule>V³ → V⁴ / in syllables with /ɦ/</rule>
<rule>? → ∅</rule>
<rule>V⁵ → V² / in syllables with /ɦ/</rule>
<rule>V⁵ → V⁴ / in syllables with a nasal or liquid</rule>
<rule>V⁵ → V³ / else</rule>
<rule>ɦ → h / S_V²</rule>
<rule>ɦ → ∅ / else</rule>
<rule>Cʲ → C / {f,v}_</rule>
<rule>w → o / Cʲ_?w</rule>
<rule>i → ∅ / C(w)_a</rule>
<rule>ia → e / else</rule>
<rule>C → Cʲ / {K,C[+pharyngeal]}_{a,a(ch)}</rule>
<rule>o → a / C_w ! C = {K,C[+pharyngeal]}</rule>
<rule>ŋ → n / n_Cʲa(ta)</rule>
<rule>ŋ → ∅ / #_ ! #_o(w)</rule>
<rule>k → j / {e,a,o}_</rule>
<rule>k → w / V_</rule>
<rule>{o,a} {a(ta),a(ch)} → e a / _ŋ</rule>
<rule>{a(ta),a(ch)} → o / else</rule>
<rule>? → o / _ŋ</rule>
<rule>{Aʲ,Fʲ}[+alveolar] →{A,F} / _?ʲ</rule>
<rule>ʈ → ʈʂ / _r</rule>
<rule>C → Cʲ / ?ʲ{p,t}_</rule>
<rule>{p,t} → ∅ / V_</rule>
<rule>o → a / Cʲ_w</rule>
<rule>∅ → w / C_o# ! C = {K,C[+pharyngeal]}</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / C̣Cʲ_?w (“optional”)</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / w?_</rule>
<rule>? → a / Cw_Cʲ</rule>
<rule>{o,e} → ? / _Cʲ</rule>
<rule>wʲ → w / _?Cʲ</rule>
<rule>∅ → w / C̣_aŋ</rule>
<rule>e → o / w_ŋ</rule>
<rule>wʲ → w / _aŋ</rule>
<rule>wʲ → w / C̣_oŋ</rule>
</section>

<h2>36.2 Sin Sukchu to Guānhuà</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Coblin, W. South (2000), “A Diachronic Study of Míng Guānhuà Phonology”. Monumenta Serica 48:267 – 335</cite>
; Initials:
<rule>b d dz ɡ → {p,pʼ} {t,tʼ} {ts,tsʼ} {k,kʼ}</rule>
<rule>ŋ → ∅ / _{i,j,w,y}</rule>

<rule>w → u / _V</rule>
<rule>v ʋ → f v</rule>
<rule>z → {s,tsʼ} (the former “without exception” “in oblique tone words”)</rule>
; “[i]n oblique tone syllables”
<rule>ɖʐ →ʈʂ /</rule>
; “in píng-tone syllables”
<rule>ɖʐ → ʈʂʼ /</rule>
<rule>ʐ → ʂ</rule>
<rule>r → ʐ (Apparently there was some situation where this went to ∅, and then something happened with the output syllable being [ɚ])</rule>
<rule>ʔ → ŋ / _V[-high]</rule>

<rule>ʔ → ∅ / _u (not always? Perhaps some variation with [ɣ] here?)</rule>
<rule>ɣ → x</rule>
<rule>j → i</rule>
<rule>There seems to ahve been some stuff going on with palatalized [ŋ] → {ɲ,n} but it seems highly dialectal and I’m not entirely sure just what exactly was going on here</rule>
<rule>w → {v,u}</rule>
; Finals:
<rule>uj → (ɣ)u</rule>
<rule>m → n</rule>
<rule>long-tail vowel thing → i (or [truncated vowel-thing] “where GH sibilant initial variants occur”)</rule>
<rule>{long-tail vowel thingʔ,əʔ} → ɛʔ</rule>
<rule>i → [long-tail vowel thing] / C̣_ (“sometimes”, in “variant readings”); when following /ʂ ʐ/, sometimes yields [ʂi], other times [s truncated vowel-thing]</rule>
<rule>iʔ iw → eʔ ew / C̣_ (the latter with variant iɛu?)</rule>
<rule>iʔ → ieʔ (→ i[truncated vowel-thing]ʔ?)</rule>
<rule>uʔ → oʔ (occasionally → uɛʔ?)</rule>
<rule>uj → u(ɛ)i / m_</rule>
<rule>uj → uɛi / {P,C[+guttural],∅}_</rule>
<rule>uj → ui / {C[+dental],C[+sibilant]}_</rule>
<rule>ujʔ → uɛʔ (dialectally → uɔʔ?)</rule>
<rule>un → uɛn / ! {C[+dental],C[+sibilant]}_</rule>
<rule>uɛn → ɛn / ʋ_</rule>
<rule>uŋ → oŋ (in one source?)</rule>
<rule>juŋ → iuŋ / _{∅,x,ʋ,ʔ}</rule>
<rule>juŋ → iuŋ / _ɡ[+píng tone]</rule>
<rule>juŋ → uŋ (→ oŋ dialectally?)</rule>
<rule>jujŋ ujŋ → iuŋ uŋ</rule>
<rule>y → ɥ (→ y~u dialectally?) / C̣_</rule>
<rule>yʔ → yɛʔ / {l,C[+dental +sibilant]}_ in “QYS -juət-type” finals, dialectally?</rule>
; “QYS -k-types”
<rule>yʔ → oʔ (eventually → {ʊʔ,(i)uʔ?) / {l,C[+dental +sibilant]}_, in</rule>
<rule>yʔ → yʔ(~yɛʔ?) / {∅,C[+guttural]}, in -juət-types</rule>
<rule>yʔ → ioʔ (→ iʊʔ dialectally?) / {∅,C[+guttural]}_, in -k-types</rule>
<rule>yʔ → {ɥʔ,yʔ,uʔ} / C̣, in -juət-types</rule>
<rule>yʔ → oʔ (→ ʊʔ?) / C̣_, in -k-types</rule>
<rule>yjʔ → yʔ</rule>
<rule>yn → un / C̣_ (may have stayed yn or → ɥn in at least one area?)</rule>
<rule>je jeʔ yeʔ → ɛ ɛʔ uɛʔ / C̣_</rule>
<rule>je jeʔ → iɛ iɛʔ</rule>
<rule>yeʔ → yɛʔ (→ {uɔʔ,yɔʔ} in southern speech?)</rule>
<rule>ye jej → yɛ i</rule>
<rule>jew → au / C̣(C?)_</rule>
<rule>jew → iau / else</rule>
<rule>{jem,jen} → iɛn / sometimes after C̣_ (but ! ʐ_) (only in one variety?)</rule>
<rule>yen → uɛn / C̣_</rule>
<rule>yen → yɛn</rule>
<rule>ɔ → a / in two cases cited; extremely rare change</rule>
<rule>wɔ → ɔ (occasionally → uɔ after a guttural?)</rule>
<rule>wɔʔ → uɔʔ / C[+guttural]_</rule>
<rule>wɔʔ → ɔʔ / else</rule>
; “[t]his final occurs exclusively after SR gutturals”
<rule>ɔn → an;</rule>
<rule>wɔn ja wa → uɔn ia ua</rule>
<rule>aʔ → ɔʔ / C[+guttural]_</rule>
<rule>jaʔ → iaʔ</rule>
<rule>waʔ → aʔ / C[+labiodental]_</rule>
<rule>waʔ → uaʔ</rule>
<rule>aj jaj waj aw jaw → ai iai uai au iau</rule>
<rule>awʔ → ɔʔ (“Trigault gives a variant in -ɛʔ, which becomes general in the later GH varieties”)</rule>
<rule>{jawʔ,wawʔ} → ɔʔ / C̣_</rule>
<rule>jawʔ wawʔ → iɔʔ uɔʔ</rule>
<rule>am → an</rule>
<rule>{jam,jan} → iɛn</rule>
<rule>wan → an / C[+labiodental]_</rule>
<rule>wan → uan / else</rule>
<rule>aŋ jaŋ → uaŋ aŋ / C̣_</rule>
<rule>waŋ → uaŋ</rule>
<rule>əjʔ əw {əm,ən} → eʔ ɛu ɛn</rule>
; Tones:
<rule>əjŋ → ɛn (varies with ɛn?)</rule>
; qı̄ng píng → yı̄n píng
; zhuó píng → yáng píng
; qı̄ng shăng → shăng
; zhuó shăng → qù
; (There seems to be some conflict between shăng tones and qù tones, the latter noted as being the spoken forms)

<h2>36.3.1.1 Proto-Naish to Laze</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Jacques, Guillaume, and Alexis Michaud (2011), “Approaching the historical phonology of three highly eroded Sino-Tibetan languages: Naxi, Na and Laze”. Diachronica 28:4 (2011), 468 – 498</cite>
<rule>a u i iN → e y ɯ i / T_%</rule>
<rule>a → i / {ŋ,w}_%</rule>
<rule>a → wɤ / {K,ŋ}w_%</rule>
<rule>{a,i} → ɯ / R_%</rule>
<rule>a → ie / ! K_%</rule>
<rule>a aS → ɑ {ɑ,u}</rule>
<rule>iN → æ / {P,C}r_%</rule>
<rule>i → v̩ / m_%</rule>
<rule>u o → v̩ u</rule>
<rule>B → o / {qʰ,(N)q}_</rule>
<rule>V% → low tone</rule>
<rule>{Np,mb} → b / _V (the paper implies similar developments occurred at other POAs)</rule>
<rule>{r,s}p(ʰ) {r,s}{Np,(m)b} → f v / _V (the paper implies similar developments occurred at other POAs)</rule>
<rule>{r,s}k {r,s}Nk → f w / _V</rule>
<rule>S{b,ɡ} Sk → v h / _V</rule>
<rule>{r,s}l {r,s}n → l̥ N̥ / %_V</rule>
<rule>n̥ → l̥ / %_V</rule>
<rule>N̥V → hṼ</rule>
<rule>l̥ → ɬ / %_V</rule>
</section>

<rule>a → e / {R,T}_%</rule>
<rule>a → wɤ / {K,ŋ}w_%</rule>

<h2>37.1 Proto-Siouan-Iroquoian to Proto-Iroquoian</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Chafe, Wallace L. (1964), “Another Look at Siouan and Iroquoian”. American Anthropologist New Series, 66:852 – 862; and from cedh aumdmanh’s Iroquoian changes</cite>
<rule>w → ∅ / _{o,õ,ı̃}</rule>

<h2>37.2.1 Proto-Siouan to Catawba</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wolff, Hans (1950), “Comparative Siouan I”. International Journal of American Linguistics 16(4):168 – 178; and cedh audmanh’s changes above, which assisted me in deciphering the vintage phonetic transcription scheme</cite>
; NB: Does not include developments in unstressed non-nasal vowels. L was apparently either /ɹ/ or /l/; Lʲ (Lʸ in the text) was apparently /j/, or maybe /ʎ/. Also, the changes of /p/ before a consonant are unclear, as described within the text. Changes appended with an asterisk are putative; there was a seeming lack of material for this language, so I’ve attempted to do some tracking work from the examples given in the text.
<rule>ç x → x ʃ</rule>
<rule>p → {p,b,m,w} / _C</rule>
<rule>p → b / V_V (*)</rule>
<rule>tʲ → ʃ / in ”U</rule>
<rule>tʲ → ʒ / else</rule>
<rule>t → ∅ / _k, when medial</rule>
<rule>”V[+nas] → Vn (*)</rule>
<rule>∅ → ʔ / C_# (only sometimes?) (*)</rule>
</section>

International Journal of American Linguistics 16(2):61 – 66; Wolff, Hans (1950), “Comparative Siouan II” International Journal of American Linguistics 16(3):113 – 121; Wolff, Hans (1950), “Comparative Siouan III”. International Journal of American Linguistics 16(4):168 – 178; and cedh audmanh’s changes above, which assisted me in deciphering the vintage phonetic transcription scheme</cite>
; NB: Does not include developments in unstressed non-nasal vowels. L was apparently either /ɹ/ or /l/; Lʲ (Lʸ in the text) was apparently /j/, or maybe /ʎ/. Also, the changes of /p/ before a consonant are unclear, as described within the text.
<rule>p → {p,b,m,w} / _C</rule>
<rule>p → w / V_V, apparently as a result of some dissimilation, as this appears to be an allophone of /p/ here, IIUC</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / #_ (sporadic)</rule>
<rule>m → w / #_</rule>
<rule>L → d</rule>
<rule>ã → an (sporadic)</rule>
<rule>ʔ → ∅ / C_</rule>
<rule>r → {d,n}</rule>
<rule>m → ∅ / #_n</rule>
<rule>k → ∅ / #t_</rule>
<rule>k → ∅ / ”Vs_</rule>
<rule>k → ∅ / _x”V</rule>
<rule>kx → xk / ”V_</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>37.2.7.2 Proto-Ohio-Valley to Ofo</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wolff, Hans (1950), “Comparative Siouan I”. International Journal of American Linguistics 16(2):61 – 66; Wolff, Hans (1950), “Comparative Siouan II” International Journal of American Linguistics 16(3):113 – 121; Wolff, Hans (1950), “Comparative Siouan III”. International Journal of American Linguistics 16(4):168 – 178; and cedh audmanh’s changes above, which assisted me in deciphering the vintage phonetic transcription scheme</cite>
; NB: Does not include developments in unstressed non-nasal vowels. L was apparently either /ɹ/ or /l/; Lʲ (Lʸ in the text) was apparently /j/, or maybe /ʎ/. Also, the changes of /p/ before a consonant are unclear, as described within the text.
<rule>p → {p,b,m,w} / _C</rule>
<rule>s {ç,x} → f s</rule>
<rule>w → ∅ / #_ (sporadic)</rule>
<rule>m → w / #_</rule>
<rule>L j → t tʃ</rule>
<rule>ã → {an,õ}</rule>
<rule>ʔ → ∅ / C_</rule>
<rule>sr kr → ft kV₀lV₀</rule>
<rule>m → ∅ / #_n</rule>
<rule>∅ → V / k_m</rule>
<rule>k → ∅ / #t_</rule>
<rule>sk → f / ”V_</rule>
<rule>x → s / _k</rule>
<rule>kx → sk / ”V_</rule>
<rule>kx → s</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>37.2.7.3 Proto-Ohio-Valley to Tutelo</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wolff, Hans (1950), “Comparative Siouan I”. International Journal of American Linguistics 16(2):61 – 66; Wolff, Hans (1950), “Comparative Siouan II” International Journal of American Linguistics 16(3):113 – 121; Wolff, Hans (1950), “Comparative Siouan III”. International Journal of American Linguistics 16(4):168 – 178; and cedh audmanh’s changes above, which assisted me in deciphering the vintage phonetic transcription scheme</cite>
; NB: Does not include developments in unstressed non-nasal vowels. L was apparently either /ɹ/ or /l/; Lʲ (Lʸ in the text) was apparently /j/, or maybe /ʎ/. Also, the changes of /p/ before a consonant are unclear, as described within the text. Changes appended with an asterisk are putative; there was a seeming lack of material for this language, so I’ve attempted to do some tracking work from the examples given in the text.
<rule>p → {p,b,m,w} / _C</rule>
<rule>L → l</rule>
<rule>k → ᵑk / _ʔ</rule>
<rule>ʔ → ∅ / C_</rule>
<rule>mn → mV₀nV₀ / #_ (in morphemes)</rule>
<rule>sn → sV₀nV₀</rule>
<rule>∅ → V / k_m</rule>
<rule>s → ʃ / ”V_k</rule>
</section>

<rule>w → ∅ / m_</rule>
<rule>{f,v} {m,b} → pʰ b</rule>
<rule>pl b{l,r} → v pj</rule>
<rule>{l,r} → ∅ / {d,ɗ}_</rule>
<rule>d ɗ → t d</rule>
<rule>ŋ̊ → h</rule>

<rule>ɣ → kʰ</rule>
<rule>ɡl → k(w)</rule>
<rule>x → ∅ / _r</rule>

<cite>Pogostick Man, from Li, Fang Kuei (1977). “A Handbook of Comparative Tai”. Oceanic Linguistics Special Publications (15), i – 389</cite>
<rule>ɓ ɗ → b d / ! _{l,r}</rule>
<rule>{l,r} → ∅ / n,ɗ_</rule>
<rule>f → pʰ</rule>
<rule>{m,w} → v</rule>
<rule>l → ∅ / p_{ɯ,e,i}</rule>

<rule>xʷ ɣʷ → kʷʰ {v,w}</rule>
<rule>V → Vː / _%</rule>
<rule>iə → ı̆ / _C%</rule>
<rule>ɨ̯a → aː</rule>
<rule>ɛi ei → ai iː</rule>
</section>

<h2>39.1 Proto-Tanoan to Jimez</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2012), “Tanoan languages”. Mémoires de la Société Finno-Ougrienne 253. &lt;http://www.sgr.fi/sust/sust253/sust253_janhunen.pdf&gt;</cite>
<rule>ŋ → k / _#, in latives</rule>
<rule>ŋ → n / _# else</rule>
<rule>iw ow → y uː / _(C) (*)</rule>
<rule>x → @ / _C</rule>
<rule>m → n / _{t,tsʲ,#}</rule>
<rule>ɤ(ː?) ɑː æː → ɑ(ː?) oː eː / stressed</rule>
<rule>a → æ / {a,e,ê,i,y}(X)(C)(C)_, when unstressed</rule>
<rule>Aw → o / unstressed (possibly analogical)</rule>
<rule>i → e / _C, when unstressed</rule>
<rule>iw → u / unstressed</rule>

<rule>w → ʋ</rule>

<h2>42.1.1.1 Proto-Finnic to Proto-Finnish</h2>
<cite>Tropylium, from Hakulinen, Lauri (1979), “Suomen kielen rakenne ja kehitys”. Otava; Häkkinen, Kaisa (2004), “Nykysuomen etymologinen sanakirja”. Mémoires de la Société Finno-Ougrienne 253. &lt;http://www.sgr.fi/sust/sust253/sust253_janhunen.pdf&gt;</cite>
<rule>j w → i U / V_{C,#}</rule>
<rule>i → ∅ / {VC,ks}_ at the ends of a suffix</rule>
<rule>e → ∅ / C[+coronal]_%</rule>
<rule>pː tː tsː kː → pˑ tˑ tsˑ kˑ</rule>
<rule>p t ts s k → b d s z ɡ</rule>
<rule>b d ɡ → β ð ɣ / ! N_</rule>
<rule>ɣ → j~ʋ</rule>
<rule>βi → U / _#</rule>
<rule>f → ʋ / #_</rule>
<rule>ʋ → ∅ / #_{o,u,y}</rule>

<rule>∅ → ʋ / #_{yː,øː,oː}</rule>

<rule>Vː → V[-long] / _i</rule>
<rule>{kt,pt} {kts,pts} → tː tːs</rule>
<rule>xk → kː (even across word boundaries)</rule>
<rule>(t(ː))sn kx(tx) rn ln → sː xː rː lː</rule>
<rule>{p,t,k}({p,t,k})n {p,t,k}({p,t,k})m → nː mː</rule>
<rule>{p,t,k} → ∅ / _st  </rule>
<rule>{ks,nts} nt → s t / _#</rule>
<rule>ts tsˑ tsː → s θˑ θː</rule>
<rule>{z,x(ː)} → h</rule>
<rule>e → @ / h_ (suffixal)</rule>
<rule>{p,k} → h / _t</rule>
</section>

<h2>42.1.1.1.1 Proto-Finnish to Standard Finnish</h2>
<cite>Tropylium, from Hakulinen, Lauri (1979), “Suomen kielen rakenne ja kehitys”. Otava; Häkkinen, Kaisa (2004), “Nykysuomen etymologinen sanakirja”. Mémoires de la Société Finno-Ougrienne 253. &lt;http://www.sgr.fi/sust/sust253/sust253_janhunen.pdf&gt;</cite>
<rule>mb nd ŋɡ → mː nː ŋː</rule>
<rule>eː øː oː → ie yø uo</rule>
<rule>pˑ tˑ θˑ kˑ → p t θ k</rule>
<rule>j → i / C_, when initial in a suffix</rule>
<rule>Vh → hV / {j,v,n,r,l}_# (also some double-metathesis triggered by the condition of being m_ ?)</rule>
<rule>sn → ns</rule>
<rule>V → Vː / h_hC</rule>
<rule>V → Vː / _hC (sporadic)</rule>
<rule>{k,x} → ˟ / _#</rule>
<rule>t → ∅ / s_r</rule>
<rule>p → β → U / _R</rule>
<rule>t → z → U / _r{A,O}</rule>
<rule>t → z → @ / _r{i,e}</rule>
<rule>k → z → @ / _j</rule>
<rule>k → ɣ i / {i,e}_R{i,e}</rule>
<rule>k → ɣ → U / {A,O,U}_R ! R = j</rule>
<rule>β → ʋ</rule>
<rule>ʋ → ∅ / _UC</rule>
<rule>ið → j / ”V_V</rule>
<rule>lð rð → lː rː</rule>
<rule>ð → ∅ / ! ”V(X)_</rule>
<rule>ɣ → j / C_e</rule>
