<rule>ɣ → ∅ / else</rule>
<rule>h → ∅ / V[-stress](X)_V</rule>
<rule>AO → {A,O,U}ː / unstressed</rule>
<rule>e → i / A_, when unstressed</rule>
<rule>Ue → eː / unstressed</rule>
<rule>VU → Vː / _#</rule>
<rule>iU OU → Uː Oː</rule>
<rule>æ → a / e(C…)_(C…)o</rule>
<rule>e → ø / #(C…)_y</rule>
<rule>i → y / #(C…)l_y</rule>
<rule>i → y / #(C…)_væ</rule>
<rule>θ(ː) ð → ts d (this latter does have some highly sporadic exceptions; additionally, in some dialects these may become {t(ː),h~t} and {r,∅}, respectively)</rule>
</section>

<h2>42.1.2 Proto-Finnic to Livonian</h2>
<cite>Tropylium, from Hakulinen, Lauri (1979), “Suomen kielen rakenne ja kehitys”. Otava; Häkkinen, Kaisa (2004), “Nykysuomen etymologinen sanakirja”. <rule>t ts s(C) n l r → tʲ tsʲ sʲ(C) nʲ lʲ rʲ / _i</rule>

<rule>e → ɤ / _C(C){a,o,u}</rule>
<rule>æ → ɑ / unstressed</rule>
<rule>h → ∅ / {#,C}_</rule>
<rule>Vn → Vː / _s</rule>
<rule>ɑ æ → æe / _(C…)i</rule>
<rule>V → ə / unstressed ! V = ɑ</rule>
<rule>ɑ → ə / VC(C)ɑC(C)_# when unstressed</rule>
<rule>Vh → Vːɦ / _C, except maybe ! _j and/or _ʋ</rule>
<rule>LV → VL / {#,V,O}_</rule>
<rule>p t(ʲ) s(ʲ) k → b d(ʲ) z(ʲ) ɡ / ! #_ or adjacent to C[-voice]</rule>
<rule>∅ → ʔ / (C)V_CV</rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / _#</rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / VC_CV</rule>
<rule>C → Cː / ʔ_V</rule>
<rule>dj lj rj ɡj → dʲ lːʲ rːʲ jɡ</rule>
<rule>ʋ → ∅ / {d,z}_</rule>
<rule>lʋ rʋ jʋ → lː rː jː</rule>
<rule>ʋ → ∅ / C_</rule>
<rule>VCːɑ → VːCɑ</rule>
<rule>Cː → C[- long] / ! in verbal forms when V_ə</rule>
<rule>ɑː au → ɔː ɔu (though sometimes ɑː develops, apparently at least partially due to metathesis?)</rule>
<rule>eː øː oː ɤ(ː) → iːe yːø uːo ɨ(ː)</rule>
<rule>ɦ → ʔ</rule>
<rule>sʲ tsʲ zʲ dzʲ → ʃʲ tʃʲ ʒʲ dʒʲ</rule>
<rule>æy ey → æu eu</rule>
<rule>y ø → i e / else</rule>
<rule>V → Vː / _RC(C)ɑ (includes diphthongs)</rule>
<rule>a → aː / VC_</rule>
<rule>e o → eː oː / _Cɑ</rule>
<rule>o → oː / _{RC#,i}</rule>
<rule>eː oː → je wo</rule>
<rule>w → ʋ / #_o</rule>
<rule>wo → ʊ / P_</rule>
<rule>ɔ(ː) → o(ː)</rule>
</section>

<cite>Radius Solis, from from Voegelin, Charles F., Florence M. Voegelin, &amp; Kenneth L. Hale (1962), “Typological and Comparative Grammar of Uto-Aztecan: I (Phonology)”. International Journal of American Linguistics 28: Memoir 17</cite>

; “What happened to PUA */r/ in O’odham is difficult to say. There are only two known cognates, each showing a different reflex: /ɭ/ and /ɖ/”
; “in all dialects, and varies by dialect after other consonants”
<rule>l → i / {p,m,k(ʷ),w}_#</rule>

<rule>r[+ fortis] → ɾ / _#</rule>
; Fortis *L is of uncertain outcome, being written as 〈l〉 or 〈ll〉
<rule>r[+ fortis] → r</rule>
; There seem to have been a few (variant?) forms which possibly show height assimilation in vowels
<rule>ɡ → k / #_ (sometimes?)</rule>

Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Navarro-Lapurdian_dialect&amp;oldid=601150726&gt;; and Campbell, Lyle, “Language Isolates and Their History, or, What’s Weird, Anyway?”. &lt;http://www2.hawaii.edu/~lylecamp/CAMPBELL%20BLS%20isolates.pdf&gt;</cite>
; Pre-Proto-Basque may have had some stuff involving reduplication that ended up dropping the first consonant
; fortis → aspirated / “in a prominent position” (i.e., word-initially?)
; fortis → [- voice] / else
; lenis → devoiced / “in a prominent word-initial position”
; lenis → voiced fricative (→ approximant, at least by the 12th Century?) / unstressed
; lenis (voiced) → fricative / {l,r,s̺,s̻,V}_{l,r,s̺,s̻,V}
; — At least one reconstruction seems to indicate *s and *ś, which may have been an affricate and /s/. Pretty reliably, *-s tends to turn into -ts̺, and *-ś → -ts̻, probably after the below-mentioned affrication. Beyond that, it’s messy. *-tso seems to have become -tʃo/-tʃu, though.
<rule>Sʰ → F → h (→ ∅) / #_</rule>
; “[T]wo similar vocalic segments” usually contract, though some dialects (especially Biscayan) seem not to exhibit this
<rule>S → S[+ voiced] / #_</rule>
<rule>Vn → Ṽ / _# (seems to have been reverted in most dialects, except for Souletin)</rule>
<rule>Vn → Ṽ / _V (?)</rule>
<rule>V → Ṽ / _N (Souletin, perhaps in other dialects?)</rule>
<rule>d → l / #_ (except verbs)</rule>
<rule>n →m / u_V</rule>
<rule>n → ɲ / {i,ɪ}_V</rule>
<rule>n → h̃ / V_V</rule>
<rule>nb → mː → m</rule>
<rule>N → [+ same POA] / _C</rule>
<rule>b → m / _VN</rule>
<rule>{ɾ,r} → ∅ / #_</rule>
<rule>l → ɾ / V_V</rule>
<rule>ɾ → r / _C</rule>
<rule>r → ɾ / _#</rule>
<rule>C → ∅ / r_</rule>
<rule>Cr → Cɾ → CVɾ (perhaps not a sound change per se, just a historical tendency)</rule>
<rule>ɾ → ∅ / V_V (Souletin)</rule>
<rule>*L (fortis) → l (or *lh → lː, which then lost gemination?)</rule>
<rule>ð → some sort of tap distinct from ɾ (Biscayan, Guipuscoan, High Navarrese)</rule>
<rule>b → ∅ / #_B (a few exceptions, mostly before _u)</rule>
<rule>F[+ voiced] → ∅ / V_V (sometimes, usually involving “compound surnames”?)</rule>
<rule>S[+ voice] → S[- voice] / F[+ sibilant]_</rule>

<rule>h̃ → h (not Souletin)</rule>

<rule>u ũ → y ỹ / _{s̻,ts̺,ts̻} (but not _s̺) (Souletin)</rule>
<rule>õ → ũ (Souletin)</rule>
<rule>∅ → a / #_{ra,ro} (sporadic)</rule>
<rule>∅ → e / #_r</rule>
<rule>∅ → e / #_{s̺,s̻}C</rule>
<rule>i → u / _(C…)u (Roncalese)</rule>
<rule>i → y / _(C…)y (Souletin)</rule>
<rule>e → o / _(C…)o (eastern dialects, Bermeo Biscayan)</rule>
<rule>e → o / o(C…)_ (eastern dialects)</rule>
<rule>a o e → ɛ u i / {i,u}(C…)_ (this [ɛ] is tentatively marked as such; Egurtzegi transcribes it as /e/ but says it’s not as close as /e/)</rule>
<rule>o → u / _n{C,#} (Souletin; some raising occurred elsewhere)</rule>
<rule>a → e / _$a (Biscayan, Alavese, some Guipuscoan)</rule>
<rule>o e → u i / _$a (raising of *o is less common)</rule>
<rule>e → i / _{n,C[+ sibilant]} (sporadic)</rule>
<rule>e → a / {V,C}_r (“mainly in the western dialects”)</rule>
; “[S]ome variations between /a/ and /e/ or /e/ and /i/” / _l{C,#}
<rule>u i → o e / _r{C,#}</rule>
<rule>∅ → j / V_{N,s̺,s̻}S</rule>
<rule>∅ → j / u_V (eastern dialects)</rule>
<rule>{w,y} → ∅ / _ja</rule>
<rule>∅ → m / o$_V (Orozko Biscayan)</rule>
<rule>∅ → V / Vk_# (Zeberio Biscayan)</rule>
<rule>e → ∅ / #_ (Navarrese, rare)</rule>
<rule>e → j / #_V (at least a few times?)</rule>
<rule>a → ∅ / _V</rule>
<rule>V → ∅ / Vj_</rule>
<rule>h → ∅ (western dialects)</rule>
; *h may have metathesized given that it’s only found in the first two syllables of proto-forms
<rule>{w,β}h → f</rule>
<rule>h…h → ∅…h (“affect[s] both the oral /h/ and the nasalized aspiration”)</rule>
<rule>*-ɾ → -h stuff in compounds</rule>
<rule>l n →ʎ ɲ / E_</rule>
<rule>{ɾ,r} → ʎ / {i,j}_ (eastern dialects)</rule>
<rule>s̻ ts̻ → ʃ tʃ / {E,j}_ (mostly Biscayan)</rule>
<rule>t → c / {E,j}_ (“some areas”)</rule>
<rule>t → tʃ / {E,j}_ (partially spread amongst Biscayan and Guipuscoan)</rule>
<rule>d ð → ɟ ʝ / {E,j}_ ? (“some dialects”)</rule>
<rule>d ð → ɟ ʝ / {ʎ,ɲ}_ (Guipuscoan, High Navarrese)</rule>
<rule>{ɡ,ɣ} → {ɟ,ʝ} / {E,j}_</rule>
<rule>ɡ → dʒ / {E,j}_ (“in some Biscayan areas”)</rule>
<rule>j → ʝ → j (northern High Navarrese, most Labourd, some Biscayan)</rule>
<rule>j → ʝ → ʒ (Souletin, sporadic in northwestern Biscayan)</rule>
<rule>j → ʝ (some Biscayan and Navarrese)</rule>
<rule>j → ʝ → ɟ (typical of Low Navarrese)</rule>
<rule>j → ʝ → ʒ → dʒ (northwestern Biscayan)</rule>
<rule>j → ʝ → ʒ → ʃ (Aescoan, Salazarese, Roncalese, most southern High Navarrese)</rule>
<rule>j → ʝ → ʒ → ʃ → x (Guipuscoan, northwestern High Navarrese, eastern Biscayan)</rule>
<rule>j → χ (probably through intermediates like above, Wikipedia doesn’t go into particulars of how and where)</rule>
; Vowel syncope:
<rule>ʎ ɲ → jl jn (“common in Low Navarrese, Labourdin, and is even regular in the High Navarrese of Sakana”)</rule>
<rule>— V → ∅ / S_{ɾ,l} (more common in Roncalese and Salazarese, but also in Navarrese and Aescoan?)</rule>
<rule>— V → ∅ / C[+ sibilant]_ɾ (Roncalese and Salazarese)</rule>
<rule>— V → ∅ / {O,ɾ,r}_O (Roncalese, Salazarese, Navarrese, Aescoan)</rule>
; Something about final devoicing of stops and initial stops losing voicing as a result of vowel deletion
<rule>n[+ fortis] → n</rule>
<rule>e → ∅ / #Ur_</rule>
<rule>a → ∅ / V_# (Guipuscoan; happens because of reanalysis of the definite article)</rule>
<rule>V → ∅ / _#, in trisyllables</rule>
<rule>i → ∅ / _#, in disyllables</rule>
<rule>u {o,e} → ∅ a / _#, in disyllables (eastern dialects)</rule>
; “-a or -e from the definite article” is dropped Markina Biscayan and Getxo Biscayan
; Some vowel metathesis only when vowels are matched in height
; Something about diphthongs occurring where intervocalic /n/ was lost
<rule>hu hi → ʊ ɪ / {o,e}_ (also happened with /a/ sometimes, but usually such sequences just dropped one vowel)</rule>
; Glide dissimilation if the homorganic vowel was in the following syllable, but usually the glide just deleted
<rule>VɪC → VCʲ</rule>
<rule>aɪ → eɪ → e (rare)</rule>
<rule>aʊ → aɪ / !_{ɾ,r,s̺,s̻} (Souletin, Roncalese)</rule>
<rule>eʊ → {e,eɡu}</rule>
<rule>eɪ → e / #_</rule>
<rule>oɪ → uɪ (rare)</rule>
<rule>eð (→ e ?) → j / #_V</rule>
<rule>e → j / #_a</rule>
<rule>e → ∅ / #_e</rule>
; “especially after a velar stop”
<rule>ʊa → o</rule>
<rule>ʊe → e</rule>
<rule>C[- voice] → C[+ voice] / {l,N}_ (not Roncalese or Souletin)</rule>
; Some speakers (Labourd and Low Navarrese?) have ʀ for r, and a few have ɢ̆ for ɾ
; l gets a velar(ized?) articulation in Souletin (possibly only in the coda?)
; Souletin preserves something involving historical aspiration in pretonic position, apparently
; Souletin keeps initial ʃ- and tʃ- distinct; Labourdin only has ʃ-, and the rest apparently only have tʃ-?
<rule>C[+ sibilant] → C[+ affricate] / _#</rule>
<rule>s̻ ts̺ → s̺ ts̻ (Biscayan, partially in Guipuscoan, Donostia, San Sebastián, though these latter two may be varieties of Guipuscoan)</rule>
; From the Wikipedia article on Biscayan: “Convergence of sibilants: z, x and s &gt; x, s and tz, tx and ts &gt; tz.” I’m not sure what this means. 〈s z〉 are apparently s̻ s̺, and 〈x〉 is ʃ.
<rule>s̻ → s̺ / _{C,#} (sometimes)</rule>
<rule>its̺ → tʃ / _# (Biscayan)</rule>
; Beterri Guipuscoan has VjV# where Biscayan has VɲV# and regular Basque has VV#
<rule>oa ea → u(e) i(e) / _#</rule>
<rule>s̺ → tʃ / #_ (Guipuscoan)</rule>
<rule>s̺ → ʃ̺ “for most French Basque speakers (Trask 1997:84), due to French influence” according to Campbell</rule>
; Accentual changes:
; — Navarrese and Labourdin seem to have gotten rid of phonemic accent; High Navarrese typically stresses the penult, while Low Navarrese and Labourdin are claimed to lack stress on the word level.
; — Guipuscona, southeastern Biscayan, and western varieties of Navarrese stress the second syllable (unless it is a disyllable, in which case the first syllable gets the accent, though a few varieties don’t do this).
; — North Biscayan does something with roots and affixes marked for prosody; ”[m]ost native roots and almost all singular affixes are unaccented”; loans, ”compounds and plural affixes” tend to be accented. Stress is typically assigned to the syllable immediately before the accent, but a few areas accent the penult or the antepenult.
; — Souletin does its own thing with accent. Stress usually falls on the penult, but contractions can mess with this (one of the examples given in the paper is “alhába ‘daughter’ + abs. sg. -a &gt; alhabá ‘the daughter”’). Something similar is posited for “older… Salazarese”. Roncalese was much the same, but the stress was stem-oriented as opposed to word-oriented unless contraction occurred, and there’s some stuff about proparoxytones that Souletin didn’t have.
</section>

<h2>46.5 Old English-to-Scots Vowel Shifts</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Phonological history of Scots”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Phonological_history_of_Scots&amp;oldid=582962563&gt;; and Wikipedia contributors (2014), “Scottish Vowel Length Rule”. Wikipedia, the Free Encyclopedia. &lt;https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Scottish_vowel_length_rule&amp;oldid=589349104&gt;</cite>
<rule>ai → ɛi → əi / when stem-final</rule>
<rule>uː → ʌu / when-stem final, in northern varieties</rule>
<rule>øː → wi / {k,ɡ}_ (in Mid Northern dialects)</rule>
<rule>øː → i (in northern dialects)</rule>
<rule>øː → (j){u,ʌ} / _{k,x} (outcome varies depending upon dialect)</rule>
<rule>a → i / _n (in northern varieties)</rule>
<rule>a → e / _n (otherwise)</rule>
<rule>a → {ɛ,e} / _rC</rule>
<rule>ai oi ui ei au ou iu ɛ(o)u → eː oe əi iː {ɑː,ɔː} ʌu ju j(ʌ)u</rule>
<rule>ɛː → ɛi (→ əi?) / in some northern varieties</rule>
<rule>iː eː ɛː aː oː uː {øː,yː} → əi i {i,e} e o u ø</rule>
<rule>æ → ɛ / _C[+alveolar]</rule>
; Application of the Scottish vowel-length rule:
<rule>a ɔ u → {a,ɑ} ɔ ʌ</rule>
<rule>— V → Vː / _{r,F[+voiced],$,#}</rule>
<rule>— əi → aɪ / _{r,F[+voiced],$,#} (pursuant to the above)</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>46.6 Great Ngamo Tone Shift</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Schuh, Russel (2005), “The Great Ngamo Tone Shift”</cite>
; In the Gudi dialect, the tone on a given domain (which can be more than one syllable/mora, as long as said syllables/morae are consecutive and share the same tone) shift to the following domain, with a low tone cropping up on the first domain. The original tone of the word-final domain floats or tacks itself onto the next domain, depending upon the surrounding conditions. When utterance-final, these tones remain on that domain. This can cause a falling tone, but not a rising tone, which Ngamo does not permit; where such would occur, tone goes to high.
</section>
<section>

<rule>ɛː → eː → iː </rule>
<rule>aː ɔː → ɛː oː → eː ow → ej (əw)</rule>

<rule>eː øː oː → ie yø uo</rule>

<rule>ɑː æː ɑi æi → uɑ iæ ɑe æe</rule>

<h2>46.11 Middle Chinese to Cantonese Vowel Shift (“The Inner-Outer Flip”)</h2>
<cite>LoneWolf, from Newman, J. (1983). Cahiers de Linguistique Asie Orentale XII.1:65 – 79.</cite>
; Relevant changes occurring before the shift:
<rule>a → ɔ / _{ŋ,k} </rule>
<rule>uə yə → ɔ œ / _{n,t} </rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / i_{ŋ,k} </rule>
<rule>∅ → ə / C[+ labiovelar]_i </rule>
<rule>∅ → ə / _u </rule>
; The actual vowel shift:
<rule>V → Vː / _#</rule>
<rule>ə → a </rule>
<rule>a → {aː,ə} “(the environments for these respective changes are somewhat unclear)” </rule>
<rule>ə → a / _{i,u} </rule>
<rule>iəu → au </rule>
<rule>a → aː / _{i,u} </rule>
; Other relevant changes occurring at the same time:
<rule>a → ə → ∅ / i_u</rule>
<rule>ə → ∅ / W_ </rule>
; (Apparently, /i u/ either were or became glides in the appropriate positions)
<rule>W → ∅ / C_ </rule>
</section>

<section>
<h2>46.13 Old Norse to Faroese Vowel Shift</h2>
<cite>johanpeturdam</cite>
; NB: “The reflexes of the vowels are given in the order of their reflex when stressed/long, and unstressed/short”
<rule>{a,æː} → ɛa / a</rule>
<rule>aː → ɔa “(except NE of the Faroes → aː)” / ɔ</rule>
<rule>e → eː / ɛ</rule>
; “(except Suðuroy → ɛ)”
<rule>eː → ɛa “(except Suðuroy → eː)” 	/ a</rule>
<rule>{i,y} → iː / ɪ</rule>
<rule>{iː,yː} → ʊi / ʊ(i)</rule>
<rule>o → oː / ɔ</rule>
; “(except Suðuroy → ɔ)”
<rule>oː → {ɔu,ɛu,œu} / œ</rule>
<rule>u → uː / short: ʊ / unstressed: {o,ɔ}</rule>
<rule>uː → ʉu / ʏ</rule>
; “(except Suðuroy → ʏ)”
<rule>{œ,ɔ} → øː/œ</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>46.14 Pre-Slavic Vowel Changes</h2>
<cite>Macska</cite>
; “PIE *a and *o with their variants have merged in the Balto-Slavic period; below they’re written both as *a.”
<rule>eː → æ</rule>
<rule>en an → ẽ ã</rule>
<rule>ej → i</rule>
<rule>ew → ju</rule>
; “in modern languages”
<rule>i → ı̆ [ə?] → {e,a} (strong)/∅ (weak)</rule>
<rule>iː → i</rule>
<rule>a aː → o a</rule>
; “(reduced)”
<rule>aj → {æ₂,i₂}</rule>
<rule>aw → u</rule>
; “in modern languages”
<rule>u → ŭ [ɤ?] → {e,o,ɤ,a} (strong)/∅ (weak)</rule>
<rule>uː → ɨ</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>46.15 Proto-Japanese to Old Japanese Vowel Shift</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Frellesvig, Bjarke and John Witman (2005), “The Japanese-Korean vowel correspondences”</cite>
<rule>e o → je wo / _# </rule>
<rule>e o → i u / else </rule>
<rule>{ɨ,ə} → o </rule>
<rule>{u,ɨ}i {,a,i {ɨi,i{a,ə}} u{ɨ,a,ə} → wi e je wo</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>46.16 Development of Proto-Lolo-Burmese -i(C)# and -u(C)# to Lahu</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Jacques, Guillaume, and Alexis Michaud (2011), “Approaching the historical phonology of three highly eroded Sino-Tibetan languages: Naxi, Na and Laze”. Diachronica 28:4 (2011), 468 – 498; citing Matisoff 2003:186, 248 – 249, 314</cite>
<rule>-i -i{p,k} -it -i{m,ŋ} -in → -i -ɨʔ -iʔ -ɛ -ɨ</rule>
<rule>-u -up -ut -uk -um -un -uŋ → -u -ɔʔ -əʔ -uʔ -ɔ -ə -ɛ</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>46.17 Proto-Maidun to Nisenian Vowel Shift</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Ultan, Russell (1964), “Proto-Maidun Phonology”. International Journal of American Linguistics, Vol. 30, No. 4 (Oct., 1964), 355 – 370.</cite>
<rule>u i e a → y e a o</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>46.18 South African Chain Shift (English)</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Mesthrie, Rajend (2002), Language in South Africa</cite>
; NB: The author gives ı̆ as the shifted vowel but calls it “centralized”; based on this description, I’m calling it /ɨ/.
<rule>æ ɛ ɪ → ɛ e ɨ</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>46.19 Southern [United States] Shift (English)</h2>
<cite>Pogostick Man, from Wikipedia contributors (2012), “Southern American English”.</cite>
; “Lax and tense vowels often neutralize before /l/” 
<rule>ɛ → ɪ / _N </rule>
<rule>aɪ → aː / _# </rule>
<rule>aɪ → aː / _C[+ voiced] </rule>
<rule>aɪ → ɑeː / else (only for some speakers) </rule>
<rule>aɪ → aː / else (only for some speakers) </rule>
<rule>æ ɛ ɪ → æj(ə) ɛj(ə) ɪj(ə) </rule>
<rule>ɛj(ə) ɪj(ə) i eɪ → ej(ə) ij(ə) ɪi ɛi </rule>
<rule>uʊ oʊ → ʉʊ̈ əʊ̈ (a bit of a guesstimate based upon the prose description in the article and the mean-formant-value chart cited from Labov, Ash &amp; Bobert (2006))</rule>
<rule>ɔ → ɑɒ (for some speakers) </rule>
<rule>ɑɹ → ɒɹ (“often”) </rule>
<rule>z → d / _n (not strictly a vowel shift but included here anyway because it’s cool, and also because it doesn’t occur in 〈hasn’t〉 because of the influence of 〈hadn’t〉) </rule>
; Stress reassignment to the initial syllable (again, not strictly a vowel shift) 
; Merger of ɔɹ and ɑɹ (“in some regions”) 
; Loss of distinction between ɪɹ and ɪəɹ, and between ʊəɹ and ɔɹ 
; Pronunciation of the 〈l〉 in words like 〈walk〉 and 〈talk〉 (again, not really a vowel development)
<rule>æ/ɑː → æɪ</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>47 Most-Wanted Sound Changes</h2>
; This section replicates the “Most wanted sound changes” article from Knee Quickie. It is presented mostly as it was found with the following modifications:<ul>
<li>The formatting rules are not adhered to any longer due to the format, and the preamble (and table of contents) are omitted.
</li><li>Some liberty has been taken with the presentation of the *f → p change in Afro-Asiatic.
</li><li>Bullets are no longer present.
</li><li>Some corrections have been made (e.g., to the conditioning of the Ōkami change of *t → k).
</li><li>Instead of footnotes, sources (where cited) are listed next to the relevant entries.
</li><li>Some URLs have been shortened using http://is.gd due to potential conflict with the way LaTeX handles the 〈%〉 character.
</li><li>Subsection 4 is specifically listed as being “empty as of yet” for aesthetic purposes.
</li><li>Wikipedia URLs have been changed to <code>https://</code>.
</li></ul>
</section>
<section>
<h2>47.1 List 1: Simple Consonant Changes</h2>
<rule>w → p (Navajo, some Polynesian languages)</rule>
<rule>kʲ ɡʲ → k ɡ (Danish)</rule>
<rule>ś → k (Possibly unconditional; some Samoyedic langs)</rule>
<rule>pʲ → kʲ (some Romanian dialects, Tsakonian)</rule>
<rule>ts → t (unconditional; some Samoyedic langs)</rule>
<rule>t → k (general Polynesian)</rule>
<rule>n → ŋ (Samoan, but only in colloquial speech)</rule>
<rule>j → p (some Polynesian languages, such as Levei and Drehet) (https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proto-Austronesian_language)</rule>
<rule>b → #c, -nc- (Sundanese)</rule>
<rule>ŋ → {x,h} (various Mayan languages)</rule>
<rule>h → ŋ (Nyole)</rule>
<rule>ʕ → ŋ (allegedly in European Hebrew, both Sephardic and Ashkenazi, but possibly not a sound change so much as a substitution) (https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebrew_language#Varieties_of_ayin, http://sites.google.com/site/londonsephardiminhag/pronunciation, http://www.forward.com/articles/105938/)</rule>
<rule>f → p (? claimed to have occurred independently in Proto-Semitic and Proto-Omotic, and to a limited extent in Egyptian (but this may be related to the Semitic change); note that the Wikipedia article is cited to a single source and that source is admittedly theoretical, and acknowledges on page 77 another reconstruction that doesn’t believe Proto-Afro-Asiatic had /f/ at all) (Page 77 of http://is.gd/WNyXdn, https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proto-Afro-Asiatic_language)</rule>
<rule>tš → t (general Baltic-Finnic; may not be unconditional but was certainly the most common outcome)</rule>
<rule>r → ɡʟ → ɣ (Hiw) (http://is.gd/jCDLO1)</rule>
<rule>mb nd → ʙ dr (Nias)</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>47.2 List 2: Conditional or complex consonant changes</h2>
<cite>Western and Eastern Armenian often have swapped voicing in stop consonants: e.g. vardapet vs. vartabed. This is a result of changes related to aspiration.</cite>
<rule>w → f (Common Celtic; I’m not sure of the conditions)</rule>
<rule>m → n / _i (Tsakonian)</rule>
<rule>{t,k} → ∅ / V_V (Marathi) probably with voiced stops as intermediates, since they also became silent</rule>
<rule>p → w / V_V (Marathi)</rule>
<rule>b d ɡ → bː dː ɡː / V_V (some dialects of Italian; there may be more to it than this, since words like “repubblica” are in standard Italian and not just dialects)</rule>
<rule>t → k / _s̩ (Ōgami) (NB: The article doesn’t have an underscore indicating whether this occurs before or after the /s̩/, but the linked page indicates where this change occurred) (http://amritas.com/101023.htm#10192359)</rule>
<rule>n → i / _s and sometimes other fricatives (Montana Salish)</rule>
</section>
<section>
<h2>47.3 List 3: Vowels</h2>
<rule>y → u (some mainland Greek dialects, and Tsakonian; this particular sound change has been said in some places to be impossible) (https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tsakonian_language#Consonants)</rule>
<rule>i u → s̩ f̩ (Ōgami) (http://amritas.com/101023.htm#10192359)</rule>
</section>
